1 
 
 
In Situ UV-VIS-NIR Absorption Spectroscopy 
and Catalysis 
Authors:  
Max Bolsa, Jing Maa, Fatima Rammal, Dieter Plessers, Xuejiao Wu, Sara Navarro-Jaén, 
Alexander J. Heyer, Bert Sels*, Edward Solomon*, Robert Schoonheydt* 
Contact corresponding authors at: Bert.sels@kuleuven.be, Edward.solomon@stanford.edu, 
Robert.schoonheydt@kuleuven.be 
a These authors contributed equally 
Abstract: This review highlights in situ UV-VIS-NIR range absorption spectroscopy in 
catalysis. A variety of experimental techniques identifying reaction mechanisms, kinetics, and 
structural properties is discussed. Stopped flow techniques, use of laser pulses, and use of 
experimental perturbations are demonstrated for in situ studies of enzymatic, homogeneous, 
heterogeneous, and photocatalysis. They access different time scales and are applicable to 
different reaction systems and catalyst types. In photocatalysis, femto- and nanosecond 
resolved measurements through transient absorption are discussed for tracking excited states. 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopies for structural characterization are demonstrated 
especially for Cu and Fe exchanged zeolites and metalloenzymes. This requires combining 
different spectroscopies. Combining magnetic circular dichroism and resonance Raman 
spectroscopy is especially powerful. A multitude of phenomena can be tracked on transition 
metal catalysts on various supports, including changes in oxidation state, adsorptions, 
reactions, support interactions, surface plasmon resonances, and band gaps. Measurements of 
oxidation states, oxygen vacancies, and band gaps are shown on heterogeneous catalysts, 
especially for electrocatalysis. UV-VIS-NIR absorption is burdened by broad absorption bands. 
Advanced analysis techniques enable the tracking of coking reactions on acid zeolites despite 
convoluted spectra. The value of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy to catalyst 
characterization and mechanistic investigation is clear, but could be expanded.

1. Introduction  
The Ultraviolet-Visible-Near Infrared (UV-VIS-NIR) range of photon energies encompasses 
wavenumbers between 4 000 - 50 000 cm−1, or wavelengths between 200 – 2 500 nm. It 
corresponds to the energies of several chemically relevant electronic excitations, but excludes 
ionizations that require vacuum UV energies or above. The electronic excitations most notably 
include n → π* and π → π* transitions in organic molecules, the d-d, ligand-to-metal charge 
transfer (LMCT) and metal-to-ligand charge transfer (MLCT) transitions in transition metal 
ions (TMI) complexes, various transitions in the electronic shells of rare earth metals, and band 
gaps of semiconductors. Also structural defects in materials can be effectively probed through 
their effect on electronic transition energies. In the NIR region overtones and combinations 
bands of O-H, C-H and N-H vibrations are observed. This plethora of possibilities to study 
atoms, molecules and solids has moved researchers to use UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy in the 
study of catalysts and catalytic reactions, especially those involving 3d TMIs. Their d-d 
transitions are observed in the VIS-NIR region and their MLCT absorption bands lie mainly in 
the UV-VIS. For centrosymmetric complexes, d-d transitions are symmetry (Laporte) 
forbidden, and often they are also spin forbidden. Therefore they are often weak. LMCT and 
MLCT transitions on the other hand are usually symmetry and spin allowed and therefore 
intense (e.g. Figure 1). Two standard reference works on ligand field theory are the books by 
Lever and Solomon covering the fundamentals and interpretation of d-d and charge transfer 
transitions.1, 2 Further selection rules can be exploited to derive structural and electronic insight 
on molecules using circular dichroism (CD) and magnetic circular dichroism (MCD). 
Spectroscopic investigation of a catalyst under reaction conditions is called in situ spectroscopy. 
If the reaction products are simultaneously measured - for instance by gas chromatography 
(GC), liquid chromatography (LC), or mass spectrometry (MS) - at the outlet of the reactor, 
the term operando spectroscopy is used. In this review the umbrella term in situ spectroscopy 
is preferably used whether or not the reaction products are analyzed, unless the measurement 
of reaction products is of particular relevance. For photon absorption spectroscopy, commonly 
used measurements are transmission mode, specular reflection, and diffuse reflectance 
spectroscopy (DRS). In situ spectroscopy requires spectroscopic reaction cells. For solid or 
powdered catalysts fibre optic spectroscopy is used. An overview of such setups is given in 
Weckhuysen’s recent book chapters,3, 4 and the reaction cells have been discussed by Bravo-
Suarez and Srinivasan.5 Whether these reaction cells deliver relevant kinetic data was discussed 
by Valecillos et al.6 Given the ample attention the topic has received in excellent recent reviews, 
we will not focus on this aspect. 

4 
 
 
The review starts with some fundamentals of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. The 
selection rules for the absorption of non-polarized and circularly polarized light are mentioned. 
Subsequently different techniques and analysis methods to extract valuable information on 
catalysis are discussed. UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy has been applied in all areas of 
catalysis: 
heterogeneous, 
homogeneous, 
enzymatic 
catalysis, 
photocatalysis 
and 
electrocatalysis, and will be discussed per subfield in separate sections. Techniques and 
spectroscopies that are specific for one area will be highlighted by state-of-the-art examples in 
that section. In the area of heterogeneous catalysis zeolites with TMIs are given ample attention. 
In zeolites TMIs can occupy well-defined crystallographic sites enabling the measurement of 
site-specific or fingerprint spectra of the active sites. Such spectra, or specific bands in these 
spectra, can be used for kinetic measurements and they serve as guides to identify these sites 
in other catalysts. Other types of heterogeneous catalysts, including supported nanoparticles, 
metal oxide surfaces, and single atom catalysts are then explored. For homogeneous and 
enzymatic catalysis the focus lies on metal-organic transition metal complexes. The use of 
various techniques to measure kinetics is highlighted, as well as the use of (magnetic) circular 
dichroism in the UV-VIS-NIR range. Coupled amperometry and other electrochemical 
analytics, and UV-VIS-NIR absorption under different applied potentials are central in the 
examples given in the electrocatalysis section. In the photocatalysis section, attention is given 
to ultrafast measurements through transient absorption (TA). 
The contents of this review provide an integrated update of several related publications, with a 
widened scope and a deeper focus on experimental methods with recent literature examples. 
The starting points are the reviews and chapters by Jentoft, Weckhuysen and Schoonheydt.4, 7, 
8 Related topics were also recently reviewed specifically for photocatalysis,9 and 
electrocatalysis.10 Further elements are treated on (magnetic) circular dichroism, modelling of 
the absorption spectra, and the use of time- and conditions dependent measurements. While 
theoretical backgrounds are provided in brief, a selection of handbooks is referred to for further 
reading. At the end critical conclusions and an outlook on the further potential of in situ UV-
VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy for catalysis are given. 
2. The role of in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopies 
Spectroscopies in the UV-VIS-NIR range enable access to structural and electronic information 
for several categories of materials in both gas phase and condensed phase. It can do so using 
relatively low cost equipment and for a range of physical conditions. Several experimental and 
data analysis methodologies that include UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopy can be employed 
for different purposes. 
Table 1 gives an overview of the topics included in this review. The application of in situ UV-
VIS-NIR spectroscopy in each of these catalysis types will be presented and the findings of the 
studies will be discussed.

2.1 Characteristics of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra in catalysis 
This section briefly runs through a few fundamentals on absorption spectroscopy in the UV-
VIS-NIR range. To find a more rigorous and extensive treatment of the topic, the handbook by 
Lever et al.1 is advised. It explains the theory behind electronic spectroscopy in general, and 
also includes chapters on symmetry and group theory. An updated version focusing more on 
inorganic compounds was published in 2000 by Lever and Solomon.2 A recent counterpart on 
organic molecules is more difficult to find. There are the publications on solvatochromisms by 
P. Suppan and the handbook by Mataga and Kubota, as well as the spectrum database by 
Hirayama and Kenzo.11-13 For a fundamental treatment of the physical chemistry the handbook 
by Atkins and Friedman is advised.14 

6 
 
 
The UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum is visualized as photon intensity that remains after 
irradiation of the sample, plotted against photon energy. Usually this is displayed relative to a 
reference measurement. The photon energy can be plotted in several units. The use of 
frequency (commonly as wavenumbers in cm−1) is popular, as well as wave period (commonly 
as wavelength in nm). The latter is however inversely proportional to energy and therefore not 
recommended. Another common unit is electronvolts (eV) popular in band gap analysis. 
An electronic absorption spectrum of a catalytic system is generally challenging to interpret 
due to the multitude of possible excitations and their limited spectral resolution under relevant 
conditions. Each absorption event has its particular excitation energy and intensity. The 
excitation energy associated with a single electronic excitation event is spread over an energy 
range mainly through vibronic coupling due to excited state distortion. This results in  a Poisson 
distribution that becomes Gaussian at large distortion. Other contributions to absorption band 
broadening are temperature dependent doppler broadening (Gaussian profile), and subtle 
inhomogeneities in the coordination sphere. This broadening leads to overlapping absorption 
bands that can be difficult to resolve. In solutions, dynamic solvent interactions additionally 
contribute to broadening. Absorption bands may also become asymmetrical depending on the 
predominant broadening phenomena. To deconvolute spectra, single absorption bands are most 
regularly approximated by Gaussian or more general Voight distributions which are then 
superimposed to approach the experimental spectrum (example in Figure 1).15, 16 Especially in 
condensed matter with conjugated systems or in composites, macromolecules, and in solutions, 
discerning individual bands becomes difficult (e.g. Figures 32-34,44,45,57), but also for nearly 
single site supported TMI complexes this can be the case (e.g. the low energy d-d bands in 
Figure 1). Increasing density of energy levels eventually culminates in continuous energy 
bands, leading to a bandgap spectrum (e.g. Figures 10,11). As a consequence, while electronic 
absorption energies are highly sensitive to structure, they are difficult to use as unique 
identifiers absent a good preliminary understanding of the material structure and composition. 
 
Figure 1. Room temperature diffuse reflectance spectrum of a calcined *BEA zeolite (Si/Al=12.5) loaded with 
0.3 wt. % Fe (black curve) to contain nearly single site square planar coordinated Fe2+.17 The spectrum is 
deconvoluted with five gaussian band shapes (blue), the sum of the gaussian bands is given by the red curve. The 
insert gives the parameters of the gaussian bands; obtained from experiment. Copyright 2016 Springer Nature. 
Traditionally, the analysis of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra has been based on databases of 
well identified complexes, looking for structural similarities. The book by Lever remains an 

7 
 
 
important reference work for transition metal complexes, and the collection by Hirayama and 
Kenzo for organic molecules.1, 12 Simple spectra can, at least qualitatively, be explained with 
crystal field theory or ligand field theory. Important parameters are the coordination 
environment (symmetry and composition) and orbital energies determining band energy and 
absorption coefficients. Band interpretation is also aided by the band shape, and vibronic 
structure. Various selection rules arise from theory resulting in widely varying absorption 
probabilities that dictate the absorption intensity for individual electronic excitations.1 For the 
UV-VIS-NIR range of electronic excitations the most important selection rules are eq 1 and eq 
2. 
 
The spin selection rule: ΔS = 0 
(1) 
 
The orbital (‘Laporte’) selection rule: Δl = +1 or –1 
(2) 
Both arise from the predominant excitation mechanism which occurs through the interaction 
of the photon and electronic transition electric dipole moments, together with the assumption 
that the spin (𝜃𝑠) and orbital (𝜑) parts of the electronic wavefunctions can be considered 
independently. Formally, the absorption probability is proportional to the square of the 
transition moment integral 𝑃̅ (eq 3). 
 
𝑃̅ = 〈𝜑2|𝑟̅|𝜑1〉〈𝜃1,𝑠|𝜃2,𝑠〉 
(3) 
Where the ground and excited state wavefunctions are denoted with respective subscripts 1 and 
2, and with 𝑟̅ the electric dipole operator. 
The orbital selection rule is dependent on the symmetry of the involved orbitals, but also of the 
polarization of the incident light. Linear polarization can reveal the transition’s electric dipole 
orientation, but requires an oriented sample and is not useful for the randomly oriented samples 
(powders, solutions, …) generally encountered in in situ spectroscopy for catalysis. Circular 
polarization is sensitive to enantiomers, but is scarcely applied to the in situ study of catalysts. 
The differential absorption of differently polarized light can be measured, resulting in linear or 
circular dichroism spectroscopy.. CD intensity is determined by the projection of the electric 
dipole transition moment into the magnetic dipole moment. Evaluating circular dichroism in a 
longitudinal magnetic field under varying temperatures and magnetic field strengths results in 
variable-temperature-variable-field (VTVH) magnetic circular dichroism (MCD), which has 
been applied to TMI catalysts (vide infra). In this case the magnetic field is used to lift the 
degeneracy of ground and/or excited electronic states, resulting in different transition moments 
and enabling circular dichroism for non-chiral absorption centra.18, 19 
For ligand field transitions the selection rules are generally relaxed, and the degree to which 
they are relaxed determines the absorption probability. Typical ranges for absorption 
coefficients are given in Table 2. Foremost, the classification of TMI complexes’ electronic 
transitions into d-d and charge transfer (CT) transitions is an approximation. Molecular orbitals 
of TMI complexes have a mixed metal and ligand character and the electronic transitions have 
varying degrees of CT and d-d (or f-f) character. Symmetry imposed selection rules are relaxed 
through non-centrosymmetric distortion of the ligand field and through vibronic coupling to 
odd parity vibrations. The spin selection rule is relaxed through spin-orbit coupling.14 
Table 2. Typical ranges for molar absorption coefficients for UV-VIS-NIR electronic transitions depending on 
the relaxation of the selection rules.1 
Type of Transition 
Molar absorption coefficient range, ε 
(cm−1 M−1) 

8 
 
 
Spin & Laporte forbidden 
10–3 - 102 
Spin allowed & Laporte 
forbidden 
100 - 104 
Spin & Laporte allowed 
102 – 106 
 
The previous points indicate that the assignment and interpretation of individual absorption 
bands, let alone of the full spectra, is difficult. Both the excitation energies and the absorption 
probabilities depend on a large number of factors, and it is generally not feasible to obtain 
accurate orbital energies and absorption probabilities without computational aids. Several 
computationally implemented molecular modelling methods to simulate absorption spectra 
(including CD and MCD) from an optimized geometry are available.20 The most popular is 
time-dependent density-functional theory (TD-DFT) which offers an appropriate balance 
between accuracy and speed to model systems of a size relevant in catalysis research. It is 
important that the chosen TD-DFT method is benchmarked for the type of system for which 
the spectrum is simulated, but also that the fundamental shortcomings of the method are 
carefully considered.21, 22 More advanced methods (e.g. multireference and coupled cluster) 
can yield more accurate results, but they are computationally heavy. Therefore their application 
is limited to small structures, or small parts of larger structures.20 Also here lies a risk for 
accuracy as long range charge transfer character may be missed, besides other 
misspecifications that can be made in setting up the calculation. Also for species in solution 
further extensions of the models are needed to accurately simulate the spectroscopy, 
introducing quantum mechanics/molecular mechanics (QM/MM) approaches.23 In addition, 
further information on individual absorption bands can be obtained by coupling other analytic 
techniques, site selective spectroscopies, or exploiting synthesis-spectroscopy relations. In turn 
these can guide the computational modelling. 
Unlike many other characterization techniques, UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy probes 
events and characteristics that are directly catalytically relevant. This is true especially for 
photocatalysis (Figure 2 B), but electronic ground and excited states are generally relevant to 
catalytic reactions as interpreted through frontier molecular orbitals (FMO) (Figure 2 A). The 
highest occupied molecular orbitals (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied orbitals (LUMO) play 
central roles in catalysis and in reactivity in general. The HOMO-LUMO gap often falls in the 
UV-VIS-NIR range. In many cases the LUMO functions as an electron acceptor in catalytic 
mechanisms (e.g. Figure 2 A, C). However, depending on the type of catalysis the electronic 
excitation may be induced via different mechanisms following different selection rules. 
Especially the possibility to study these electronic states in situ enables unique insights into 
catalysis. 

9 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2. (A) The HOMO and LUMO and HOMO-LUMO gap of a homogeneous aryl-NiIV complex and the role 
of its LUMO in C-H bond activation. (B) A CdS nanorod photocatalyst with a bulge along its z-axis, with band 
gaps in the UV-VIS-NIR range. The band gap is affected along the z-axis by the presence of the bulge. (C) The 
SyrB2 non-heme FeIV-oxo active site, similar to other non-heme FeIV-oxo sites in homogeneous, enzymatic and 
heterogeneous catalysis, with its NIR-accessible excited state playing a key role in H atom abstraction. Adapted 
from refs. 24-26. Copyrights 2019 Springer nature, 2014 and 2022 American chemical society.  
2.2 Collection and processing of in situ absorption spectra 
Before quantitative analysis is possible, also the irradiance, photon collection, and detection 
processes must be considered. In the calculation of extinction coefficients it is assumed that all 
photons get the chance to excite the electron, and that all not-absorbed photons are measured. 
This is not always the case, and depends on the measurement mode and interfering processes. 
Therefore processing of raw experimental spectra is required. Moreover, measurement 
conditions as well as the light absorption itself can significantly influence the state of the 
absorption centra and thereby influence further absorption. Such phenomena can be exploited 
to gain insight in specific catalyst properties through data collection and processing techniques. 
2.2.1 Modes of irradiation and light collection 
Several spectroscopic methodologies have been developed for the UV-VIS-NIR range to study 
different reaction systems and answer different questions on catalyst structure and catalytic 
mechanisms. In lab scale heterogeneous catalysis, also including electrocatalysis and some 
photocatalysis, the catalyst is usually a powdered solid with a high surface area. DRS is then 
commonly used, in combination with reaction setups that enable the collection of spectra at 
high temperature, and under a flow of reagent and product molecules. Alternatively mulls can 
be prepared enabling transmission spectroscopy, but this is less common. UV-VIS-NIR spectra 
on homogeneous and enzymatic catalysis are typically collected in transmission mode as the 
reactions generally occur in a translucent solvent. With the addition of inert particles or for 
suspensions and colloids in general, DRS can also be used (Figure 3 B).27-30 For both 
transmission and DR modes cells for in situ characterization are technically feasible and 
commercially available.4 

10 
 
 
2.2.1.1 Transmission and diffuse reflectance spectroscopy 
For sufficiently translucent samples, the absorption spectrum can be easily obtained by 
measuring the portion of light that remains of an incident light beam after passing through the 
sample, i.e. the transmission. This approach is applicable to translucent liquid solutions and 
very thin solid wafers, and other translucent samples. For opaque samples other approaches are 
needed. When samples are opaque due to strong absorption, dilution is an option. When they 
are opaque due to scattering, diffuse reflectance is used. The latter is relevant for the study of 
solid phase catalysts, which are mostly studies in powdered form, or as deposits forming a 
rough surface. This allows a better approximation of the intended use of catalysts in packed 
beds, fluidised beds or on electrodes, and yields more representative in situ measurements. 
For powders or rough surfaces with particle sizes of the order of the wavelength the incident 
light is partially scattered and partially absorbed, as schematically shown in Figure 3 A. The 
particles in the bed are illuminated from all possible directions by the scattered light. 
Consequently the scattered light escapes the particle bed in all possible directions. In the ideal 
case all angles of incidence are equally probable and the same holds for the scattered light 
leaving the bed of particles. If there is no transmission through the bed of particles (infinitely 
thick bed) the light is either absorbed or scattered, and the scattered beam escaping from the 
surface can be detected. This is diffuse reflectance spectroscopy (DRS). 
 
 
  
Figure 3. (A) Visualization of the interaction between the incident light and the rough surface. (B) The suspension 
of α-Al2O3 in a stirred liquid medium to enable diffuse reflectance mode measurement.30 Copyright 2022 Royal 
society of chemistry. 
When using DRS quantitatively, a particular challenge is the determination of the pathlength 
of the light ‘through’ the sample (Figure 3 A). In DRS, the (average) pathlength is affected by 

11 
 
 
the scattering events, and by the absorption itself. With more scattering, photons ‘bounce 
around’ in the surface layer longer giving a higher probability of absorption. With higher 
absorption, the observed diffuse reflected photons are more likely to have spent less time in the 
surface layer, and their average pathlength is decreased. The result is a complex non-linear 
problem that depends on material properties. The Beer-Lambert law for processing 
transmission measurements and equations for processing DRS absorption data will be 
discussed further in section 2.2.3.1. 
2.2.1.2 Fiber optics 
Spectroscopic investigation of a working catalyst requires a spectroscopic cell that also 
functions as a reactor, best designed to accurately measure kinetics. The light must be guided 
to and from the catalyst at high temperatures and pressures. On heterogeneous catalysts this is 
best achieved using glass fibre optics bundles in heat resistant UV-VIS-NIR probes. These 
irradiate the sample and collect reflected and scattered light. 
For a gas phase reaction, a homogeneous flow of reagent molecules over the catalyst bed is 
required. For a powdered catalysts, the powder should be loaded as pellets smaller than 10% 
of the inner diameter of the reactor tube. The dead volume must be kept as small as possible, 
and the conversion should be small to avoid temperature and pressure gradients.31-35 
Measurements at the top and bottom are not representative of the catalyst bed. Thus, the high 
temperature probe of the spectrometer is placed outside the reactor against a quartz tube 
containing the catalyst to probe the middle of the catalyst bed and achieve proper kinetic data. 
Multiple measurements at different locations on the bed can also provide information on the 
spatial evolution (e.g. see Figure 34). If the NIR region is probed together with the VIS region 
the quartz must be high grade quartz with a low amount of OH groups. A schematic 
representation is shown in Figure 4. More generally, the design of kinetically appropriate 
spectroscopic cells has been discussed by Meunier.31 
 
Figure 4. Scheme of an in situ/operando spectroscopy set up. Reproduced from ref. 31. Copyright 2010 Royal 
Society of Chemistry. 
As an illustration (Figure 4), a fiber optic reflection probe could contain five fibers: one central 
irradiation or emission fiber and four read or collection fibers. Depending on the distance to 
the sample, such fibers analyze a smaller or larger area of the sample, but usually this is below 
1 mm2. This is an extremely small fraction of the surface of the catalyst bed, and a 
supplementary reason to repeat the spectroscopic measurements at different heights of the bed. 
This simultaneously verifies that a uniform distribution of the absorption centers in the catalyst 
bed is present. 

12 
 
 
With the optical fiber probe as shown in Figure 4, the catalyst is illuminated perpendicularly 
to the catalyst bed and the scattered light is collected under the same angle. This means that 
diffuse reflection and specular reflection are measured together at every wavelength. In 
situ/operando cells that irradiate the sample at 45° and collect the light either at 90° (diffuse 
reflectance only) or at –45° (specular reflectance only) also exist,36 but they give other practical 
difficulties. 
2.2.1.3 Improving time and spatial resolution 
Time-dependent processes are of interest in catalysis research and high measurement 
frequencies are needed to resolve them. On stable analytes, high quality UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption spectra can be obtained using a light source that is passed through a monochromator. 
Such measurement can take several minutes, too long for many experiments. The required and 
desired measurement time depend respectively on the system studied and on the phenomenon 
of interest (Figure 9). The timescale of the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy measurement 
itself lies in the attosecond range (10−18 s). A single photon absorption, the transformation to 
an electronic excited state, and the thermalization of ‘hot’ electrons all occur on the attosecond 
(10−18 s) scale. Such measurements are of value in characterizing excited states and are relevant 
for understanding photocatalysis in particular. 
In most cases one wants to experimentally track the statistical conversion of many particles 
through activated processes, adding a probabilistic component (e.g. looking at half lives). This 
brings the photon absorption and electronic excitation time scale to the femtosecond (10−15 s) 
range. The decay of electronic excited states can occur with half-lives ranging from 
femtoseconds to hours. Similarly, the time scales of activated chemical reactions can vary 
strongly. A selection was tabulated in a review by Shi, Lercher and Yu.14, 37 
With commercially available UV-VIS-NIR spectrophotometers, sufficient signal-to-noise is 
generally obtained for sub-second resolution, enabling relevant operando UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption measurements. In this case, the time resolution limit is set by the method of 
irradiation since, on the collection side, array charge coupled device (CCD) detectors allow 
collection over the full UV-VIS-NIR range in milliseconds.38 This is still insufficient to track 
the short lived excited states exploited in photocatalysis. 
Nanosecond and femtosecond range experiments can be achieved using laser pulses and 
transient absorption spectroscopy (TAS).39 For easily repeated experiments (e.g. repeated 
excitation with laser pulses) step-scan measurements can be used. Independent single 
measurements are aggregated into a time series of spectra with good signal-to-noise.39 A good 
overview of irradiation methods and detectors is given in a book chapter by Skoog et al.40 A 
diagram of the femtosecond and nanosecond resolution setups and experiments is shown in 
Figure 9. 
Choosing the spatial resolution is a trade-off between analyzing a sufficient catalyst portion to 
be relevant for the full catalyst bed and capturing the heterogeneities that exist in the bed. The 
best spatial resolution therefore depends on the research question. Generally high spatial 
resolution is targeted and the results are then validated for larger areas, or with repeatedly 
displaced high resolution measurements. A lot of advances have been made in the latest decade 
using microspectroscopy, achieving resolutions on micrometer scales.3, 4 UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption microspectroscopy studies remain so far limited to the study of surface bound 
aromatics and coke formation involving molecules with high absorption coefficients.3, 41-43 

13 
 
 
2.2.2 Conditions- and time-dependent measurements 
In situ spectroscopic setups enable the collection of spectroscopic data at selected conditions. 
One can choose to simulate the conditions relevant to the intended catalytic process, or to vary 
conditions for analytical purposes. The former generally implies elevated temperatures and 
reactant feeding, and is often used in combination with catalytic measurements resulting in 
operando spectroscopy. The latter approach involves the addition of probe molecules or redox 
conditions, the application of external magnetic fields, temperature conditions, and/or electric 
potential, the trapping of intermediates, and the monitoring of responses to pulses and step 
changes in various conditions. Numerous reviews have addressed setups and applications of in 
situ/operando setups.4, 7, 8, 39 In this section we will give an overview of some experimental 
spectroscopy techniques to gain further electronic and structural information. 
2.2.2.1 Varying temperature and magnetic field 
The population of excited states depends on temperature through the Boltzmann distribution, 
eq 4. 
 
𝑁𝑒𝑥
𝑁𝑔=
𝑔𝑒𝑥
𝑔𝑔𝑒−(𝐸𝑒𝑥−𝐸𝑔)/𝑘𝐵𝑇 
(4) 
with Nex and Ng the populations in the excited and ground states, gex and gg the degeneracies of 
the excited and ground states, Eex and Eg the energies of the excited and ground states, kB the 
Boltzmann constant and T the temperature. 
For energy levels separated by photon energies in the UV-VIS-NIR range (Eex – Eg > 4000 
cm−1), the population of the electronic excited states is very limited (e.g. still < 1% at 1250 K), 
but for close lying electronic states the relative populations can be drastically adjusted with 
large changes in the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum. A recent example is the in situ 
variable-temperature measurement of S=0 to S=2 spin crossover in pseudo-tetrahedral 
[BP3]FeII-X complexes from 188 K to 373 K (Figure 5).44 Additionally, smaller separations are 
achieved by splitting (near) degenerate electronic states energy levels according to their spin 
and orbital angular momenta by applying an external magnetic field. The relative population 
of these levels depends on the strength of the applied field and temperature. Such experiments 
are called ‘variable temperature variable field’ (VTVH) spectroscopy, and are applied in 
various spectroscopies (Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR), Mössbauer, X-ray absorption 
spectroscopy (XAS), …). Applying an external magnetic field is particularly interesting in UV-
VIS-NIR range circular dichroism measurements, resulting in magnetic circular dichroism 
(MCD). 
 

14 
 
 
Figure 5. Variable temperature UV-VIS-NIR absorption of [PhBPPh3]Fe(NPPh3) showing its temperature 
dependent transition from a low spin state to a high spin state. Adapted from ref. 44 Copyright 2016 American 
chemical society. 
MCD observes the differential absorption of left and right circularly polarized light by a sample 
in a large magnetic field parallel to the propagation direction of the circularly polarized light. 
There are three different intensity mechanisms (A-term, B-term and C-term), but the richest 
information is gained from bands with C-term intensity. Its analysis has been particularly useful 
to probe the coordination geometry of non-heme iron catalysts, and examples of this are found 
for Fe-zeolites, homogeneous catalysts, and enzymes in sections 3.1.2., 3.2.1.2 and 3.3.3. C-
term intensity originates from degenerate ground states that are Zeeman split in a magnetic 
field. At low temperatures, this splitting results in a different Boltzmann population of the spin 
sublevels. From the selection rules (LCP: ΔL = +1; RCP: ΔL = –1), different sublevels absorb 
light selectively depending on the circular polarization. The difference in absorption of left vs 
right circular polarized light is detected in MCD using modulation methods to enhance signal 
to noise (Figure 6).18 
 
Figure 6. Magnetic circular dichroism C-term intensity with increasing field at low temperature. Absorption of 
left circularly polarized (lcp) light (green), absorption of right circularly polarized (rcp) light (purple), and the 
differential absorption (solid black) due to the differences in Boltzmann population of the m = +1 and m = –1 
levels as the Zeeman splitting increases towards the right. 
The selection rule for C-term intensity requires two perpendicular electric dipole transition 
moments. Low symmetry metal sites, like those in metalloenzymes, have nondegenerate 
electronic states, thus electronic transition dipoles to an excited state are in one direction. Thus, 
to have the two perpendicular transition dipole moments in one transition, there must be spin 
orbit coupling (SOC) between states. Metal atoms have much larger SOC constants compared 
to the atoms on the ligands. Thus, the greater the metal character of a transition, the greater the 
SOC, and the greater the MCD C-term intensity relative to absorption intensity (referred to as 
a C0/D0 ratio, see also Figure 50 for an example). This is important in observing and assigning 
bands. Ligand field transitions have large C0/D0 ratios due to the transitions being largely 
localized on the metal center. This allows these bands to be observed in MCD at low 
temperature. LMCT transitions have small C0/D0 ratios due to the transition being more 
centered on ligands, with smaller SOC constants. VTVH-MCD experiments on C-terms are 
typically conducted over a domain of 0 - 7 T and 2 - 50 K. Saturation magnetization isotherms 
and isofields are derived for individual absorption bands that give information on the spin state, 
zero-field splitting, and magnetization of both the ground and excited state (see also Figure 51 
for an example). MCD on C-terms is thus always measured at cryogenic temperatures and is 

15 
 
 
not useful for operando spectroscopy in catalysis. A- and B-terms on the other hand are 
accessible at room temperature, but they lack the rich information content of C-terms.19, 45 
In conventional UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy the shifts obtained in practically 
feasible magnetic fields are small and difficult to resolve, so VTVH perturbations are not used. 
In situ cells are, however, used to measure spectra in controlled atmospheres and temperatures. 
The example of spin crossover of Fe complexes was already given.44 Different roto-vibrational 
levels are also easily populated at achievable temperatures, vibronic coupling can be resolved 
at cryogenic measurements, and different coordination numbers, adsorption modes, other 
equilibrium distributions, and geometric isomers may be obtained depending on the 
atmosphere composition, pressure, and temperature. Recent examples are the cryogenic 
observation of vibronic coupling on RuII complexes of tridentate bisphosphino-
oligothiopentene (Figure 7), and cycloreversion of dithienylethene compounds.44, 46-49 These 
types of variable-temperature measurements are currently mostly limited to homogeneous 
complexes and enzymes. Cryogenic measurements in diffuse reflectance mode (vide infra) are 
scarce, but it has been successfully applied to study thermochromism in Salicylideneanilines.47 
In general the application of both VTVH-MCD and VT UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy 
to extract information on electronic structure and adsorption behavior remains scarce on 
heterogeneous catalysts, despite its potential. 
 
Figure 7. UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of the RuII(DMSO)4Cl2 complex recorded at 293 K and 85 K. The low 
temperature spectrum resolves the vibronic coupling in the 390 nm absorption band. Adapted from ref. 48. 
Copyright 2011 Royal Society of Chemistry. 
2.2.2.2 Time dependent measurements 
While the measurement of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra on systems in equilibrium or steady 
state can contain features of reaction intermediates, more mechanistic insight can be derived 
from time-dependent spectroscopic tracking. Several types of experiments have been 
developed to enable the tracking of different types of processes with absorption spectroscopy. 
Both UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy and circular dichroism are used as the analytical 
methods, although the former is much more common, especially in catalysis. Some examples 
of CD used in stopped-flow and ultrafast transient-response experiments exist in literature, and 
a very limited number of time-resolved MCD spectroscopy can be found, both applied mostly 
to protein analysis.50-57 
Depending on the rate of the process under investigation, different methods are employed. To 
track slow transformations, simple spectroscopic tracking of an ongoing reagent limited 
reaction is sufficient, e.g. with a setup as shown in Figure 4. From the temperature and 
concentration or pressure dependence of the transformations kinetic information can be 

16 
 
 
obtained. Arrhenius plots can be constructed to derive activation energies, Eyring plots for 
transition state (TS) entropy and enthalpy, and concentration dependence plots for reaction 
orders. Some examples in section 3 are the tracking of methane oxidation to methanol with Cu-
zeolites, N2O reduction with Fe zeolites, and coking on solid acids. 
For reactions in liquid phase with millisecond-range kinetics, and to observe short-lived 
intermediates, a stopped-flow setup can be used (e.g. Figure 35). This is especially popular in 
the study of enzymatic catalytic cycles. Often these progress through a series of multiple key 
intermediates which would not be observed under steady state conditions where only the rate 
determining step is revealed. Stopped-flow experiments can be designed so that only a single 
turnover is performed and the formation and decay of intermediates can be observed and 
measured (example in Figure 8).58 Separate solutions of catalyst and substrate are rapidly 
driven into a mixing chamber, and UV-VIS absorption spectra are rapidly collected with an 
array detector.59, 60 Measuring the decay of an intermediate at various temperatures allows 
derivation of an Eyring plot to extract out the enthalpic and entropic contributions to reactivity. 
Additionally, by isotopically labeling substrates, the appearance of (or lack of) kinetic isotope 
effects provides insight in the reaction mechanism. 
 
Figure 8. (A) Stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectra of FeII-tryptophan hydroxylase with O2. From 0 - 175 ms 
(top) and 0.175 - 2 s (bottom). (B) Speciation curve for formation and decay of intermediates. Adapted from ref. 
58. Copyright 2021 National Academy of Sciences. 
For still faster reactions, where the system reaches equilibrium too quickly for the measurement 
of enough absorption spectra, the equilibrium can be disturbed in various ways (Table 3 and 
Figure 9, bottom) after which the response can be tracked spectroscopically. The fastest 
response technique, achieving femtosecond resolutions, is transient absorption spectroscopy 
(TAS) tracking the relaxation of electronic excited states after ultrafast laser pulses for 
photocatalyst characterization (Figure 9 top and middle).39, 61, 62 Another class is modulation 
excitation spectroscopy (MES) producing periodic disturbances that can be of various types, 
and chemical composition perturbation methods such as transient analysis of products (TAP) 
and steady state isotope transient kinetic analysis (SSITKA), using for example a setup as show 
at the bottom of Figure 9.63-65 These techniques can detect and temporarily concentrate short 
lived intermediates, map reaction networks, quantify adsorbed species, and access kinetic 
parameters. While they are combined most commonly with in situ infrared (IR), Raman, XAS 
and X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectroscopies, they can be particularly helpful tools in 

17 
 
 
understanding complex UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra as well, especially for heterogeneous 
catalysts. They were recently reviewed in separate chapters in a book edited by Wachs and 
Bañares.66 
Table 3. Overview of techniques that enable tracking the response to equilibrium disturbances

After re-stabilization, the disturbance can be repeated to collect more data and reduce further 
the signal-to-noise through step-scan measurements. The time scales depend strongly on the 
setup and on the system under study. Kinetic models are needed to analyze the transient 
response in the reaction. The analysis of the absorption spectra time series can be aided by 
statistical methods including regression models and data reduction tools such as factor analysis, 
principal components analysis (PCA), non-negative matrix factorization (NNMF) and 
multivariate curve resolution (MCR).67 
For further analysis with different ex-situ techniques, or to enable longer measurement times, 
rapid freeze quenching techniques, or less involved quench procedures depending on the 
sample stability, can be used to trap intermediate samples from any of the methods described 
above.68
 

18 
 
 
Figure 9. Timescales of some different processes related to in-situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy, and 
specialized techniques used to access these time scales. Fs-TA = femtosecond transient absorption, OPA = optical 
parametric amplifier, BBO = beta barium borate, CCD = charge-coupled device, ns-TA = nanosecond transient

2.2.3 Processing of spectroscopic data 
The primary data obtained from an absorption experiment gives the ratio of detected light 
intensity vs incident light intensity versus photon energy, usually relative to a reference. It is 
commonly preferred to transform the measure of absorption into a form that correlates linearly 
with the concentration of the analyte. For transmission mode absorption spectroscopy the Beer-
Lambert law is used, and the absorption is quantitatively measured by ‘absorbance’ (A). For 
diffuse reflection mode the Kubelka-Munk (K-M) formula is most popular yielding a 
quantitative measure in ‘Kubelka-Munk units’. Further processing usually involves 
deconvolution of the individual absorption bands from the spectrum. Popular band shapes were 
already mentioned in section 2.1. For quantitatively correct deconvolution one should naturally 
first apply the Beer-Lambert or Kubelka-Munk transformation to the full spectrum and then 
extract the individual absorption bands. Quantitative tracking through time or with changing 
conditions then allows to derive information on kinetics and thermodynamics. Arrhenius, 
Eyring, and isotherm plots are easily derived provided a reliable quantitative deconvolution 
can be obtained.  
Apart from the measurement and tracking of concentrations, other forms of processing can be 
used to access different properties. Commonly derived from UV-VIS-NIR absorption are band 
gap energies through Tauc plots, reaction stoichiometries through Job plots, and equilibrium 
constants and stoichiometries of reversible complexation with the Benesi-Hildebrand method. 
In this section we further discuss only the two basic processing functions (Beer-Lambert and 
Kubelka-Munk) to obtain quantitative data in in situ spectroscopy, and Tauc/Cody plots for 
their intrinsic association to UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy. 
2.2.3.1 The Lambert-Beer and Kubelka-Munk functions 
Several metrics can be used to quantify experimental absorption, absorbance being the most 
popular. For transmission measurements on a uniform sample, the Beer-Lambert law, eq 5, 
describes the decay of light intensity (of a particular photon energy) as it passes through an 
absorbing translucent sample: 
 
𝐴= log⁡(

at the particular photon energy. Often A10 is used as notation instead of A to specify the use of 
log10. For mixed samples, a weighted sum of absorption coefficients should be used. For non-
uniform samples corrections should be used due to the exponential (non-linear) decay of I0/I in 
A. 
With an analogy to transmission in transmission spectroscopy, the diffusively reflected light 
intensity (the absolute remittance) of an infinitely thick sample 𝑅∞ is defined by eq 6.

Infinite thickness means that none of the impinging light penetrates through to the bottom of 
the sample and transmits. 
In a classical spectrophotometer an integration sphere with a (near) fully reflecting (‘white’) 
inner surface in the UV-VIS-NIR region is installed in the sample compartment. Popular 
materials are Halon polymeric materials, barium sulphate, or potassium bromide. This sphere 
contains a sample port and a reference port. The reference measurement is usually performed 
with a fully reflecting standard material at the sample and reference ports. The diffuse 
reflectance of the sample at the sample port is then measured against the diffuse reflectance of 
the standard by eq 7.

In transmission mode the pathlength is simply the linear path through the sample followed by 
the beam and, for uniform samples, the linear Lambert-Beer law can be used. In DRS the 
pathlength is affected by the scattering events and by the absorbance itself (vide supra, Figure 
3 A). The scattering probability increases with 1/𝜆𝑛, where n is around 1 for particles of the 
size in the range of the wavelength. In addition, with higher absorbance, the observed diffuse 
reflected photons are more likely to have spent less time in the surface layer, and the average 
pathlength is decreased. To obtain specific absorption coefficients, it is required to solve a 
complex non-linear problem that depends on material properties. Several approaches have been 
suggested to give an acceptable representation of the phenomenon.29, 69 These models make 
assumptions on how the photons scatter and on certain properties of the rough surface, and they 
enable a mathematical transformation of the diffuse reflectance into a measure that varies 
linearly with absorbent’s concentration (analogues to Lambert-Beer). Different models may be 
better suited to different types of materials. In heterogeneous catalysis, the Kubelka-Munk 
formula (eq 9) is most used, based on the assumptions listed in Box 1.

The full derivation of eq 9 can be found in Kortüm’s book and in more recent papers.7, 69-71

2. Infinite extension of the catalyst bed in the x-y directions
