Box 1. The assumptions in the Kubelka-Munk derivations. In orange the least acceptable assumptions in in situ 
DRS measurements on catalysis using a state-of-the-art setup. 
 
Note that it will already be difficult to fulfill these assumptions in the sample setup shown in 
Figure 3 B and a different treatment will be needed. Also for other scenarios, for example 
measurements on thin absorbing films on coarse reflective surfaces, other methods should be 
used.72, 73 For the application of UV-VIS-NIR DRS to heterogeneous catalysis, a few of the 
assumptions in Box 1 should be given special attention: 
At the surface of a powder sample, a small fraction of light is reflected under the same angle 
as that of the incoming light beam without absorption. This is called specular reflection. In 
practice, this fraction is often negligibly small and it can also be adjusted for by modifying the 
spectroscopic setup. Deviations occur for strongly absorbing materials, i.e. large values of the 
K-M function. In these cases, the amount of diffusively reflected light is low and the specular 
reflection part is not negligible. Scattering increases with decreasing wavelength and with 
decreasing particle size of the catalyst. It is almost constant in the NIR and VIS range and 
increases continuously in the UV. Absorption bands in the UV region are thus superposed on 
the increasing scattering background. Both the position of the band maximum and the intensity 
are influenced, making it very difficult to quantify the absorption bands in the UV region 
accurately. In the NIR and VIS, with almost constant scattering coefficient 𝑆, the K-M function 
can be written as eq 10. 
 
F(𝑅∞) =
𝐾
𝑆=
𝜖⁡𝐶
𝑆  
(10) 
with C the concentration of absorbing species, S the K-M scattering constant, and K the K-M 
absorption coefficient. The K-M function for powders can then be considered an analogue of 
the Lambert-Beer law for solutions. Still, it requires a constant scattering coefficient over the 
width of the absorption band. 
The K-M theory has been critically evaluated by multiple authors, and several alternatives or 
extensions have been proposed.29, 69 No alternatives have however been developed that are 
generally superior without significantly sacrificing on simplicity. 
2.2.3.2 Band gap energies 
The optical band gap energy (𝐸𝑔) of a semiconductor is the minimum energy required to absorb 
a photon transferring a valence band (VB) electron to the conduction band (CB). It is an 
important parameter for photocatalysis, photovoltaics and photodetection, and an often 
measured property on heterogeneous catalysts holding structural information. A technique for 
obtaining 𝐸𝑔 for an amorphous semiconductor from the absorption spectrum was originally 
proposed by Tauc and Vancu in 1966.74 Their technique is now widely used, although not 
always in a rigorous manner. Here we review the theory and common errors in its usage applied 
to catalyst characterization. 
Tauc et al. demonstrated that the energy-dependent absorption coefficient α can be expressed 
by eq 11.: 
 
(α ∙ℎ𝑣)1/2 = 𝐵(ℎ𝑣−𝐸𝑔 ) 
(11) 
with ℎ Planck’s constant, 𝑣 photon frequency, and B the band tailing parameter, generally 
taken to be 1 for amorphous materials. For diffuse reflectance measurements, α can be replaced 

21 
 
 
by the K-M function (equation 10). For transmission and reflectance (T&R) measurements on 
thin film semiconductors α is derived using the Beer-Lambert law (equation 5). 𝐸𝑔⁡estimated 
from (equation 11) is only reliable for sufficient absorption, and could be inaccurate with 
optically thin films. The multiple-reflection interference model has been proposed as an 
alternative to determine the absorption coefficient from T&R spectra of thin films.75, 76  
For crystalline semiconductors the ½ exponent is replaced by 𝛾−1 , accounting for the 
expression for the transition rate depending on the nature of the electronic transition. 𝛾 is equal 
to 1/2, 3/2, 2 or 3 for the direct allowed, direct forbidden, indirect allowed and indirect 
forbidden transition band gaps respectively. The mathematical derivation of these values can 
be found in the handbook by M. Cardona and P. Yu, among others.77 A tangent line in the 
linear region of a Tauc plot, with (α ∙ℎ𝑣)1/𝛾 plotted versus ℎ𝑣, is extrapolated to the ℎ𝑣 axis 
and the zero-intercept gives 𝐸𝑔. 
The obtained optical bandgap is, however, very sensitive to the quality and handling of the 
spectroscopic data, and to the choice of 𝛾. This leads to diverging bandgap values reported in 
literature for the same materials. Firstly, as the method depends on an extrapolation making 
use of a wide energy domain of the spectrum, extra care is needed to guard the assumptions 
behind deriving α, for example using the Beer-Lambert or Kubelka-Munk relations. Photon 
energy dependent scattering may stand in the way of correct linearization, and absolute 
deviations from α may affect the slope of the tangent line. Secondly, a proper baseline must be 
used. In a defect-free crystalline semiconductor the absorption spectrum abruptly terminates at 
the energy gap, but for defected, doped, bulk or surface modified materials and amorphous 
semiconductors inter-band gap states give so-called ‘Urbach tails’ in the absorption 
spectrum.78-80 Heterogeneous catalysts typically belong to these last categories. 
Depending on the exponential tail region different approaches are appropriate. When unrelated 
absorption bands are present in the tail region (e.g. interbands, sub-bands, or absorption by 
intermixed species), extra care should be taken. An example is given by Makula et al.78 Tauc 
plots of pure TiO2 are compared to those with methyl orange added (MO + TiO2) in different 
amounts (indicated with a, b and c) (Figure 10 A). The changes in the measured Eg are 
substantial. It is necessary to subtract the contribution of MO from the spectrum (Figure 10 B). 
Since it can be challenging to deconvolute the K-M spectrum into spectra of individual 
components, alternatively, a linear fit is applied for the slope below the investigated absorption 
(baseline fitting) to estimate the 𝐸𝑔 (Figure 10 C). Both methods allow decent fitting of the 
TiO2 optical bandgap (Figure 10 D). 

22 
 
 
 
Figure 10. (A) Tauc plots of pure TiO2, MO and MO + TiO2 samples with different MO addition (a, b, c), 
including the Tauc lines. (B) Tauc plots after subtraction of the MO contribution from the spectrum. C) Tauc plot 
of MO + TiO2 with baseline fitting. (D) Table with the optical band gaps obtained from the methods in the plots 
of panels A-C. Adapted from ref. 78 Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. 
With mixed phases, multiple bandgaps of intermixed semiconductor phases may be present. 
Alternatively a single semiconductor material may boast both an indirect bandgap energy and 
a direct bandgap energy, and the different values of 𝛾−1 should be evaluated. Finally, one 
should avoid vertically correcting the absorption spectrum based on a false assumption of 
incomplete light collection.81 
When this is all done correctly, there can still be a significant method-dependence in drawing 
the tangent line. An automated approach is therefore advised, for example as developed by 
Schwarting et al.82 Note that also when using an algorithmic bandgap derivation, the outcome 
should be evaluated equally critically. 
Alternative methods were proposed to deal with the limitations of Tauc plots. Some are of the 
same type as the Tauc method, but are based on slightly different assumptions.83 These include 
the Cody method (eq 12), which generally yields better linearity and therefore a less ambiguous 
fit,84 and the Sokolov or Mott-Davis modifications of the Tauc method.85 
 
(α/ℎ𝑣)1/γ = 𝐵(ℎ𝑣−𝐸𝑔) 
(12) 
In still other methods simply the inflection point is taken as the direct optical band gap, or a 
certain cut-off value of the absorption coefficient is chosen. Zanetta et al. propose the fitting 
of a sigmoidal curve onto the pure semiconductor edge to derive both the direct and indirect 
optical bandgaps (Figure 11).86 One may ask the question what the definition of an optical 
bandgap should be, and which methods yield the most correct result in agreement with this 
definition. For now, it is mostly advised to be clear in the methodology of obtaining the reported 
optical bandgaps. 
 

23 
 
 
 
Figure 11. Absorption spectra and Boltzmann sigmoidal curve fitting with indication of the derived direct and 
indirect optical bandgap energies for crystalline Germanium. Adapted from ref. 86. Copyright 2019 Springer 
Nature. 
2.3 Coupling to complementary techniques 
The measurement of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra enables fast, cheap, and non-destructive 
measurement of experimental parameters related to the electronic structure of the species under 
investigation, and to kinetic properties. The technique is also flexible in measurement 
temperature and pressure. However, it is almost never sufficient to derive a full characterization. 
A few challenges are relevant. The computational simulation of absorption spectra is not very 
accurate (errors of 2000 cm−1 are not uncommon, even with complete active space perturbation 
theory 2 (CASPT2)).87, 88 The electronic structure parameters give only indirect information on 
the geometry, and especially on the elemental composition. In many applications, the 
absorption spectrum is poorly resolved and is a superposition of several unrelated spectra of 
coexisting species. Therefore, the link between a model structure and its simulated spectrum 
on the one hand and the actual structure and its experimental spectrum is ambiguous. Moreover, 
UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopies may encounter more difficulties than other spectroscopies 
in measuring across phases (e.g. through bubbles in electrocatalysis) and on strongly absorbing 
support materials (e.g. graphene), and it is surface specific. For certain applications, 
interference by fluorescence and phosphorescence may be an issue. Especially in the UV range, 
irradiation damage can still occur giving erroneous results, especially for organic materials. 
For these reasons UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is rarely used as a standalone 
characterization technique. As will repeatedly be demonstrated in section 3, and summarized 
in Table 1, multiple spectroscopies and other analytical techniques are usually combined. These 
can provide complementary information on elemental composition, atomic vibrations, 
oxidation states, stability, electronic properties, geometry, reactivity and catalysis, … . Some 
commonly used complementary techniques are listed in Table 4. Some complementary 
techniques can easily be implemented simultaneously under the same conditions (‘in situ’). For 

24 
 
 
some combinations specific cells or setups (e.g. stopped flow) may be required to achieve good 
in situ overlap, for others still sample isolation is needed. For example, through a rapid freeze 
quench (RFQ) where the reactants are first rapidly mixed and then frozen in a medium (e.g. 
isopentane) after an optimized mixing time that maximizes the concentration of an intermediate 
as determined through stopped-flow kinetic measurements.68 Alternatively, closed sampling 
systems can be used. 
Examples of in situ cells include those used for UV-VIS-NIR absorption in combination with 
Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), EPR, various synchrotron measurements, ….4-6, 39, 89-92 
Also in this category are operando measurements of catalysis, spectrophotometric titration, 
temperature programmed desorption, -oxidation and -reduction, and kinetics. Alternatively the 
complementary measurements can be ‘ex situ’, after isolating a sample. Some examples using 
a quench procedure are found in section 3.3.1. For some analytical techniques popular in 
catalysis research, UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is an excellent partner. Three 
examples stand out. 1) The ‘in situ’ monitoring of radiative damage in X-ray experiments,93, 94 
2) providing a fast response operando spectroscopy dimension to kinetic measurements with 
online MS or GC analysis (e.g. TAP, SSITKA, step response),95-98 and 3) selectively probing 
specific absorption features which we elaborate on further. 
Table 4. Characterization techniques used simultaneously with in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. 
Complementary technique 
Derived information 
DRIFTS 
Bond vibration energies, change in dipole moment selective 
Raman 
Bond vibration energies, change in polarizability selective 
NMR 
Element specific atomic chemical environment 
EPR 
Electron spin and its interactions 
EXAFS 
Element specific, sample averaged local structure 
XANES 
Element specific, sample averaged oxidation state & coordination 
XPS 
Electron binding energies 
XRD 
Crystallographic structure 
GC 
Product characterisation and quantification 
MS 
Product characterisation and quantification 
Voltammetry 
Electrochemical properties 
The complementary information derived from the coupled techniques enables site-selective 
spectroscopy, where the spectroscopic data are unambiguously linked to the same UV-VIS-
NIR absorption band. Most notably is the combined use of absorption, MCD and resonance 
Raman (rR), originally popular in the study of metalloenzymes, but by now also applied to 
TMI zeolites and other catalysts. The features typically analyzed in Raman are those where the 
scattered light is redshifted from the incident radiation by the energy of a vibrational mode 
(referred to as Stokes scattering). The selection rules for Raman are based on the change in the 
polarizability of the molecule upon distortion in the vibrational mode, providing a method to 
view vibrations complementary to those observed through infrared spectroscopy.99 Raman 
spectroscopy often involves weak transitions that are difficult to observe in dilute mixtures. 
Resonance Raman spectroscopy solves this issue. Whereas normal Raman scattering involves 
using a laser at a non-absorbing wavelength, rR specifically tunes a laser into an intense 
absorption feature, generally a LMCT transition. In the charge transfer (CT) excited state, the 
molecule distorts along ligand-metal bonds, selectively enhancing the vibrations associated 
with this excited state distortion. This resonance Raman effect greatly enhances the intensity 
of these totally symmetric vibrational modes. In the case of metalloenzymes, this allows 
vibrational modes of an active site buried in a dilute protein to be clearly observed. Additionally, 
it associates these vibrations with a particular charge transfer band (and its associated 
intermediate in a mixture) as the intensity is only enhanced when the laser is tuned into the 
absorption band. Based on the types of vibrations enhanced, this can also provide additional 

25 
 
 
information on the nature of the charge transfer band as the enhanced vibrations indicate how 
the molecule is distorted in the excited state.99 Examples for the identification of catalytic active 
sites and intermediated are given for Cu and Fe zeolites in section 3.1.2. and for oxy-
hemocyanin in section 3.3.2. 
3. Research in catalysis with in situ UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy 
3.1 Heterogeneous thermal catalysis 
In situ UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy has been applied to metal (oxide) clusters and TMIs 
dispersed on zeolites, and other high-surface-area metal oxides, which show remarkable 
catalytic performance in a range of reactions. Studies concerning TMI zeolites are particularly 
relevant as they can host well defined TMI complexes. Regarding other metal oxides, research 
has focused on the tracking of surface properties of the catalyst, including oxidation state(s), 
defects and oxygen vacancies relevant to redox catalytic processes. This section highlights the 
in situ UV-VIS-NIR based research in the field of TMI-based heterogeneous catalysis and 
further presents the most recent works on supported metal oxide catalysts. Due to their partially 
filled d-shell and multiple oxidation and spin states, TMIs are relevant to the catalysis of 
oxidation-reduction reactions.100-102 Most of these reactions are carried out in the liquid or in 
the gas phase. In these applications, the use of solid catalysts is beneficial as separation of the 
catalyst and reaction mixture is facilitated. As such, supported transition metal catalysts show 
promise as they combine the superior redox capabilities of TMIs with the practical advantages 
of using a stable, solid support.100, 103, 104 The section also covers the measurement of 
chromophoric organic products from zeolite based acid catalysis. Recent research on methanol 
to hydrocarbons is shown. 
3.1.1 Tracking metal and metal oxide bulk and surface properties 
Metals and metal oxides supported on non-microporous oxides are widely used commercial 
catalysts. They are preferred for their easily scaled up synthesis, simple structure, stability, and 
good thermal and mass transport properties. Their versatile elemental composition provides the 
freedom to substitute elements (doping) and many oxides exist in amorphous form as well as 
in different crystal phases with diverging properties.105, 106 Besides functioning as structural 
supports, the oxides also influence catalysis through induced electronegativity and 
polarizability or by offering storage for surface intermediates.107 In many cases, the oxides play 
an active role in catalysis through oxygen vacancies, surface functional groups, surface defects 
or electron-hole pairs, and these can be tracked with UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopies. The 
active oxides may in turn be supported on porous materials or on other oxides. They may be 
the only catalytically active phase or they may be a contributing phase to a bi- or 
multifunctional catalyst.108  
3.1.1.1 Supported noble metal nanoparticles 
Nanometer scale metal particles (NPs) boast increased surface-to-volume ratio, and electronic 
and geometric structures that differ from the bulk.109, 110 This unlocks their remarkable catalytic 
properties, particularly when dispersed on a support. The support can play a direct role in the 
catalytic performance or an indirect one. It may influence the size and morphology of the metal 
NPs, enable charge transfer from or to the metal, provide metal-support interactions, or 
stabilize the active metal species.111, 112 The catalytic performance of these materials therefore 
depends on numerous aspects which can be difficult to unravel and control. Several of these 
properties have been tracked with UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. 

26 
 
 
Surface plasmon measurements on noble metal nanoparticles 
Noble metals exhibit localized surface plasmon resonance (LSPR), a unique optical feature in 
the UV-VIS-NIR range from the oscillation of electrons in the conduction band of metal NPs 
with an electromagnetic field.113 The LSPR spectrum holds information on the size and shape 
of the metal NPs, as well as on the refractive index (RI) of the surrounding media.114, 115 These 
are relevant properties to catalysis, and a handful of articles have tracked LSPR bands in this 
context, mainly focused on gold-based catalysts. 
Au-based catalysts catalyze a variety of oxidation and hydrogenation reactions, such as CO 
oxidation,116-118 the water-gas shift (WGS), the reverse water-gas shift (RWGS),119-121 and the 
hydrogenation of CO and CO2 to methanol.122, 123 Understanding the interaction of Au 
supported catalysts with O2 and H2 is of interest to identify key intermediates and conversions. 
Srinivasan et al. studied the adsorption of oxygen and hydrogen at 398 K by in situ UV-VIS 
DRS in a series of Au catalysts supported on SiO2, Al2O3, ZrO2, ZnO and TiO2 P-25.98 The Au 
plasmon peak position was analyzed as a function of the exposure time for 5 hours. Before 
exposure to an oxidative or reductive atmosphere, a broad Au plasmon centered at ~570 nm 
was measured. After exposure to O2, the peak slightly redshifted in all cases, whereas the 
subsequent exposure to H2 caused a blueshift. The blueshift indicates an increase in the 
conduction band electron density due to charge transfer from H2 to the Au NPs. Interestingly, 
the largest Au plasmon energy shifts occurred for catalysts supported on reducible oxides 
(Au/ZnO (Δλ= 11 nm) and Au/TiO2 (Δλ= 9 nm)) (Figure 12). Although TiO2 is more rapidly 
reduced by H2 than ZnO, the higher Δλ on the ZnO-supported catalyst indicates a smaller Au 
particle size on ZnO than on TiO2. This demonstrated that the adsorption capacity is a function 
of both the reducibility of the support and the Au surface sites density. 
 
Figure 12. Au plasmon position changes as determined from in situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy after 
exposure to alternating H2 and O2 flows at 398 K. Reproduced from 98. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. 
In the most accepted hypotheses, adsorption and catalysis occur at the gold-support interface.124, 
125 To evaluate the nature of the active sites in the Au-based catalysts, the authors analyzed the 
relative change of electron density (ΔN/N) in Au NPs during O2-H2 switching with in situ UV-
VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. After flushing with He, O2 and H2 were found to adsorb 
only weakly on undercoordinated (coordination number = 6,7) top sites, indicating that the 
majority of O2 or H2 species should adsorb at the Au-support interface. For further 
characterization of the adsorbed species, UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is less 

27 
 
 
informative and complementary in situ and operando IR or Raman spectroscopies are usually 
used. In this sense, Wang et al. combined transient in situ diffuse reflectance infrared fourier 
transform spectroscopy (DRIFTS) and UV-VIS-NIR reflectance spectroscopy to monitor the 
changes induced by the CO oxidation reactants (CO and O2) in the Au NPs structure of an 
Au/TiO2 catalyst.126 FTIR results showed a sudden intensity increase of the CO vibrational 
band together with an intensity decrease of the CO2 band and a redshift of the CO band. The 
increase of the adsorbed CO band was not related to an increase of the CO2 formation rate, 
indicating that during this unstable state the O2 coverage was decreased probably due to 
structural changes on the Au/TiO2 surface, which were tracked by in situ UV-VIS-NIR 
reflectance spectroscopy. UV-VIS-NIR absorption analysis of the Au/TiO2 LSPR at ~600 nm 
(Figure 13 B) showed that exposure to the CO + O2 mixture immediately affected the LSPR, 
pointing at a change in the Au electronic properties. 
 
Figure 13. (A) Integrated UV reflectance in the 750-950 nm range as a function of time during CO adsorption, 
O2 adsorption and CO oxidation at 20 and 100 °C. (B) UV-VIS DRS spectra of TiO2 and Au/TiO2. Reproduced 
from 126. Copyright 2022 Springer Nature. 
The LSPR region was not uniformly affected. The 500-600 nm region changed little while in 
the 750-950 nm region reflectance increased, indicating that only a subset of the Au NPs was 
involved (Figure 13 A). In good agreement with the FTIR results, the addition of the CO + O2 
mixture first sharply decreased the 750-950 nm reflection to then grow back. The change in the 
Au NPs structure that leads to the change in LSPR can therefore be related to the deactivation 
period. The separate introduction of CO and O2 demonstrated that the Au structural changes 
were induced by CO adsorption, but also that in the presence of CO, O2 was needed for the 
reconstruction of the Au NPs. It was shown that O2 adsorption on Au/TiO2 is hindered, leading 
to a lower CO oxidation activity before reaching the steady state characterized by an enhanced 
catalytic performance. 
Effect of the catalytic support: transition metal and rare-earth oxides 
Transition metal and rare-earth (lanthanide) oxides have some properties that make them 
interesting support materials for catalysis. TiO2 and CeO2 have been highlighted for their 
inherent redox properties derived from intermixed oxidation states (Ti3+/Ti4+ and Ce3+/Ce4+ 
pairs), responsible for the presence of oxygen defects with catalytic relevance. In situ UV-VIS-
NIR absorption spectroscopy has been a useful tool to study these effects by virtue of its 
sensitivity to different oxidation states.8 
The RWGS reaction (eq 13) is commonly catalysed with oxide supported gold catalysts. Two 
main mechanisms are known: the redox mechanism and the associative mechanism.127, 128 The 

28 
 
 
redox mechanism applies to Au nanoparticles supported on reducible supports, where CO2 
reoxidizes the partially reduced support, leading to the formation of CO. In the associative 
mechanism, CO is formed from the decomposition of formate species derived from the 
interaction of CO2 with H2. Since formate species cannot be decomposed on Au nanoparticles, 
their evolution during reaction is associated with the support material. 
 
CO2 + H2 ↔ CO + H2O 
(13) 
Bobadilla et al. investigated the mechanism of the RWGS with Au/Al2O3 and Au/TiO2 
commercial catalysts.129 The catalytic activity of Au/TiO2 is superior to that of Au/Al2O3, and 
based on DRIFTS results the associative mechanism was followed with Au/Al2O3 catalyst 
while the redox mechanism was followed with Au/TiO2. TiO2 in its reduced form (Ti3+) is 
known to contain a high concentration of oxygen vacancies, which act as sites for CO2 
activation. The authors studied the importance of the TiO2 oxygen vacancies through the 
reduction of Ti4+ to Ti3+ using in situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy during activation with 
H2 and the subsequent RWGS reaction. At room temperature, the spectra of pristine TiO2 
(Figure 14 A) show the TiO2 band gap at ~320 nm. H2 causes the appearance of a broad band 
in the 400-800 nm range, indicative of the reduction of Ti4+ to Ti3+. The wide absorption band 
arises from a continuum of electronic energy levels derived from oxygen vacancies. After 
switching to the reaction mixture, the 400-800 nm band decreased in intensity, suggesting the 
participation of the oxygen vacancies in the reaction mechanism. The plasmon of the Au 
nanoparticles partially overlaps with the band gap edge of TiO2 (Figure 14 B), so one should 
be careful with its influence on the band gap estimate. In the difference spectra (Figure 15) a 
positive difference band centered at ~350 nm is observed, and attributed to the formation of 
Ti3+ modifying the bandgap absorption band. The positive difference feature at 400-800 nm 
confirms the involvement of oxygen vacancies.  
 
Figure 14. In situ UV-VIS absorption spectra as a function of the temperature of (A) TiO2 P25 and (B) Au/TiO2 
catalyst during activation under H2 (orange) and RWGS reaction (blue). Reprinted with permission from 129. 
Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. 

29 
 
 
 
Figure 15. Au/TiO2 UV-VIS-NIR difference absorption spectra of before and after reaction, for different reaction 
temperatures. Reprinted with permission from 129. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. 
The RWGS catalytic performance on Au/CeO2 is related to the presence of oxygen vacancies 
and to the particle shape of the support.130 Ceria polyhedra (which terminate with the CeO2 
(111) surface) and polycrystalline ceria (which resembles sheets and contains stepped sites) 
were evaluated as supports by Ziemba et al. using a combination of in situ Raman spectroscopy, 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy, and transient DRIFTS.131 Changes in the degree of 
CeO2 reduction were tracked following the evolution of a VIS absorption band at 550 nm, 
attributed to a charge transfer from Ce3+ to Ce4+.132 At room temperature, the spectra of 
Au/CeO2 catalysts show a ~550 nm absorption band of Au surface plasmons. The CeO2 
polyhedra supported materials give stronger surface plasmon absorption, due to a higher 
concentration of gold metallic species. When the flow is switched from Ar to H2/Ar, an increase 
of the 550 nm absorption is observed for every sample, indicating the reduction of the CeO2 
support. After addition of the reaction mixture (CO2/H2/Ar), the absorption intensity decreases 
due to the re-oxidation of CeO2. These results evidence changes in the oxygen dynamics as a 
function of the gas atmosphere. 
To gain futher insights, the gold-loaded samples were analyzed by in situ UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption spectroscopy under reductive (H2/Ar) and oxidative (CO2/Ar) pretreatments 
followed by the RWGS reaction (CO2/H2/Ar) (Figure 16). Under CO2 atmosphere, a decrease 
of the band intensity is observed, especially in the catalyst supported on polycrystalline CeO2, 
illustrating that this CeO2 morphology is more prone to oxidation than the polyhedral 
counterpart. When the flow is switched to the CO2 and H2 mixture, the 550 nm absorption band 
increases, particularly for the Au/polycrystalline CeO2 sample. The RWGS reaction after a 
reductive pretreatment, however, has little effect on the absorption band. Together, the results 
indicate that the redox mechanism is predominant on bare CeO2 samples, although the 
associative mechanism also participates in the CeO2 polyhedra. The pure redox mechanism in 
polycrystalline CeO2 is related to the higher reducibility of the support. The associative 
mechanism is dominant on Au/CeO2, although the redox mechanism also takes place.  
 

30 
 
 
 
Figure 16. In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of Au/CeO2 samples during H2 pretreatment, CO2 
pretreatment and RWGS reaction. Reproduced from 131. Copyright 2022 Elsevier.  
The same group used time-resolved in situ Raman spectroscopy and UV-VIS DRS to study the 
effect of the pretreatment in the oxygen dynamics of the support in a 0.5 wt.% Au/CeO2 catalyst 
and its influence on the room temperature CO oxidation.133 
The reduction state of the support was elucidated by monitoring of the F2g Raman band and the 
570 nm UV-VIS band, as shown in Figure 17. The reducing pretreatment allows a higher 
degree of CeO2 reduction than the oxidative one. Under reaction conditions a fast re-oxidation 
of the CeO2 surface is detected, but the reducing pretreatment draws out the re-oxidation of the 
CeO2 subsurface. These results show a correlation with the catalytic activity, which is higher 
after the catalyst is subjected to a reducing pretreatment, thus showing a strong influence of the 
subsurface reduction state in the CO oxidation activity.  
 
Figure 17. Catalytic activity (black), time-dependent Raman (blue) and UV-Vis bands (brown) evolution for the 
CeO2 subsurface reduction state in a 0.5 wt% Au/CeO2 catalyst, (A) after two reaction cycles, (B) during CO 
oxidation after reducing pretreatment. Reproduced from ref. 133. 
Spectroscopic results also show that no charge transfer takes place from the support to the Au 
NPs, contrary to what was previously observed for Au/TiO2 catalysts. In the Au/CeO2 catalyst, 

31 
 
 
the electron is proposed to remain in the support (Ce3+), accelerate oxygen activation and not 
hamper CO adsorption on the metal particles.  
3.1.1.2 Transition metal oxide catalysts 
Transition metal oxides (TMO) are catalytically interesting thanks to their partially filled d 
orbitals, which facilitate electron transfer between reactants and active sites.134 This feature is 
complex to investigate due to the multitude of oxidation states and coordination numbers, but 
is well suited for study with UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy.135 Bensalem et al. used in situ UV-
VIS-NIR DRS to study the reduction kinetics of chromium oxide catalysts supported on Al2O3 
and SiO2 in a CO/He mixed atmosphere.136 By combining in situ UV-VIS-NIR DRS, on-line 
GC analysis and experimental design, Weckhuysen et al. correlated the activity of CrOx/Al2O3 
in the dehydrogenation of isobutane with the oxidation state of chromium surface species. The 
combined amount of surface Cr2+ and Cr3+ species, tracked by ‘pure component’ absorption 
fingerprints constructed by PCA, is a good predictor of dehydrogenation activity.137 
Supported VOx catalysts have also been extensively studied due to their promising catalytic 
behavior in partial oxidation, SCR or oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) reactions. In situ UV-
VIS-NIR DRS has provided information about the reduced states of VOx supported on Al2O3, 
SiO2, ZrO2 or TiO2.138-140 Among them, TiO2 was shown to significantly enhance the catalytic 
properties of VOx.141-143 Godiksen et al. studied the V2O5/TiO2 system for its application in the 
NH3 assisted SCR, using combined transient UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy and in situ 
Raman spectroscopy.144 A 3% V2O5/TiO2 catalyst was first activated at 400 °C under air and 
then exposed to an SCR gas mixture of 500 ppm NH3, 500 ppm NO, and increasing O2 
concentrations in He from 0.8 to 10 vol.% O2.  
Raman spectra after activation shows a vanadyl band at ca. 1030 cm−1, ascribed to the terminal 
V=O bond on anatase. During exposure to NH3 and NO at 400 °C, the vanadyl band undergoes 
a redshift and decreases in intensity. This is probably due to the lower absorption coefficient 
of V4+=O compared to V5+=O, but may also indicate a deeper vanadium reduction to V3+. The 
addition of O2 gives rise to V4+=O and V5+=O species, with an increase of V5+=O species with 
increasing O2 partial pressure. UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra were recorded during the flow 
change from O2 to the NH3 and NO mixture. The difference spectra show a decrease of the 
band at 25 500 cm−1, attributed to CT transitions, as well as the appearance of a band at 15 000 
cm−1 (Figure 18). The latter is ascribed to d-d transitions of octahedral VO2+,145 thus indicating 
a reduction of V5+ to V4+. The UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra were compared to those 
obtained during H2-TPR. Vanadium undergoes a two-electron reduction under H2 yielding V3+, 
whereas under a NH3 + NO mixture, the reduction is a one-electron process yielding V4+. V4+ 
species were proposed to be part of a [VIV=O]2+ unit, detectable in UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectroscopy due to its d1 electron configuration. Further confirmation of this oxidations state 
by EPR was not achieved in the study due to paramagnetic interaction at high V loadings. 

32 
 
 
 
Figure 18. In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption difference spectra of a 3% V2O5/TiO2 catalyst during the gas flow 
change from O2 (green) to NO + NH3 (pink). Reproduced from 144. Copyright 2019 Elsevier. 
Hess and co-workers studied the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane over VOx/TiO2 
(P25) catalysts with different vanadium surface densities (2.5, 1.2, 0.5 and 0.1 V/nm2).140 A 
combination of operando UV-Raman, solid state NMR, transient IR and operando UV-VIS 
spectroscopies was used to elucidate the propane ODH reaction mechanism. Bare P25 and 
vanadia-loaded samples were studied by operando UV-VIS spectroscopy under oxidizing and 
reactive conditions to understand the role of reduced vanadia in the reaction mechanism. When 
switching from oxidative to reactive conditions, the band gap slightly red-shifts and an 
absorption band at 520 nm emerges in the UV-VIS spectra of P25 , ascribed to a charge-transfer 
transition due to reduction of Ti4+ to Ti3+, and thus implying the formation of oxygen vacancies. 
This points to the oxidation of propane by the support lattice oxygen, leading to the formation 
of oxygen vacancies. UV-VIS spectra of the vanadia-loaded samples do not show a band gap 
energy shift nor a Ti3+ transfer signal, except for the sample containing 0.1 V/nm2. An intensity 
increase due to vanadium d-d transitions indicates the reduction of V5+ to V3+/V4+. 
Yun et al. used in situ Raman spectroscopy and UV-VIS-NIR DRS to investigate the electronic 
changes to VOx/TiO2 by nitrogen doping.146 They then correlated the electronic structure of the 
vanadium clusters with the activity for selective ethanol to acetaldehyde oxidation. The authors 
derived the average number of vanadium-oxygen-vanadium covalent bonds (CVB) from the 
edge energy (Eg) values collected from the UV-VIS-NIR absorption band through the 
empirically determined eq 14. 
 
CVB = 14.03 – 3.95Eg (eV) 
(14) 
Changes in the onset energy of the edge (Eg’) were used to monitor the formation of reduced 
vanadium centers during the partial oxidation reaction. The UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra 
of the sample supported on pristine TiO2 (Figure 19 A) are dominated by a broad band in the 
1.0-3.5 eV range, ascribed to LMCT transitions of tetrahedral V5+ moieties. The broad and 
weak nature of the absorption in the d-d transition energy range prevents the proper 
identification of individual V4+ components. The redshift of the peak and the appearance of a 
tail are indicative of the presence of vanadium polymeric species.139, 147, 148 

33 
 
 
 
Figure 19. In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of (A) VOx/TiO2 after reduction with ethanol at different 
temperatures for 1 h; (B) VOx/N-TiO2 catalysts with varying nitrogen content, after reduction with ethanol at 
200 °C for 1 h. Inset: UV-VIS-NIR absorption intensity at reduced centers as a function of N/V mol ratio. Adapted 
from 146. Copyright 2018 Elsevier. 
After reduction with ethanol at temperatures ranging from 100 to 250 °C, Eg’ increases with 
increasing reduction temperature in the N-free samples from 1.5 to 1.8 eV. The values are lower 
than those previously reported for oxide-supported VOx reduced species,138, 149 due to overlap 
of the V4+ and Ti3+ signals. The spectrum of the N-free sample after reduction at 200 °C was 
compared to those obtained for a series of VOx/N-TiO2 samples with different nitrogen contents. 
In the latter, the d-d bands are blue-shifted (Figure 19 B), indicating a larger reduction of 
vanadia and resulting in an increased number of active sites. Raman spectroscopy reveales 
minimal geometrical changes of the vanadia moieties, and the exceptional catalytic 
performance of the N-doped samples was attributed to the enhanced reducibility of VOx species 
in the presence of nitrogen. 
Recently, cubic In2O3 has emerged as efficient catalyst for CO2 activation.150, 151 Ziemba et al. 
used a combination of in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption and impedance spectroscopies to 
elucidate the CO2 activation process, the role of oxygen vacancies, and the participation of the 
adsorbates in the RWGS reaction.152 The VIS absorption band indicative of oxygen defects at 
700 nm was monitored under different gas atmospheres and temperatures, to understand the 
evolution of the oxygen defects (Figure 20 A). When switching between O2 and H2 at 130 °C, 
the spectrum shows an increase of the absorbance with the simultaneous detection of gas phase 
H2O by IR spectroscopy, indicating the reduction of In2O3. Under reaction conditions at the 
same temperature, no CO was detected in the gas phase since CO2 is not activated at such low 
temperature. However, the absorbance continues to increase with exposure to H2 through In2O3 
reduction. At 160 °C In2O3 reduction continues, but still no CO is detected. At 220 °C, the 
RWGS reaction occurs, giving rise to an absorption increase indicative of catalyst reduction. 
The absorbance then decreases over the next 30 minutes. The decrease of the absorption is the 
result of CO2 conversion, leading to re-oxidation of the surface. The maximum absorption is 
observed at 190 °C and decreases with temperature, suggesting that catalyst oxidation by CO2 
increases with the temperature, whereas reduction remains constant since the maximum 
amount of oxygen vacancies was reached. These results suggest CO2 activation and subsequent 
CO formation as the rate-limiting step. The above behaviour is confirmed by UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption experiments at 250 °C, while switching between reactive and reducing conditions 

34 
 
 
(Figure 20 B). A significant increase of the VIS absorption band at 532 nm can be related to 
the temperature increase and/or the oxidation. Subsequent H2 exposure causes an increase of 
both the absorptions at 532 and 700 nm, together with the detection of H2O in the gas phase, 
pointing out the reduction of the (sub)surface and the presence of small metallic indium 
domains. Under CO2 atmosphere, a decrease in absorption at 700 nm occurs indicating the 
reversibility of the reduction process. 
 
Figure 20. (A) In situ and operando VIS 700 nm absorption band evolution and CO2 conversion during O2 and 
H2 exposure at 130 °C followed by RWGS reaction from 130 to 280 °C. (B) Top: In situ and operando UV-VIS-
NIR absorption results for In2O3 under different gas exposures at 250 °C. Bottom: Gas-phase IR analysis during 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption measurements. Reproduced from 152. Copyright 2022 Wiley. 
3.1.2 Identification and tracking of dispersed catalytic molecular clusters 
In the investigation of transition metal complexes the use of UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopies 
has been indispensable. The examples in the later sections on homogeneous and enzymatic 
catalysis demonstrate their advanced characterization based on absorption, CD and MCD 
spectroscopies coupled to many more complementary spectroscopies. Their susceptibility to 
advanced spectroscopic characterization generally enables detailed understanding of the 
geometry and electronic structure on the molecular level, and consequently of the catalysis. 
Similar examples exist on heterogeneous catalysts where TMI active sites are successfully 
dispersed into well-defined molecular clusters. Particularly on TMI exchanged zeolites the 
advances have been spectacular in the preceding decade. 
Zeolites are porous, crystalline aluminosilicates, built from linked [SiO4] and [AlO4]− 
tetrahedra with every oxygen atom shared between neighboring T-atoms.32, 33 Different zeolite 
topologies are distinguished by their crystal structure and pore system. Every [AlO4]− leads to 
the presence of one negative lattice charge which can be charge compensated by exchangeable, 
extra-framework cations. These can be protons, giving zeolites their well-known acidic 
properties,34 or (transition) metal cations. The zeolite matrix thus serves as a support to the acid 
or metal active sites, usually with a variety of exchange sites offering different coordination 
geometries and catalytic properties. Relevant zeolite topologies and their exchange sites are 

35 
 
 
shown in Figure 21. Six or eight membered ring exchange sites will be referred to again in this 
review and relate to any of the sites in the insert of Figure 21 that have open rings of six or 
eight tetrahedrons. The crystalline support gives rise to well defined active sites that – to some 
degree – can be tuned by varying the zeolite topology and the aluminum distribution in the 
lattice. With these levers, the effect of structural modifications on catalysis can be studied. 
Additionally, atoms outside the first coordination sphere can contribute to reactivity by guiding 
the approach of substrate or tuning the TS. These influences are referred to as ‘second-sphere 
effects’.26, 100, 153 Spectra of TMI-exchanged zeolites tend to be complex superpositions of 
absorption bands from different TMI species, coordinated in the different exchange sites. Their 
interpretation is usually complicated, but less so than those of TMI supported on many other 
materials by virtue of their strong tendency to disperse the supported TMIs. In some cases, the 
TMI species in the zeolite can be kept down to (quasi) single site, and spectroscopy becomes 
more evident. 
 
Figure 21. Framework structures and exchange sites of zeolite topologies mentioned in this review. Colors of the 
exchange sites match their naming in the legend. Adapted from ref. 154. Copyright 2021 Elsevier. 
3.1.2.1 UV-VIS-NIR fingerprinting on Cu and Fe exchanged zeolites 
In situ UV-VIS-NIR studies are especially numerous for copper and iron exchanged zeolites 
with applications in direct oxidation of methane to methanol (MTM),155, 156 deNOx catalysis,157-
159 and oxidation of benzene to phenol.160-162 In these reactions essential steps are the activation 
of N2O and O2 to form reactive surface bound oxygen species that perform challenging 
selective oxidation reactions. The first is the µ-oxo-dicopper(II) site,163-167 the second is the α-
O site (a mononuclear FeIV=O).17, 168-170 The unique spectroscopic handles of µ-oxo-dicopper 
and α-O in zeolites give the opportunity to study reaction kinetics and mechanisms by 
following the intensity changes of the relevant bands. Moreover, with good understanding of 
structure-spectroscopy relations, small structural changes in the species under investigation can 
be tracked spectroscopically and their impact on the catalytic processes can be studied.171 
The first step is to determine the structure of the reactive oxygen sites and other intermediates, 
to unambiguously link the site-specific UV-VIS-NIR absorption handles to the processes of 
interest. High resolution room temperature UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra were collected in 
diffuse reflectance mode using a quartz cell under controlled atmosphere of the materials after 
exposure and reaction with O2, nitrogen oxides, CH4 and benzene.17, 162, 163, 170 These 

36 
 
 
measurements are in situ in the sense that the sample is reacted and analysed in the same cell 
preserving the atmosphere. The information from UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy alone 
is insufficient to be conclusive on structure and electronic properties. To obtain the required 
information, the controlled atmosphere UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy was coupled to 
in situ resonance Raman (rR), Mössbauer, MCD, and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS, 
including X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES), extended X-ray absorption fine 
structure (EXAFS), nuclear resonance vibrational spectroscopy (NRVS)) measurements 
without disturbing the atmosphere. For the first two, borosilicate transfer tubes were connected 
to the quartz reactor, for the latter three a sample preserving preparation method in perfluoro-
2-methylpentane was used.17, 163, 169 
The combination of UV-VIS-NIR absorption, resonance Raman, and MCD spectroscopies is 
particularly powerful. They all use excitations in the UV-VIS-NIR range and the spectroscopic 
features at the same excitation energies are unambiguously related.100 The association of 
Mössbauer, XAS and NRVS spectral features with identical active sites, on the other hand, 
relies more strongly on statistical correlation of their appearance and on theoretical 
assumptions (incl. molecular modelling).  
Operando UV-VIS-NIR absorption measurements were then used to correlate the structurally 
assigned spectroscopic handles to reactivity. Finally, time resolved in situ UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption spectroscopy on selected spectroscopic handles provided site-selective kinetic data 
quantitatively linking reactivity to structure. The operando kinetic analysis with in situ UV-
VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is however rarely straightforward. In situ spectroscopy 
provides lower resolution and sensitivity, and usually more targeted synthesis is needed to 
increase active site loading and purity. This improved synthesis could be guided by the 
previously acquired structural information (for example see refs. 166, 168, 171, 172). 
If the absorption bands are particularly weak and broad, a careful background correction on the 
spectral baseline becomes essential and the baseline should be constant during the experiment. 
The latter condition requires that the reaction is highly selective to avoid contamination of the 
kinetic measurement from parallel transformations that give overlapping changes in the UV-
VIS-NIR absorption spectrum. This has been a particular challenge with Cu zeolites. Multiple 
[CuOCu]2+ species at different locations in the zeolite as well as [CuIIOH]+ and spectator 
species are present in the same material.166 The spectators do not take part in the reaction, but 
might have absorption bands that overlap with those of the active site. Likely some published 
quantitative kinetic data is contaminated by at the time unknown reactive sites that react 
simultaneously.164 The amount of spectator Fe species, on the other hand, can easily be limited 
to < 10% through careful synthesis and low loadings.172 
Another challenge is the limited area of the catalyst bed that is probed by the optic fiber used 
in in situ/operando UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy. This makes the measurement sensitive to 
inhomogeneities in the catalyst bed and in the flow, but also to disturbances in the setup. A 
stable flow and bed temperature must be established before reliable activity data can be 
collected. For relatively slow reactions the challenge is to keep the system stable over time, 
and multiple repeated measurements are recommended. Another solution is needed for very 
fast reactions where a stable flow cannot be achieved in time. The collection of kinetic data on 
the reaction of the extremely reactive alpha-O site with CH4 was attempted by cooling down 
with dry ice and liquid nitrogen, but condensation issues, methane condensation, and diffusion 
limitations remained problematic and a dedicated cell should be used.173 

37 
 
 
3.1.2.2 Partial oxidation of methane to methanol with Cu-zeolites 
The last two decades, both Cu- and Fe-exchanged zeolites have been researched for the 
selective oxidation of methane to methanol under milder conditions than in syngas routes. 
These reactions are generally run stoichiometrically because the recovery of the methanol is 
often achieved through addition of water, deactivating the catalyst. Fe-exchanged zeolites 
require N2O as oxidant while Cu exchanged zeolites can be activated with O2 as well.100 Recent 
work also reports the use of H2O as oxidant using Cu zeolites.174, 175 One of the challenges in 
this conversion, especially with N2O and O2, is to prevent thermodynamically favored 
overoxidation.176 Typically, the MTM process occurs on Cu-zeolites in a stepwise fashion to 
separate methane from the oxidant and prevent partial oxidation products from 
overoxidation.177 First, the Cu-zeolite is activated with O2 or N2O to form the active copper-
oxygen (Cu/O) species. This step can be split up into an autoreduction step in He at high 
temperature (~500 °C), and an activation with O2 or N2O at lower temperature (~200 °C).178, 
179 In the second step, methane is reacted over the copper zeolite at moderate temperatures, 
usually between 150 and 200 °C. The methoxy and methanol products are adsorbed onto the 
zeolite, and need to be extracted with water or steam.103, 179-182 Many different Cu/O species 
have been proposed in literature as the active sites for MTM.177 Mononuclear [CuOH]+ and 
binuclear [CuOCu]2+ species are described most often, based on spectroscopic results and ab 
initio modelling.163-165 Their reaction mechanisms are drawn in Figure 22. 
 
Figure 22. Reaction schemes and intermediates of methane to methanol over Cu-zeolites with (left) [CuOCu]2+ 
and (right) [CuIIOH]+ active sites, including the known UV-VIS-NIR absorption bands. 
The most analysed spectroscopic handle connected to MTM with copper zeolites is a ~22 000 
cm−1 absorption band found in Cu-MFI,163 Cu-MOR,153, 164, 167, 183 and Cu-CHA.165 The ~22 
000 cm−1 absorption band, through its rR profile, is assigned as a bridging oxo → Cu2+ LMCT 
of an extraframework [CuOCu]2+.164 Recently, also a [CuIIOH]+ in Cu-MOR was found to have 
an absorption band at 20 700 cm−1, while similar in energy to the 22 000 cm−1 band of 
[CuOCu]2+, they are easily distinguished from rR. 
which was assigned to an HO− → Cu2+ charge transfer.166 Given the similar energy of their 
absorption bands, there is a risk to interpret the results assuming only one of the two sites, and 
previously published results on site-specific reactivities must be assessed critically. To 
distinguish the two sites, the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is best coupled with rR and 
EPR spectroscopy. If accessible, cryogenic UV-VIS-NIR absorption may also be used to 
resolve the species. 

38 
 
 
MTM reaction kinetics are useful to disentangle and to compare kinetic parameters of different 
active sites for MTM versus methanol overoxidation.184, 185 Experimental kinetic parameters of 
elementary steps helped identify the electronic structure and crystallographic location.153, 164, 
186 In-silico proposed reaction mechanisms have been verified against the experimental 
activation barriers,153, 165 which has helped reveal structure-reactivity relationships.103, 153, 165 In 
situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy was used to study the mechanism of [CuOCu]2+ 
formation from a binuclear 2 Cu+ precursor in Cu-MFI, with O2 as well as through oxygen 
atom transfer (OAT) from N2O.187 The kinetics of N-O cleavage were evaluated by tracking 
the growth of the 22 700 cm−1 absorption band at different reaction temperatures from 25 to 
100 °C. The growth of the absorption band as a function of time was evaluated with a first 
order rate equation and the obtained rate constant at each particular temperature was used to 
construct an Arrhenius plot.⁡The resulting Arrhenius plot gave an apparent activation barrier 
Ea = 2.5 kcal/mol, which is consistent with DFT calculated apparent barriers of pathways with 
μ-1,1-O and μ-1,3-O,N N2O-bridged structures as intermediates in the conversion of 2 Cu+ to 
[CuOCu]2+ (Figure 23 A). 
When the 2 Cu+ precursor is contacted with O2 at room temperature, a 29 000 cm−1 absorption 
band appears (Figure 23 B). This band does not appear with N2O activation and was related to 
a μ-η2:η2 peroxo-bridged dicopper site [Cu2O2]2+ based on its characteristic resonance Raman 
vibrations. The peroxo species is not active in methane oxidation, but it transforms upon 
heating in inert or O2 atmosphere into [CuOCu]2+ with simultaneous growth of the 22 700 cm−1 
absorption band and loss of the 29 000 cm−1 absorption band. Where the second oxygen of the 
peroxo bridge ends up has not yet been confirmed. 
 
Figure 23. (A) Binding modes of N2O on the binuclear Cu+ center. (B) UV-VIS spectra of autoreduced Cu-MFI 
during O2 treatment at room temperature (top) and subsequent heating in He atmosphere to form [CuOCu]2+ 
(bottom). Adapted from refs. 187, 188. Copyright 2014 and 2010 American Chemical Society. 
The [CuOCu]2+ active sites react with methane with decay of the corresponding 22 700 cm−1 
absorption band and formation of 2 Cu+. The kinetics of the reaction were evaluated by 

39 
 
 
monitoring the disappearance of the absorption band at different temperatures on Cu-MFI 
(Figure 24).165 From the resulting Eyring plot, thermodynamic parameters of the transition state, 
including activation enthalpy ∆H⧧ and entropy ∆S⧧, were obtained (Table 5). Also, a 
significant KIE was found when reacting with CD4 instead of CH4, indicating that C-H bond 
breaking was involved in the rate limiting step of the CH4 oxidation.189 This was further 
confirmed by NMR product analysis of the reaction with CH2D2.  
Table 5. Comparison of the TS parameters for the [CuOCu]2+ sites in Cu-MFI and Cu-CHA from ref. 165 and for 
the [CuIIOH]+ site in Cu-MOR from ref. 166.

40 
 
 
 
Figure 24. (A) Decay of the 22 700 cm−1 absorption band in time during reaction of [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-MFI with 
methane at 90 °C (left) and Eyring plot for the reaction of CH4 with [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-MFI (right). (B) Decay of 
the 22 400 cm−1 absorption band in time during reaction of [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-CHA with methane at 80 °C (left) 
and Eyring plot for the reaction of CH4 with [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-CHA (right). (C) Decay of the 20 700 cm−1 
absorption band in time during reaction of [CuIIOH]+ in Cu-MOR with methane at 130 °C (left) and Eyring plot 
for the reaction of CH4 with [CuIIOH]+ in Cu-MOR (right). (D) Schematic of bidentate oxygen ligation of 
[CuOCu]2+ cores in Cu-MFI (green box) and Cu-CHA (purple box) assigning out of plane (OOP) and in plane 
(IP) ligation with respect to the CuOCu plane. (E) DFT-calculated potential energy surface of [CuOCu]2+ (purple) 
and of localized structure (green). Adapted from refs. 163, 165, 166. Copyright 2021 American Chemical Society, 
2009 Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences and 2022 American Chemical Society. 

41 
 
 
 
If the absorption band of the species under study overlaps with other absorption bands, one 
best integrates the absorption band used for kinetic tracking in a range where the influence of 
the superposing absorption band is limited. This is illustrated for the UV-VIS-NIR tracking of 
the reaction of [CuOCu]2+ with CH4 in Cu-CHA.165 The decay of the band during reaction at 
80 °C is shown in Figure 24 B. An isosbestic point was observed on the low energy side of the 
absorption band but not at the high energy side, indicating overlap with another higher energy 
absorption band connected to a different chemical transformation. Since a reliable 
deconvolution of the spectrum was unavailable, the integration was limited to the lower energy-
range. [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-CHA is significantly more reactive than the [CuOCu]2+ species in MFI. 
The high reactivity of Cu-CHA is due to a comparatively low enthalpy barrier ∆𝐻⧧ (Table 5). 
Further DFT calculations show that the relative rotations of the two bidentate oxygen ligands 
with respect to each other significantly affect the interaction between Cu atoms and the 
bridging oxo (Figure 24 D). The near 0° relative rotation of ligand sets in Cu-CHA leads to a 
destabilization of the [CuOCu]2+ core and makes it more reactive compared to the structural 
analogue in MFI, in which the bidentate ligands have a ~90° relative rotation. This shows how 
the rigid zeolite lattice can enforce an unstable active site geometry, reminiscent of the entatic 
state effect in metalloprotein chemistry. 
The 22 400 cm−1 DR UV-VIS absorption band was also observed in O2 or N2O activated Cu-
MOR, where it was shown to be an unresolved superposition of the absorption bands of three 
slightly distinct [CuOCu]2+ cores designated as MOR1, MOR2 and MOR3.153, 164, 167 MOR1 is 
in the eight membered ring side pocket that is exposed to the twelve membered ring channel. 
MOR2 and MOR3 are on the other side of the eight membered ring side pocket, across the 
compressed eight membered ring channel (see Figure 21) that runs parallel to the twelve 
membered ring main channel.164, 167 However, due to a lack of reliable deconvolution for the 
22 000 cm−1 absorption band into its three constituents, no accurate kinetics of each [CuOCu]2+ 
active site can be obtained. In ref. 164 the MOR1 and MOR2 CH4 activation energies were 
compared, but considering the more recently revealed contributions of also MOR3 and 
[CuIIOH]+ to the absorption in the ~22 000 cm−1 region, the ∆𝐻⧧ and ∆𝑆⧧ values should be re-
evaluated. The [CuIIOH]+ species in Cu-MOR is already present in the autoreduced material, 
in absence of the [CuOCu]2+ species that require activation with O2 or N2O. Without this 
spectral overlap, its kinetics were obtained by monitoring its 20 700 cm−1 charge transfer band 
with DR UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy (Figure 24 C). The first order rate constants 
were extracted and Eyring plots were constructed.166 [CuIIOH]+ has a 9.5 kcal/mol higher 
enthalpic barrier than [CuOCu]2+ in Cu-CHA (Table 5). From experimentally validated DFT 
models, the electron delocalization via the bridging O over the two Cu atoms in [CuOCu]2+ 
stabilizes the hydrogen atom abstraction (HAA) first product ([CuIOHCuII]2+) by 17 kcal/mol 
compared to the localized structure (Figure 24 E). Therefore, it is proposed that the higher 
reactivity of binuclear [CuOCu]2+ sites compared to mononuclear [CuOH]+ sites is a direct 
result of stabilization of the [CuIOHCuII]2+ product by electron delocalization over the two Cu 
cations via the bridging ligand, indicating the importance of copper nuclearity on reactivity. 
It should be noted that Cu speciations may be significantly impacted by the activation protocol, 
and this limits the consensus in the literature. In the above discussion, when the Cu-CHA is 
first autoreduced in He at high temperature and subsequently activated with O2 or N2O at lower 
temperature, mainly [CuOCu]2+ active sites form. As shown in Figure 25, a broad LMCT band 
between 22 200 and 35 000 cm−1 disappears upon exposure of the O2 or N2O activated Cu-
CHA to methane at 473 K. This suggests that the absorption features of active copper species 
fall in this range. Based on their characteristic Raman vibrations, Ipek et al. propose that trans-

42 
 
 
μ-1,2-peroxo dicopper(II) and [CuOCu]2+ form on Cu-CHA following O2 or He treatment at 
723 K, although this assignment has been criticised.165, 190 O2 treatment then results in a higher 
concentration of this species. 
 
Figure 25. UV-VIS absorption spectra of Cu-CHA after O2 treatment at 723 K (black) followed by 2 h CH4 
treatment at 473 K (red). The difference spectrum is shown in green. Adapted from ref. 190 Copyright 2017 
American Chemical Society. 
3.1.2.3 Selective catalytic reduction of NO by NH3 with Cu zeolites 
Selective catalytic reduction (SCR) of NOX by NH3 (NH3–SCR) to N2 and water is currently 
the leading deNOX technology for diesel engine emissions control.191 eq 15 is a standard low 
temperature (<250 °C) NH3-SCR reaction of NO. 
 
4NO + 4NH3 + O2 → 4N2 + 6H2O  
(15) 
Among various catalysts studied, Cu-CHA has been widely investigated and commercialized 
owing to its remarkable catalytic reactivity, low selectivity toward undesirable N2O production, 
and excellent hydrothermal stability.157, 158 Great efforts have been devoted to reveal the 
catalytic chemistry underlying the conspicuous performance of Cu-CHA in standard low 
temperature NH3-SCR reaction. In situ UV-VIS-NIR studies have contributed greatly to 
understanding the complex mechanism, particularly in combination with EPR, XAS and 
DRIFTS.158, 190, 192-197 Figure 26 illustrates a representative reaction scheme. It has been well 
established that isolated Cu2+ and [CuIIOH]+ species coordinated to the zeolite lattice (Figure 
27 B) act as the main active centers in Cu-CHA.192-194 The Cu2+ species are associated with 
exchange sites with two Al substitutions in the lattice, separated by one or two Si tetrahedral 
sites in the same six-membered ring, while the [CuIIOH]+ motif is ligated by one Al center and 
prefers to anchor near 8MR windows.198, 199 Fundamental SCR mechanistic studies 
demonstrate that NH3-SCR over Cu-zeolites proceeds via a redox cycle of Cu2+ and Cu+. NO 
and NH3 reduce Cu2+ to Cu+ and O2 reoxidizes Cu+ to Cu2+.200-202 The active Cu2+ and [CuIIOH]+ 
sites exhibit a strong affinity toward NH3, leading to the generation of [Cu(OH)(NH3)3]+ and 
[Cu(NH3)4]2+ complexes detached from the zeolite framework.203 Subsequently, NO interacts 
with these mobile, NH3-ligated Cu2+ complexes, leading to NO decomposition and the 
generation of [Cu(NH3)2]+ complexes (note the reduction of Cu2+ to Cu+ in this step). The small 

43 
 
 
size and low, delocalized charge of Cu+ ammine allow its migration through the zeolite pore 
structure. 
Two 
[Cu(NH3)2]+ 
species 
combine 
with 
dioxygen 
to 
form 
a [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ intermediate, which then regenerates two isolated Cu2+ species.192, 204 This 
mechanism is reminiscent of O2-assisted oxidation of Cu+ sites via multinuclear intermediates 
in bioinorganic chemistry.205-207 
 
Figure 26. A proposed reaction scheme and intermediates of standard low temperature NH3-SCR with Cu-
zeolites, including suggested UV-VIS-NIR absorption bands, based on the spectroscopic studies in ref. 196.  
As Cu+ has a fully occupied d shell (3d10), d-d transitions are only observable for Cu2+ (3d9). 
Therefore, monitoring the d-d transitions during the NH3-SCR reaction makes it possible to 
track the consumption and regeneration of Cu2+ species. Moreover, the d-d and CT transition 
energies are highly sensitive to coordination environment and therefore excellent features to 
probe the copper bound intermediates throughout the reaction scheme. After the high 
temperature oxidation treatment, the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum of Cu-CHA shows 
three absorption features at ~500, ~610, and ~760 nm (Figure 27 A), which are attributed to d-
d transitions of zeolite-coordinated Cu2+ species.208 Through combined DRIFTS data, it was 
derived that the dominant Cu2+ species in the O2-activated Cu-CHA sample was the isolated 

44 
 
 
[CuIIOH]+, and all three bands are generally attributed to [CuIIOH].198, 209 However, speciation 
of Cu species in Cu-CHA catalysts is significantly influenced by Si/Al ratio and Cu loading,210 
and the band assignments cannot be generalized to all Cu-CHA samples. The isolated Cu2+ and 
[CuIIOH]+ contents in Cu-CHA can be controlled by adjusting the Si/Al and Cu/Al ratios 
(Figure 27 B).193, 198, 199 
Upon exposure to NH3, a noticeable blueshift of the d-d transition absorption peak to 640 nm 
is observed, and the d-d features of the [CuIIOH]+ disappear, corresponding to the exchange of 
O-ligands for N-ligand. Subsequent reaction of the Cu2+-[NH3]x complexes with NO decreases 
the intensity of the 640 nm band, and simultaneous N2 release is observed.208 For the 
investigation of the oxidation half cycle, various oxidizing gas mixtures were fed to Cu-CHA 
after reduction by NO + NH3, with simultaneous UV-VIS-NIR absorption measurements. The 
oxidation treatments increases the intensity of the d-d absorption band, indicating the oxidation 
of Cu+ to Cu2+. Interestingly, the intensity of the d-d band is slightly higher under oxidation by 
NO/O2 and NO/NO2/O2 than by O2, suggesting that O2 acts as the primary oxidant in the 
oxidation half cycle, and that the presence of NOX promotes this step. The right panel of Figure 
27 A shows the kinetic curves for N2 formation and the intensity of the 760 nm band during 
NH3-SCR - reduction - NH3-SCR transient reactions, in which the gas feed was changed from 
NH3+NO+O2 to NH3+NO, and then reverted to NH3+NO+O2.208 
While the in situ UV-VIS-NIR spectra enable the tracking of the copper oxidation state and its 
dependence on reaction conditions, the assignments of the specific absorption bands to well 
defined intermediates has been more involved. From EPR, XAS, DRIFTS and DR-UV-VIS-
NIR spectroscopies, across different in situ studies, the Cu2+ → Cu+ half cycle with Cu-CHA 
is generally agreed upon (eq 16).209-211 
 
[CuII(OH)(NH3)3]+ + NO → [CuI(NH3)2]+ + N2 + 2H2O  
(16) 
Of particular importance was the XAS study by Paolucci et al. showing the mobilization of 
Cu+ through its solvation by NH3, allowing it to migrate through the zeolite and cyle between 
multinuclear and mononuclear intermediates.192 This mobilization, in combination with 
dependency on reaction conditions, gives access to multiple options for the rest of the reaction 
cycle, which is still debated. The DR-UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra are generally convoluted 
and not fully understood, but some features, besides the d-d transitions of [CuIIOH]+, have been 
(tentatively) assigned on Cu-CHA (see also Figure 27). 
Based on their behaviour on samples of different Si/Al and Cu/Al, Zhang et al. 209 assigned two 
LMCT bands at 215 nm and 240 nm respectively to isolated [CuIIOH]+ and Cu2+ sites. An 
additional 355 nm band is also present in their spectra for a Si/Al = 30 Cu-CHA. this is 
commonly assigned to binuclear Cu-oxo species.190, 195 With increasing temperature, the 
intensity of the 215 nm and 355 nm bands in the Si/Al = 30 sample respectively decrease and 
increase. Therefore, they proposed that upon O2 activation, two [CuIIOH]+ sites are bridged 
into a binuclear Cu-oxo intermediate, tentatively described as a [Cu2O2]2+ site. Upon exposure 
to NH3, mobile [Cu(NH3)4]2+ and [Cu(OH)(NH3)3]+ complexes are proposed. UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption features at ~240 & 680 nm and at 205 & 660 nm are respectively linked to these 
two complexes.209 Oda et al. reported a 370 nm absorption band on Cu-AEI zeolite that is only 
observed under steady-state NH3-SCR conditions, and therefore related to an unstable 
intermediate, which they also suggest for Cu-CHA.212 The O2 dependence of this band is shown 
in Figure 27 C, when the O2 concentration is gradually reduced from 10 to 0%, the intensity of 
the 370 nm band decreases until it finally disappears. When the O2 supply is restored the 370 
nm band reappears. The intermediate was trapped and isolated by reducing the reaction 

45 
 
 
temperature. The 370 nm band develops significantly below 473 K and reaches a maximum at 
323 K. It was further identified by in situ UV-VIS DRS studies coupled with XAS and 
measurements of reactivity towards NO, NH3 and a mixture of NO and NH3 at low reaction 
temperatures.196 Through spectroscopic similarity to antiferromagnetically coupled S = 0 μ-
η2:η2 peroxo-bridged dicopper(II) present in Cu proteins and other relevant model 
complexes,205, 213, 214 the intermediate species was finally  assigned as the μ-η2:η2 peroxo-
bridged diamino dicopper(II) ([Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+). Additionally, a d-d transition at 14 000 cm−1 
(~710 nm) and LMCT bands between 27 000 and 31 000 cm−1 (320-370 nm) were assigned to 
this species. Very recently Lei et al.197 observed a transient absorption band at 225 nm on Cu-
CHA in NH3 + NO atmosphere below 250 °C. Combining DRIFT and TD-DFT, and observing 
its correlation to an absorption band at 350 nm, they assigned this also to an LMCT of the 
confined μ-η2:η2 peroxo-bridged diamino dicopper(II). 
 
Figure 27. (A) (left) In situ UV-VIS absorption spectra of Cu-CHA during reduction half cycle. The catalyst was 
first oxidized under O2, followed by exposure to NH3 and then to NO. Time dependence of d-d transition band 
intensity of Cu2+ species and MS intensity of N2 during (middle) reduction half cycle and during (right) NH3-SCR 
- reduction - NH3-SCR transient reaction. (B) Cu-speciation in the Cu-CHA catalyst as a function of Si/Al and 
Cu/Al. (C) In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of Cu-AEI under (left) different gas mixtures at 473 K and 
(right) NH3-SCR at 473 K with different O2 concentration. Adapted from refs. 196, 208, 209, 212. Copyright 2020 Wiley, 
2022 American Chemical Society, and 2020 American Chemical Society. 
The assignment of UV-VIS-NIR absorption bands to specific complexes on Cu-CHA in NH3-
SCR conditions is severely obstructed by the multitude of intermediates present and the sample 
dependence of the copper speciation. Nevertheless, the absorption bands mentioned above have 
been used to derive mechanistic insight and to identify the active sites. Zhang et al.209 
monitored the CT bands of isolated Cu2+ and [CuIIOH]+ in the NH3-SCR reaction over Cu-
CHA at different temperatures. While the ~210 nm band of [CuIIOH]+ starts to diminish at low 
temperature, the 240 nm band of isolated Cu2+ decays at higher temperature, indicating 
[CuIIOH]+ sites are more active. The 355 nm band of binuclear Cu-oxo species is present below 
∼180 °C for Cu-CHA with Si/Al = 30, and below ~200 °C for Si/Al = 6. It then decreases with 
temperature, showing the formation of reduced dimers. Negri et al.196 tracked the 370 nm 
(27000 cm−1) LMCT band of [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ through pretreatment in O2, subsequent 
reduction in NH3+NO, and reoxidation with O2 at the SCR reaction temperature. Upon 
exposure to NH3, the d-d transition and LMCT band intensities decrease, although they do not 

46 
 
 
disappear completely (Figure 28), indicating NH3 reacts with [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ and reduces a 
fraction of the Cu2+ ions to Cu+. When [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ is exposed to NO, the corresponding 
absorption bands decrease faster with simultaneous formation of N2, indicating that 
[Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ is more reactive toward NO than NH3, and low-temperature NH3-SCR 
reaction likely involves the reaction of [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ with NO. From XANES data, the 
copper site is proposed to be reduced to a [CuI(NH3)]+ in this step.196 The role of O2 in the SCR 
mechanism, and the mechanism for its activation were further investigated by Lei et al.,197 
using the 225 nm absorption band of the peroxo bridged dicopper species. They observed 
increased N2 evolution in the cofeeding of O2 which they correlated to the intensity of the 
transient 225 nm feature. They thus propose the [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ as a key intermediate in the 
low temperature NH3-SCR of NO with Cu-CHA. 
 
Figure 28. UV-VIS-NIR DR absorption spectra of Cu-CHA exposure to (left) NH3/He and (right) NO/He at 
200 °C after forming the [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ complexes. Top and bottom panels on the right are initial and 
subsequent evolution of DR UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra respectively. Blue thick line: after NH3/He exposure, 
red thick line: spectrum collected after forming the [Cu2O2(NH3)4]2+ complexes, grey thin lines: intermediates, 
light blue thick line: after NO/He exposure, orange thick line: the final spectrum in the panel. Adapted from ref. 
196. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
3.1.2.4 Selective catalytic reduction of NO by NH3 with Fe zeolites 
Cu-zeolites are effective NH3-SCR catalysts at low operating temperatures (< 400 °C), but they 
are less performant at higher temperatures where copper aggregates into inactive clusters, and 
where N2O is formed as a by-product. Fe-zeolites on the other hand are better suited for high 
temperature NH3-SCR as they demonstrate improved stability and high-temperature SCR 
selectivity compared to their Cu-exchanged counterparts.215-220 While in Cu zeolite SCR small 
pore zeolites like CHA are preferred because they reduce the copper clustering, with Fe zeolite 
SCR, large pore zeolites (e.g. MOR and *BEA) are preferred. During the NH3-SCR reaction, 
the Fe active sites cycle between Fe2+ and Fe3+,220, 221 and several active sites have been 
proposed. These include Fe oxide particles, binuclear Fe3+ species and isolated Fe cations.157, 
222, 223 UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of Fe-zeolites are generally analyzed in three regions: 
< 300 nm, 300-400 nm and > 400 nm, which are roughly correlated to isolated Fe3+ species, 
aggregated Fe3+ species and Fe2O3 nanoparticles respectively.224, 225 In the case of the isolated 
Fe3+ species, only the CT bands of O ligands to Fe3+ are observable in the UV-VIS-NIR spectra 
since the d-d transitions are symmetry and spin forbidden. Typically two CT bands, which arise 
from the t1 → t2 and t1 → e transitions, are observed for each Fe3+ species and their exact 
energies depend on the number of ligands.226 For four-coordinated isolated Fe3+ the two 
absorption bands are both below 250 nm while for octahedrally coordinated Fe3+ the two bands 

47 
 
 
are at 187-234 and 244-305 nm, respectively. With these characteristic handles to follow, in 
situ UV-VIS spectroscopy has been employed to investigate the NH3-SCR process catalyzed 
by Fe-zeolites. 
Yasumura et al. conducted an in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption study on Fe-MOR to investigate 
the redox cycle of NH3-SCR.227 A charge transfer band centered at 265 nm and a shoulder 
above 300 nm indicate the presence of octahedrally coordinated Fe3+ species and oligomeric 
Fe3+ species as the main Fe species (Figure 29 A).227, 228 The 265 nm absorption band decreases 
upon exposure to NO + NH3 with simultaneous N2 formation and subsequently recovers in 
O2 or NO + O2 flow, indicating the NH3-SCR over Fe-MOR proceeds through a redox cycle. 
In addition, the recovery of the Fe3+ species is faster under NO + O2 flow than under the 
O2 flow, suggesting that NO promotes the reoxidation of Fe2+.  
Structural changes of Fe species in Fe-*BEA during NH3-SCR were also probed by in situ UV-
VIS-NIR spectroscopy.223 The UV-VIS-NIR spectrum of the untreated Fe-*BEA sample is 
characterized by two bands at ~220 and 275 nm (Figure 29 B), indicating ion-exchanged Fe 
species are mainly present as octahedral coordinated Fe3+ species. During heating from 423 K 
to 723 in O2, absorption increases below 250 nm and between 300-400 nm, indicating the 
formation of Fe3+ species in tetrahedral coordination and Fe-O-Fe species. The transformation 
of Fe species upon heating in O2 results from the removal of hydroxyl and water ligands on 
extra-framework Fe3+ cations. Under NH3-SCR conditions, there is only a small change in the 
overall intensity of the spectra, indicating these in situ formed Fe species during O2 oxidation 
are still present under NH3-SCR conditions. The deconvolution of the UV-VIS-NIR spectra 
further indicates that part of octahedrally coordinated Fe3+ species is restored at the expense of 
bridging Fe-O-Fe species (320 nm) at 423 K. Heating to 523 K, however, reverses this, as 
confirmed by in situ EXAFS analysis. These results suggest that the octahedral coordinated 
Fe3+ species are key intermediates for the reversible changes of Fe species during the NH3-
SCR reaction. In addition, the low catalytic activity at 423 K is attributed to the significant 
dissociation of binuclear Fe clusters into isolated octahedral coordinated Fe3+ species. 

48 
 
 
 
Figure 29. (A) (left) UV-VIS absorption spectra of Fe-MOR during exposure to NO + NH3 at 300 °C. (middle) 
Changes in the UV–VIS absorption intensity at 265 nm in NO + NH3 and the MS signal of N2. (right) Time course 
of the UV-VIS absorption intensity at 265 nm under O2 or NO + O2. (B) UV-VIS absorption spectra of Fe-*BEA 
during (left) heating in O2 and NH3–SCR reaction at different temperatures. (right) Deconvolution results of UV-
