3.1.2.5 N2O decomposition with Fe zeolites 
Fe-zeolites of several topologies can stabilize a S=2 square planar Fe2+ site, commonly named 
α-FeII, with an interesting capability to abstract the oxygen atom from N2O forming a surface 
oxygen and releasing N2.17, 168 At higher temperatures the surface oxygen recombines and is 
released as O2 making the N2O decomposition catalytic. While the site offers a good hypothesis 
for the lower N2O release in NH3-SCR with Fe-zeolites compared to Cu-zeolites, its role in 
SCR has never been specifically investigated despite the availability of very clear spectroscopic 
probes. 
The α-FeII site can be prepared in almost pure form enabling full attribution of spectroscopic 
features. Key absorption band assignments are summarized in Figure 30. The experimental 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption features of α-FeII were fully explained with the Fe2+ electronic 
structure by Hallaert et al.229 α-FeII  in Fe-*BEA exhibits a particularly high energy ligand field 
band at 15 900 cm−1, distinctive for the square planar ligand field of the high spin FeII. For α-
FeII  this ligand field band is particularly intense (see also Figure 1). Similar, but weaker 
absorption bands are observed in Fe-ZSM-5, Fe-FER and Fe-CHA. The surface oxygen formed 
upon oxygen atom abstraction from N2O remains bound to the α-FeII , yielding a high spin 
square pyramidal [Fe=O]2+, commonly named α-O. Also α-O can be produced in high purity, 
and there is a clear fingerprint experimental UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum. On Fe-*BEA 
the α-O spectrum includes an intense 16 900 cm−1 CT absorption band with a high energy 

49 
 
 
shoulder (also CT) at ∼20 000 cm−1 and broad absorption at ~6000 cm−1 from a d-d transition 
with vibronic coupling. Contrary to α-FeII the exact matching of electronic excitations to their 
corresponding experimental absorption bands in inconclusive for α-O.230 However, the lower 
energy d-d transition band shows vibronic coupling when observed in MCD, with a frequency 
of ~1000 cm−1.17 It can thus tentatively be linked to the dπ-dz² ligand field transition. The Fe=O 
stretch can also be observed with NRVS for α-O on Fe-*BEA at 885 cm−1.169 
 
Figure 30. Reaction schemes and intermediates of N2O decomposition and C-H activation with Fe zeolites, 
including the known UV-VIS-NIR absorption bands.  
In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy coupled to Mössbauer and Fourier-transform 
infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectroscopy were used to identify the N2O ligation onto α-FeII 
that precedes oxygen atom abstraction.170 Around room temperature, N2O binds reversibly to 
the α-FeII end-on via the N atom to form a square pyramidal complex (α-N2O) with ligand field 
absorption bands at 5 000 and 11 500 cm−1. At low temperatures, a second N2O binds at the 
same side of the FeII site, as observed in low temperature Mössbauer and FTIR. When heated, 
the oxygen atom is abstracted to form α-O and N2. The thermodynamics of N2O adsorption 
were measured by tracking the 11 500 cm−1/15 900 cm−1 equilibrium on Fe-*BEA in different 
partial pressure of N2O at various temperatures (Figure 31 A). The different conditions shifted 
the equilibrium between both ligand field bands with a clear isosbestic point at 13 500 cm−1 
indicating a single reversible transformation. The adsorption was evaluated using a Langmuir 
adsorption model and the equilibrium constants were determined for the different temperatures 
(Figure 31 C). With the Van ’t Hoff equation the enthalpy of adsorption of ∆Ho = −4.8 ± 0.1 
kcal/mol and an entropy of adsorption of ∆So = −7.1 ± 0.4 cal/mol·K were derived.  
The oxygen atom abstraction was tracked by the decay of the 11 500 cm−1 absorption band of 
α-N2O and the simultaneous growth of the 16 900 cm−1 and 28 000 cm−1 bands of α-O (Figure 
31 B). An isosbestic point was observed at 14 000 cm−1 indicating a single transformation, 
selectively converting the adsorbed N2O into α-O. From a first order reaction model and an 

50 
 
 
Eyring plot (Figure 31 D), the enthalpy and entropy of the TS were determined to be ∆H⧧ = 
17.7 ± 1.4 kcal/mol and ∆S⧧= −22.4 ± 3.8 cal/mol·K. 
 
Figure 31. UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy of α-N2O and its thermodynamics. (A) (left) In situ UV-VIS-
NIR absorption difference spectra of Fe-*BEA at 308 K after high temperature He treatment with p(N2O) ranging 
from 0 to 0.05 atmosphere. (C) Van ‘t Hoff plot of data derived from the adsorption data from in situ UV-VIS-
NIR absorption spectra analogous to those shown in the left. (B) (left) In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption difference 
spectra of Fe-*BEA at 373 K following the transformation of α-N2O to α-O over a time interval of 15 minutes in 
an atmosphere of 35 vol.% N2O in He. (D) Eyring plot of data derived from in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectra analogous to those shown in the left. Adapted from ref. 170. Copyright 2021 Springer Nature. 
The spectroscopic and thermodynamic data were used to build an experimentally validated 
reaction mechanism that was evaluated with molecular modelling. This revealed the role of the 
coordination geometry of α-FeII. A distortion of the square planar geometry where the Fe atom 
is pulled out of the plane of oxygen atoms strengthens the η1-O binding of N2O and lowers the 
apparent activation barrier for oxygen atom abstraction. Interestingly, this structural difference 
exists between the α-FeII sites in Fe-*BEA and Fe-CHA. The comparison of their N2O binding 
and activation thermodynamics has, however, not yet been made. Note that the two α-FeII sites 
also differ in other aspects (second coordination sphere, pore system, ligand field strength) so 
that the comparison would still not be a perfect experimental representation of the molecular 
modelling findings. 
3.1.2.6 C-H bond activation with α-O on Fe-zeolites 
The α-O site is highly reactive for partial oxidations of methane, other alkanes and benzene. 
The products of these reactions have been investigated with coupled in situ spectroscopies 
including UV-VIS-NIR absorption, rR, MCD, EPR, NRVS and XAS.162, 231 From the benzene 
reaction on Fe-ZSM-5, a deactivating FeIII-phenolate was identified with a spectroscopic 
handle at 13 900 cm−1, which is composed of two overlapping bands at 11 600 and 13 300 cm−1. 
The pathway to the FeIII phenolate competes with the catalytic pathway to phenol that leads to 
the regeneration of the α-FeII precursor to α-O. On Fe-*BEA the analogous absorption feature 

51 
 
 
lies at 15 200 cm−1. A 10 500 cm−1 band was identified as the adsorption complex of benzene 
on α-FeII. Similarly, in the reaction of α-O with methane, a deactivating pathway to FeIII-OH 
and FeIII-OCH3 was identified through their UV-VIS-NIR absorption handles around 22 000 
cm−1. Here the pathway competes with the catalytic route to methanol and α-FeII. For both 
reactions, the spectroscopic handles are known, the intermediates are identified, and interesting 
conclusions for better catalyst design have been derived, but in situ UV-VIS-NIR tracking of 
the kinetics and thermodynamics remains to be achieved. 
3.1.2.7 Non-zeolitic single atom catalysts 
Zeolite supported TMI catalysts are especially interesting to study with UV-VIS-NIR 
absorption because well-defined TMI sites alleviate the disadvantageous low spectral 
resolution of the technique. Increasingly, other methods to prepare uniformly dispersed TMI 
species are developed. Notably ‘single atom catalysts’ (SAC) have emerged as a hot topic in 
catalysis in recent years, referring to heterogeneous catalysis with single isolated atoms 
decorated on a support. These materials are used for thermal heterogeneous catalysis as well 
as electro- and photocatalysis.232-234 While UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy would 
clearly be a powerful tool in their in situ study, there are only few examples to be found in 
literature so far and the technique is not commonly listed as an important characterization 
technique.233 A common limitation is the strong absorption of the support material hampering 
the detection of weaker TMI single atom absorption bands. The scarce examples that can be 
found often involve band gap energies measured using the Tauc plot method, but rarely involve 
the identification of ligand field environment and electronic structure as with zeolites.235, 236 
An exception is the detection of a unique redshift of the cinnamyl alcohol π-π* absorption band 
upon contact with atomically dispersed Pd on Al2O3.237 Other examples are the appearance of 
a clearly resolved charge transfer band of atomically dispersed Ru on defective TiO2 
nanosheets,236 and the description of a copper species on amorphous silica with an absorption 
band at 22700 cm−1 active in methane oxidation.238 
3.1.3 Tracking chromophoric products 
Besides the absorption of the catalyst itself, also chromophoric reagents and products may be 
tracked spectroscopically. In general these enable simple and cheap analytical methods to track 
reaction mechanisms and kinetics, and they are explained in standard analytical chemistry 
textbooks.40 
3.1.3.1 zeolite catalyzed methanol to hydrocarbons  
Industrial methanol conversions to hydrocarbons (MTH) are typically catalysed by acid 
zeolites (HZSM-5, HSSZ-13) or zeotype catalysts (HSAPO-34). The selectivity toward 
different hydrocarbons is directed by choosing the suitable catalyst and appropriate reaction 
conditions.239 The resulting products are either rich in gasoline, olefins, or aromatics, and the 
corresponding processes are then referred to as methanol-to-gasoline (MTG), methanol-to-
olefins (MTO), or methanol-to-aromatics (MTA). Since the MTH process was discovered and 
patented by the Mobil Research Laboratory in the late 1970s,240, 241 mechanistic understanding 
of MTH has steadily grown,242-245 which facilitates better catalyst design and preparation,246, 
247 as well as process development.248-250 
Although the formation mechanism of the first C-C bond remains under debate,251-254 the steady 
state formation of hydrocarbons is believed to proceed indirectly through a hydrocarbon pool 
(HCP), in which the active species include polyalkylated aromatics, carbocations and 
olefins.255 The HCP is then methylated by methanol and dealkylated to yield the desired olefins. 
However, the exact nature of the HCP and detailed reaction mechanism are still the subject of 

52 
 
 
debate.239, 256, 257 In addition, the HCP can further develop into species that are inactive for 
MTH, such as heavy aromatic coke, that ultimately lead to catalyst deactivation. Inside the 
zeolite, the size of hydrocarbon species is constrained by the nanospace defined by the zeolite 
framework giving rise to the ‘shape selectivity’ of zeolites. However, the hydrocarbon species 
can grow into bulky polyaromatic molecules on the outer surface of the catalyst particles, which 
tend to block the pores of the catalyst. 
In situ and operando UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is a vital tool to follow the 
formation of the HCP species inside zeolite catalysts during methanol conversion. The 
technique can differentiate between carbocationic HCP species and their neutral counterparts, 
which have distinct bands in a UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum resulting from the shifted π-
π* and n- π* relative energies.258 In addition, UV-VIS-NIR DRS can help track the 
accumulation of deactivation species (cokes) in and on the zeolite crystals.259 Coking generally 
involves a complex mixture of organic molecules, mainly polyaromatic molecules and cations 
with absorption bands in the UV-VIS range. As coke builds up in the catalyst particles, the 
catalyst darkens in color corresponding to an increase of broad unresolved ranges in the 
absorption spectrum. Through deconvolution the absorption spectra into Gaussian bands, 
meaningful information on cokes composition can be extracted.259 Assignments of UV-VIS 
DR adsorption bands of hydrocarbon species formed in the zeolite catalyzed MTH reaction are 
summarized in Table 6. However, the observed absorption bands are usually weak and broad, 
and UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy alone is not always sufficiently sensitive to 
understand 
the 
chemical 
composition 
of 
the 
cokes. 
This 
is 
exemplified 
by 
hexamethylbenzenium (HMB+) and methylnaphthyl cation, which are both potential 
intermediates in the MTH process and exhibit a similar absorption band ~390 nm.260 The 
HMB+ serves as an active HCP species, whereas methylnaphthyl cations are deactivation coke. 
To achieve a more accurate interpretation of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra, solid-state NMR 
and FTIR are generally used to supplement the analysis.258

UV-VIS spectroscopy has been extensively applied to investigate the impacts of zeolite 
topology, acidity, and reaction conditions on the formation of hydrocarbon species, which 
closely correlate to the reaction and deactivation routes in the MTH process. The nature of 
hydrocarbon species during the MTH process over the CHA zeolites (HSSZ-13 and HSAPO-
34) was investigated at different temperatures by in situ UV-VIS-NIR DRS with the aid of 
NNMF analysis.259, 263, 267, 268 The NNMF analysis allows for the division of the time-resolved 

53 
 
 
UV-VIS-NIR spectra into two groups of spectra with different kinetic behaviours, which 
separates the manifold of formed hydrocarbon species into two main groups. The first group 
of species increased with the conversion, peaked at full conversion, and subsequently declined 
with time on stream. Therefore, these species are strongly associated with catalyst activity. The 
formation of the second group commenced at the maximum formation of the first group of 
hydrocarbon species and continued to increase until the catalyst completely deactivated. Thus, 
these species are related to catalyst deactivation. The nature of the species appears to be similar 
for HSSZ-13 and HSAPO-34 (Figure 32 A-D). At low temperature (573 K), the first group of 
species of both zeolites contain absorption bands at 33 650 cm−1 (300 nm) and 26 000 cm−1 
(385 nm), which can be attributed to monoenylic and highly methylated benzene carbocations, 
respectively. The second group of species on CHA zeolites are characterized by absorption 
bands at 30 000 cm−1 (330 nm) and 24 000 cm−1 (415 nm), corresponding respectively to low 
methylated benzene and methylated naphthalene carbocations. Interestingly, the reaction 
temperature significantly influences the chemistry of the hydrocarbon species. At higher 
reaction temperatures (> 673 K), low methylated benzene carbocations (31 000 cm−1, 320 nm) 
and methylated naphthalene carbocations (24 000 cm−1, 415 nm), identified as the deactivation 
species during low temperature MTH process, become the predominant HCP active species, 
while the formation of highly conjugated neutral polyaromatics deposited on the external 
surface of the catalysts leads to a featureless spectrum of the second group. It was proposed 
that at low reaction temperature deactivation occurs because of pore filling with methylated 
naphthalene carbocations while at higher reaction temperature highly conjugated 
polyaromatics on the external surface block the pores. Besides, it was noted that the active 
hydrocarbon species in HSAPO-34 were more stable than in HSSZ-13. This is likely due to the 
stronger acidity of HSSZ-13, which promotes the formation of more conjugated and bulky 
hydrocarbon species.259 

54

by NNMF analysis. (right in each panel) The evolution of each group as a function of methanol loading on the 
catalyst material of HSSZ-13 catalyzed methanol conversion at (A) 573 K and (B) 723 K and HSAPO-34 
catalyzed methanol conversion at (C) 573 K and (D) 673 K. (red: active hydrocarbon species, black: deactivating 
hydrocarbon species). Reproduced from refs. 259, 267. Copyright 2014 Wiley and copyright 2015 American 
Chemical Society. 

55 
 
 
In situ UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy was applied to investigate how different small pore zeolites 
catalyse the MTO process. 263, 268 Figure 33 shows the formation and evolution of retained 
hydrocarbon species in small pore zeolites CHA, DDR, and LEV topologies tracked by in situ 
UV-VIS spectroscopy during MTO. Concurrently, in situ X-ray diffraction was used to track 
the zeolite lattice expansion. Although the pore structures of the three zeolite material are 
similar, as they consist of large zeolite cages interconnected by eight-membered-ring windows, 
it was demonstrated that small differences in size and shape of the cages have a substantial 
impact on retained hydrocarbon species that lead to the lattice expansion. The interaction 
between the retained hydrocarbon species and the structures of the zeolite framework results 
in distinct degrees and orientations of lattice expansion for each zeolite frameworks. In CHA 
zeolite, where the largest expansion was observed, methylated naphthalene and pyrene are the 
predominant species. For DDR zeolite, 1-methylnaphthalene and phenalene are the primary 
species formed. In LEV zeolite, methylated benzene and naphthalene are the main observed 
species. To study the kinetics of the involved hydrocarbon species during MTO process, the 
time evolution of the in situ UV-VIS spectra during the conversion of methanol over CHA, 
DDR, and LEV zeolites were analyzed using Multivariate Curve Resolution-Alternating Least 
Squares (MCR-ALS). With MCR-ALS the series of UV-VIS spectra could be deconvoluted 
into components with similar kinetic behaviours. For DDR, three components were extracted 
(Figure 33 B), corresponding to the dominant species during the three stages of MTO process: 
the induction period, the active period, and the deactivation period. These allow tracking of the 
hydrocarbon species. At a lower reaction temperature (623 K), a distinct feature around 25 000 
cm−1 (400 nm) attributed to 1-methylnaphthalene is observed during the deactivation period. 
However, at a higher reaction temperature (723 K), this feature appears during the active period. 
These findings again indicate a temperature-dependent change in the role of the hydrocarbon 
species, thereby influencing the reaction and deactivation mechanism. 
 

56 
 
 
 
Figure 33. (A) In situ UV-VIS spectra during methanol conversion at 673 K and hydrocarbon species 
corresponding to the UV-VIS absorbance bands over the (left)CHA, (middle) DDR and (right) LEV catalysts. (B) 
(left) MCR-ALS components and (right) their respective contributions to the overall in situ UV-VIS spectra 
versus time for the conversion of methanol over DDR zeolite at a reaction temperature of (top) 623 K and (bottom) 
723 K. The colors of the contribution plots correspond to the colors of the components. Reproduced from refs. 263, 
269. Copyright 2017 and 2018 American Chemical Society. 
As cokes accumulate in the zeolites, the intensity of the characteristic absorption by 
polyaromatics above 600 nm gradually increases, making UV-VIS DRS a useful tool to 
investigate the coke evolution during MTH process. By placing UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
probes at different positions along the reactor bed, the evolution of active species and coke in 
the MTO reaction along the catalyst bed can be monitored. Such spatiotemporal approach can 
track the coke front as it progresses towards the reactor end. Photos of the catalyst bed and the 
corresponding in situ UV-VIS spectra for the first 2 h of MTO reaction over HZSM-5 and 
MgZSM-5 are shown in Figure 34 A.268 In the case of MTO process over HZSM-5, coke 
formed rapidly over the entire catalyst bed at the beginning of the reaction and a broad 
absorption band below 25 000 cm−1 (400 nm) appeared. With reaction time, it shifted to lower 
wavenumbers. The spectral region below 25 000 cm−1 is generally dominated by neutral and 
charged aromatic species. The redshift corresponds to the growth of larger aromatic 

57 
 
 
molecules.260, 270 In contrast, MgZSM-5 exhibited a much lighter color and weaker intensity of 
absorption bands below 25 000 cm−1 compared to HZSM-5, indicating much less formation of 
coke species. The UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of HZSM-5 after feeding a mixture of 
ethylene and propylene showed band positions and intensities that were very similar to the UV-
VIS-NIR absorption spectra during MTO reaction, while adsorption below 25 000 cm−1 was 
not observed during light olefins conversion over MgZSM-5. Coke formation from olefins 
aromatization, the so-called secondary coke formation, was therefore slower over MgZSM-5. 
Magnesium in the zeolite leads to a reduction of the strong Brønsted acid sites (BAS) and 
therefore prevents the formation of secondary coke. The less pronounced olefin aromatization 
on MgZSM-5 explains its higher selectivity toward light olefins and longer lifetime than H-
ZSM-5. The prevention of secondary reactions by incorporating alkaline-earth metals in the 
zeolite was also reported by Yarulina et al.271 Figure 34 B shows the in situ UV-VIS DR 
absorption spectra collected during methanol conversion over two HZSM-5 zeolites with 
different Si/Al ratios, and a Ca-modified ZSM-5 at 773 K. Aromatic species did not accumulate 
in Ca-modified ZSM-5 as evidenced by the absence of absorption below 25 000 cm−1. For the 
HZSM-5 zeolites, formation of polyaromatic species (naphthalene and homologues) was 
observed during the first seconds of MTO. As evidenced by 1 H-1 H double-quantum (DQ) 
and 1 H-1 H radio frequency driven recoupling (RFDR) magic angle spinning (MAS) NMR, 
alkaline-earth metals tend to deprotonate BAS, thus neutralizing inherent zeolite acidity and 
forming Lewis acid sites (LAS) and isolated BAS. UV-VIS DRS revealed that the presence of 
LAS in Ca-modified ZSM-5 prevented the formation of polyaromatic species during MTO 
process, thereby extending the lifetime of the catalyst. 

58 
 
 
 

59 
 
 
Figure 34. (A) Photo of the reactor bed after 2h MTO reaction and in situ UV-VIS absorption spectra of 
conversion of methanol or a mixture of propylene and ethylene at three positions along the reactor bed over (top) 
HZSM-5 and (bottom) MgZSM-5. (B) in situ UV-VIS absorption spectra collected during methanol conversion 
over (left) AE3, (middle) Z2 and (right) Z1 at 773 K. Reproduced from refs. 268, 271. Copyright 2018 Royal Society 
of Chemistry and copyright 2018 Springer Nature. 
Thuy et al. reported that coating ZSM-5 with a catalytically inactive siliceous exterior rim 
dramatically improves diffusion and reduces the accumulation of coke on external surfaces.272 
The Si-zoned ZSM-5 demonstrates an enhanced catalyst lifetime for the MTH process and 
diffusion properties that resemble particles with a much smaller size. Comparison of the UV-
VIS DR spectra for representative homogeneous and Si-zoned samples reveals a notable 
reduction in both internal and external coke relative to HCP species for the Si-zoned catalyst, 
suggesting the presence of a siliceous exterior suppresses the formation of cokes. As evidenced 
by computational simulation, both internal diffusion and surface permeation are enhanced in 
Si-rich regions of Si-zoned ZSM-5, thereby improving the diffusion properties of nanoporous 
catalysts. 
3.2 Homogeneous thermal catalysis 
In homogeneous catalysis, better resolved UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra are often obtained 
compared to heterogeneous catalysts. The intermediates are structurally more uniform and the 
systems are translucent, so that transmission mode measurements with better signal-to-noise 
can be used. Operando and in situ spectroscopy are commonly used in homogeneous catalysis 
to study the reaction mechanism and to understand the catalytic cycle at the molecular level.273 
Often the tracking of these transformations yields spectral evolutions with clear isosbestic 
points. Through the detection, identification and quantification of reaction intermediates and 
organic compounds, kinetic models are proposed, and the structure-performance relationships 
of the homogeneous catalyst are understood. Thus, the gained insights are valuable for a 
rational optimization of the system and for the design of novel catalysts.  
From a technical point of view, the operando and in situ spectroscopy reactor must meet the 
requirements of a homogeneous reaction, such as stirring, thermostable conditions, and 
controlled atmosphere. Therefore, different configurations can be used, such as reactors with 
submersible optometers, a fiber optic spectrometer in a special Schlenk vessel, or a stopped 
flow combined with a diode array to monitor reactions at small time intervals (see reference 274 
for technical details and 275 for analytical insights).  
Within the field of homogeneous catalysis, in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is 
mainly applied to the study of organometallic catalysis, as it allows the detection of metal 
associated electronic transitions, in addition to the formation of organic molecules. 276, 277 
Notably, the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectrum of transition metal complexes involves two 
types of transitions; d-d transitions at lower energy levels and with lower absorption 
coefficients, and CT transitions at higher energies and with higher absorption coefficients (see 
also section 2.1). The UV-VIS-NIR absorption pattern of organometallics is dependent on the 
metal center, the ligands, and the solvent. The number of combinations that can be made is 
therefore very large and the attribution of absorption bands to specific transitions is usually 
derived from literature databases and pure component spectra (‘fingerprint spectra’).275 For 
instance, the π-π* transition of a Schiff base Ni complex is observed between 339-350 nm, 
whereas for the Au complex, the transition is found in the range of 291-331 nm.278, 279 On the 
other hand, for a Ru Schiff base complex, the π-π* transition lies at 203-223 nm, and the n-π* 
and d-d transitions lie in the 355-406 nm and 616-630 nm range, respectively. Replacing the 

60 
 
 
ligand by tridentate pyridinediimine (pydim), shifts the transition bands to 278-318 nm, 425-
449 nm, and 536-566 nm.280 
Due to their sensitivity to UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopies, most of this section will handle 
organometallic complex catalysts, including examples of stopped flow absorption, C-term 
MCD and CD. Also examples on various types of reactions are given. The section ends with 
catalysis without organometallic complexes. 
3.2.1 Organometallic catalysts 
3.2.1.1 Selected applications of stopped flow absorption 
Stopped flow is used in homogeneous catalysis to observe short-lived intermediates and study 
fast reaction events that occur within milliseconds (see also section 2.2.2.2). It uses specifically 
timed rapid mixing of reagents and halting of the reaction. Through the monitoring of time-
dependent changes in spectroscopy and reactant concentrations, reaction rates and 
intermediates are determined. 
Grubbs Catalysts 
Grubbs catalysts are ruthenium-based complexes used in metathesis reactions. The reaction 
mechanism involves an initiation step followed by conversion and decomposition. During the 
initiation step, a by-product in the form of a styrene derivative is typically generated. This by-
product can undergo competitive metathetic reincorporation at the ruthenium center, leading 
to catalyst regeneration. Notably, third-generation Grubbs catalysts (G-III-X) (where X = H, 
Cl, Br) exhibit an exceptionally fast initiation step. The lower-limit of the dissociative initiation 
rate constant (k1) of G-III-Br has been estimated to be > 4 s−1. Through the use of a stopped-
flow system, the reaction between G-III-Br and ethyl vinyl ether (EVE) was successfully 
monitored and the initiation step was studied (Figure 35). G-III-Br undergoes aerobic oxidation 
in toluene and exposure to air could intermittently increase the first-order quenching rate of the 
348 nm feature during successive runs. Refinements were made to the stopped-flow system to 
ensure anaerobic conditions (Figure 35 A). The kinetics could thus be confidently attributed to 
metathesis initiation. A 354 nm centered absorption band, believed to originate from a metal 
to benzylidene-π* MLCT was tracked.281 
The band appears approximately 8 milliseconds after mixing a solution of G-III-Br + 3-Br-
pyridine (L) with solutions of ethyl vinyl ether in toluene at 5 °C (Figure 35 B). To determine 
the empirical first-order rate constants of the reaction, a non-linear regression analysis was 
applied to the exponential decay in absorbance. Subsequently, the empirical rate constants 
(kobs.) were calculated by averaging data from a minimum of 10 experiments for each 
concentration variation. The kinetics derived from these experiments support a mechanism 
wherein the dissociative and associative pathways converge at the pyridyl-free complex (alkene 
coordination complex), preparing the alkene to undergo metathesis with the benzylidene. The 
authors propose that both the associative and dissociative pathways play significant roles in the 
initiation process. 

61 
 
 
 
Figure 35. (A) Schematic of reactants mixing via stopped-flow (UV-VIS), G-III-Br: third-generation Grubbs 
catalyst, [L]: exogenous ligand such as 3-Br-pyridine, EVE: ethyl vinyl ether, Ru=CHOEt: initiation product; (B) 
UV-VIS spectra of the reaction of G-III-Br+[L] with EVE. Adapted from ref. 281. Copyright 2019 Royal Society 
of Chemistry. 
 
Stopped flow experiments also enable in-depth kinetic investigation of ligand substitution and 
catalytic oxidation of H2O to O2 on ruthenium complexes bearing the pentapyridyl ligand 
(6,6’’-(methoxy(pyridin-2-yl)methylene)di-2,2’-bipyridine: L-OMe).282 The complex trans-
[RuII(Cl)(L-OMe-κ-N5)]1+ , referred to as trans-1+, undergoes a substitution of the sixth 
coordination 
ligand 
in 
an 
aqueous 
solution 
containing 
trifluoromethanesulfonic 
acid/trifluoroethanol (used to enhance solubility), leading to the formation of the Ru aquo 
complex trans-22+. This substitution process is relatively slow, with a pseudo-first-order rate 
constant of k1=3.3*10−4 s−1 and a half-time of t1/2=35 minutes. In contrast, the analogous 
complex with the L-Me ligand shows faster reactivity under the same conditions, with a rate 
constant of k1’=1.5*10−3 s−1 and half time of t1/2=8 min. 
The authors then investigated the substitution reaction of trans-1+ upon one-electron oxidation 
in presence of ceriumIV ammonium nitrate, towards a trans-[RuIII(Cl)(L-OMe-κ-N5)]2+ 
complex denoted (trans-12+). This rapid outer-sphere oxidation reaction was monitored using 
stopped-flow UV-VIS spectroscopy and fitted to a second-order kinetic profile, with a rate 
constant of k2=8.0*104 M−1 s−1. Interestingly, the obtained spectra reveal a second, slower 
process occurring simultaneously with the formation of the oxidized complex (trans-12+), 
corresponding to the isomerization of (trans-12+) to (cis-12+), which remains in equilibrium. 
Tracking complex formation 
The synthesis of organometallic complexes is commonly studied using in situ UV-VIS 
absorption spectroscopy. An example is the formation of μ-chloro binuclear diphosphine (PP) 
rhodium complexes [Rh(PP)(μ-Cl)]2, with the ligands BINAP, SegPhos, DM-SegPhos, and 
DiFluorPhos, through two consecutive steps starting from the precursor [(Rh(COD)(μ-Cl))2] 
(COD = cyclooctadiene).283 UV-VIS absorption spectra were used to study the kinetics of 
diolefin exchange with chelating diphosphine in the rhodium coordination sphere. For the 
BINAP exchange, an isosbestic point was observed at 338.5 nm, indicating a single 
transformation. The second order rate constants for each step were derived from stopped flow 
experiments with a diode array spectrophotometer. However, the kinetic studies were hindered 
by an equilibrium between the starting and target complex on one side, and the intermediate on 
the other. Therefore, the competitive reaction steps were separated and then successfully 

62 
 
 
quantified using UV-VIS spectroscopy. It was found that the in situ formation of these catalyst 
precursors requires a longer time than typically expected, and that the second step is rate-
determining. 
In further studies the formation of an unexpected monomeric pentacoordinated neutral complex 
of the type [RhCl(PP)(diolefin)] was reported in equilibrium with the dimeric target compound 
[Rh(PP)(μ-Cl)]2.284 The in situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy showed that this 
pentacoordinated complex is an intermediate of the reaction that may arise from the generated 
target complex [Rh(PP)(μ-Cl)]2 through reaction with the free diolefin, or even directly from 
[Rh(diolefin)(μ-Cl)]2 and the diphosphine. Thus, the quantitative formation of this 
pentacoordinate mononuclear complex can be avoided with a proper choice of the rhodium 
source and the ligands, and by controlling the reaction conditions such as the solvent and the 
temperature. To conclude this work, further detailed mechanistic investigations using in situ 
UV-VIS absorption monitoring of the [Rh(diphosphine)(μ-X)]2 preparation were performed.285 
The reaction spectra revealed different reactivity and intermediates formation depending on the 
precursor complex, the type of ligand, and the reaction conditions, especially the reaction 
temperature and the used solvent. 
Catalytic cycles 
The oxidative addition of organometallic complexes is often the rate-determining step in a 
catalytic cycle and therefore a relevant subject for operando and in situ UV-VIS absorption 
studies.286 In this context, Guilera et al. monitored the addition of iodobenzene to 
[(PPh3)2Pd(dba)] by means of in situ stopped flow UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy. The 
disappearance of the absorption bands of [(PPh3)2Pd(dba)] (at 491 and 432 nm), was monitored 
and synchronized on the millisecond scale with dispersive EXAFS.287 The study allowed the 
determination of the order and rate constant of this step, and revealed that the concerted 
oxidative addition takes place in a cis configuration, followed by a cis-trans isomerization.  
3.2.1.2 Applications of C-term MCD 
Magnetic circular dichroism spectroscopy is useful to investigate the electronic structure and 
bonding of homogeneous catalysts, especially of paramagnetic metal and f-element complexes. 
It offers insights in the interactions between ligands and the metal center.288, 289 As with 
metalloenzymes, C-term MCD intensity is most frequently analyzed due to its ability to provide 
highly informative bands. Still, its application to non-enzymatic organometallic reactions has 
so far been limited. Herein, we present lead examples that highlight the importance of C-term 
MCD in understanding reaction mechanisms, and in determining reactive species of various 
transition metal catalysts used in C-C cross-coupling reactions.290  
C-term MCD spectroscopy was used to determine the structures and coordination number of 
high-valent Ni complexes catalyzing C-C coupling.291 This catalyst shows interesting 
variations in the rate of reductive elimination, contingent upon both the oxidation state of the 
transition metal center and the characteristics of the coordinated ligands. MCD allowed the 
determination of the C-to-Ni charge transfer energies, revealing that the C-C bond formation 
during reductive elimination and the charge transfer transitions involve the same frontier 
molecular orbitals, allowing its usage for kinetic tracking. 
Iron-bisphosphine complexes represent another class of catalysts used in C-C cross-coupling 
reactions. The nature of the in situ formed iron species, the active form of the catalyst, and the 
catalytic mechanisms remain uncertain.292 Neidig et al. conducted a study on the Fe-SciOPP-
catalyzed Kumada cross-coupling of MesMgBr and primary alkyl halides, using C-term MCD 
spectroscopy to identify the reactive iron species.293 Upon adding one equivalent of MesMgBr 

63 
 
 
to FeCl2(SciOPP), the NIR MCD spectrum displays two transitions, indicating the formation 
of a tetrahedral high-spin FeII complex, FeBrMes(SciOPP) (Figure 36 A). With the addition of 
a second equivalent of MesMgBr, three new transitions emerge in the NIR MCD spectrum, 
indicating the formation of a square planar FeII complex, FeMes2(SciOPP) (Figure 36 B). When 
an excess of the Grignard reagent is introduced, the MCD spectrum reveals the formation of 
the FeMes3− complex (Figure 36 C, D). Based on these observations, it was suggested that the 
FeMes2(SciOPP) complex serves as the active species in this reaction. It first reacts with the 
primary alkyl halide to generate the C-C product and the mono-mesitylated species. Then, the 
mono-mesitylated species reacts with another equivalent of MesMgBr, leading to the 
regeneration of the active bis-mesitylated species, completing the catalytic cycle. 
 
Figure 36. 5 K, NIR MCD spectra for the reaction of FeCl2(SciOPP) with one equiv. (A), two equiv. (B), 20 
equiv. (C), and 100 equiv of MesMgBr (D). Adapted with permission from ref. 293. Copyright 2014 American 
Chemical Society. 
In a subsequent investigation, C-term MCD spectroscopy was used to study the Fe-SciOPP 
catalyst in the cross-coupling reaction involving phenyl nucleophiles and secondary alkyl 
halides.294 VTVH MCD analysis enabled the determination of crucial parameters such as the 
coordination number, oxidation state, and spin state of paramagnetic organometallic species 
generated in situ. VTVH MCD reveals that when two equivalents of PhMgBr are added to 
FeCl2(SciOPP), a four-coordinate FePh2(SciOPP) species is formed. It was further determined 
that this species adopts a high-spin distorted square pyramidal geometry. These findings 
indicate that unlike the coupling of MesMgBr and primary alkyl halides, the reactive species 
in these reactions is the mono-phenylated iron complex. 

64 
 
 
3.2.1.3 Applications of CD 
Circular dichroism (CD) spectroscopy is an optical technique highly sensitive to chirality, 
making it a valuable tool for analyzing and distinguishing analytes within a chiral host-guest 
system. It finds practical application in high-throughput screening (HTS) processes. In the 
realm of enantioselective organometallic transformations, CD spectroscopy has been employed 
to determine the enantiomeric excess (ee) for chiral catalysts. 
For metal complexes exhibiting CD-active MLCT bands, the addition of a chiral guest allows 
modulation of the MLCT bands, facilitating the differentiation of enantiomers, and thus the 
calculation of ee.  
On the other hand, Exciton-Coupled Circular Dichroism (ECCD) is used when dealing with 
compounds that contain two or more chromophores arranged in a helical manner. To establish 
the absolute configuration of the analyte the Cotton effect is analyzed. This method is 
particularly useful for resolving the handedness or stereochemistry of complex chiral molecules. 
However, the enantiomeric excess values determined from CD have relatively high absolute 
errors compared to chiral high performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) analysis, and 
therefore additional optimization should be implemented to enhance the accuracy and 
reliability of this technique.295  
ECCD was employed as an optical readout for the asymmetric copper catalyst used in the 
Michael addition of malonate to nitrostyrenes. This catalyst includes a helically chiral ligand, 
and the absolute configuration of the ligand is reversed depending on the oxidation state of the 
copper center, which in turn controls the stereoselectivity of the reaction.296 ECCD 
spectroscopy proved instrumental in differentiating between the two possible pseudo-
enantiomeric states of the catalyst that arise after reduction or oxidation. The CD spectra 
obtained for the CuII and CuI complexes displayed mirror images, consistent with the inversion 
of the asymmetric orientation of the chromophores upon change in oxidation state (Figure 37). 
These findings strongly suggest that ligand reorganization occurs alongside the inversion of 
helicity, as anticipated. 

65 
 
 
 
Figure 37. CD (L.mol−1.cm−1 ) and UV (L.mol−1.cm−1) spectra of the reduction/oxidation induced switching 
between CuII and CuI. Adapted from ref. 296 with permission from the Royal Society of Chemistry. 
3.2.1.4 Further reactions 
A large variety of chemical transformations is catalyzed using soluble organometallics. 
Examples of their in situ tracking of UV-VIS-NIR absorption are given in this section to 
emphasize the wide application. In our selection, we cover ethylene oligomerization, alcohol 
oxidation, asymmetric hydrogenation, dehydroperoxidation, and the dehydrogenation of 
formic acid. 
Ethylene oligomerization 
In situ UV-VIS was used to study the oligomerization of ethylene into metallacyclopentanes 
using for instance the rac-(ebthi)M(η2 -Me3SiC2- SiMe3) complex (with ebthi = 1,2-ethylene-
1,1’-bis(η5 -tetrahydroindenyl)) as a catalyst.297, 298 Two different organometallics with M= Zr 
(Figure 38 A) and M= Hf (Figure 38 B) were tested. The obtained reaction spectra of the Zr 
based complex with ethylene (Figure 38 C) have an isosbestic point at 436 nm indicating a 
pseudo first-order reaction with the pseudo-rate constants k’ = k.[ethylene] = 0.0365 s−1 
determined by a non-linear curve fit. Plotting k’ as a function of the ethylene concentration 
allows deriving the rate constant, here 0.35 L*mol−1*s−1.  
With the analogous Hf complex (Figure 38 B), the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra during 
reaction do not exhibit isosbestic points (Figure 38 D), and the corresponding extinction 
diagrams show two distinct straight lines indicating a consecutive reaction, wherein the first 
reaction is fast and the second is considerably slower. This allows a separate treatment of the 
two steps. However, the reaction of 1-Hf with ethylene is reversible towards 2-Hf at room 
temperature, whereas the consecutive reaction yielding 3-Hf requires 100 °C (Figure 38 B). 
Hence, an intermediate B was suggested, whose formation can occur from the reverse reaction 
of 2-Hf in presence of ethylene through an SN2 mechanism (Figure 38 B). This reveals an 
influence of ethylene on the observed overall rate formation of 2.298, 299 Plotting the observed 
rate constants k’obs.1= 78.6 s−1 and k’obs.2= 2.4 s−1, obtained from the reaction UV-Vis spectra 

66 
 
 
vs. the ethylene concentration results in the rate constants kobs.1= 929 L*mol−1*s−1 and kobs.2= 
32 L*mol−1*s−1 for both reactions. 
 
Figure 38. (A) Reaction of rac-(ebthi)Zr(η2 -Me3SiC2- SiMe3) with ethylene; (B) Reaction of rac-
(ebthi)Hf(η2 -Me3SiC2- SiMe3) with ethylene via intermediate B; (C) UV-VIS absorption spectra for the 
reaction of rac-(ebthi)Zr(η2 -Me3SiC2- SiMe3) with ethylene in toluene at r.t., k’ = 0.0365 s−1 (the pseudo-
rate constants k’ for the formation of 3-Zr as a function of ethylene concentration, rate constant k was 
determined from the slope of the linear regression); (D) UV-Vis spectra for the reaction of rac-(ebthi)Hf(η2
-Me3SiC2- SiMe3) with ethylene in toluene, at room temperzture, k’obs.1 = 78.6 s−1, k’obs.2 = 2.4 s−1). Adapted 
from ref. 297. Copyright 2010 Elsevier. 
Chromium-based 
organometallics 
complexes 
were 
also 
used 
for 
ethylene 
oligomerization.300 These catalysts promote the production of hexene or octene from 
ethylene, and are commonly employed in the reaction as precatalysts, that require an 
activator. In this process, methylaluminoxane or alkylaluminum reagents are typically 
employed as activators, playing a crucial role in reducing the Cr complex to generate the 
active intermediate. UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy was used by the group of Sydora 
and Blakemore to understand the activation processes, as well as the redox chemistry of 
these chromium-based precatalysts, prior to olefin oligomerization. Therefore, a family 
of Cr(P,N)Cl3(THF) complexes (where (P,N) represents a bidentate phosphorus- and
nitrogen-containing ligand) were studied, and the UV-VIS spectra show two d-d
absorption bands at 450 and 650 nm prior to the activation. The addition of
decamethylcobaltocene Cp*2Co as a reducing agent, results in a decrease in absorbance 
at λ = 247 and 350 nm, and a significant increase in absorbance at λ = 298 nm. Two 
isosbestic points at 237 and 276 nm are observed, indicating the reduction of the CrIII
complex to CrII. On the other hand, the reactivity of the CrIII complex was probed with 
the AlEt3 activator. The UV-VIS spectra show an increase in absorbance at λ = 227 and 
342 nm, a decrease in absorbance at λ = 297 nm, as well as two isosbestic points at 244 
and 310 nm, demonstrating the conversion of the CrIII complex to a new reduced Al-
bound Cr species. This was then titrated with Cp*2Co, resulting in a decrease of bands 
at λ = 221 and 358 nm, an increase at λ = 290 nm, with two isosbestic points at 254 and 

67 
 
 
323 nm, indicating the formation of a two-electron-reduced species. Based on these 
findings, the authors proposed that the Lewis acidity and the reductive activity of 
alkylaluminum reagents enables the multi-electron reduction of CrIII, permitting its 
backbonding with coordinated ethylene ligands, thus promoting the oligomerization 
reaction. 
Furthermore, the effect of different AlR3 activators on the performance of the Cr complex 
in the oligomerization of ethylene was investigated by Grauke et al.301 The authors used 
UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy to monitor the Cr complex formation upon mixing the 
Cr(acac)3 precursor, the (PNP) ligand (Ph2PN(iPr)PPh2), and the activator. An intra-
ligand transition band at 260 nm and a LMCT complex transition band at 337 nm were 
identified for Cr(acac)3 in cyclohexane, which remain unchanged after the addition of 
PNP, indicating no coordination to the metal center (Figure 39 A). However, upon 
addition of modified methylaluminoxane (MMAO) (used as a reference), the formation 
of (PNP)CrII(CH3)2 is detected by the appearance of a 325 nm absorption band. A higher 
band intensity is observed in the case of AlMe3, indicating an effective methylation of 
the Cr center. With AltBu, on the other hand, the alkylation is less efficient (Figure 39
B). Complementary in situ EPR, ATR-IR, and XANES/EXAFS studies were then 
conducted to better understand the valence state of Cr, and its coordination environment.
The results show that one-electron reduction is possible in presence of all Al activators, 
whereas deep reduction to CrI requires bulky activators such as AlOct3. 
 
Figure 39. (A) In situ UV-VIS absorption spectra of Cr(acac)3 before and after adding PNP ligand; (B) Spectra 
of Cr(acac)3/PNP in presence of different activators. Adapted from ref. 301. Copyright 2019 Wiley. 
Alcohol oxidation  
The aerobic oxidation of benzylic and aliphatic alcohols using Cu/TEMPO (TEMPO = 
(2,2,6,6-tetramethylpiperidin-1-yl)oxyl) is an important reaction for the synthesis of fine 
chemicals.302, 303 In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy coupled with EPR and 

68 
 
 
attenuated total reflection (ATR)-IR, and later extended to simultaneous operando 
EPR/XANES/EXAFS/UV-VIS-NIR/ATR-IR, enabled a better understanding of the 
mechanism.283, 304 Prior to the reaction, a MLCT band at 310 nm is observed when adding the 
catalyst precursor CuIOTf in MeCN. This band is shifted to a higher wavelength (348 nm), and 
a new MLCT band appears at 450 nm when bpy (2,2’-bipyridine) is added, indicating the 
replacement of the coordinated solvent by the ligand (Figure 40). The band shift revealed the 
dipolar interaction of the (bpy)CuI complex with O2, indicating a stabilization of the CuI species 
against oxidation as the band at ~660 nm, corresponding to CuII d-d transitions, remains 
negligible (Figure 40). However, coordination of NMI (N-methylimidazole) to the (bpy)CuI 
complex reduces the CuII/CuI reduction potential and raises its ability to reduce O2 towards 
(bpy)(NMI)(CH3CN)CuII-OOH and (bpy)(NMI)CuII(µ-OH)2CuII(bpy)(NMI)(CH3CN).305 This 
binuclear species is cleaved during reaction with TEMPO to form (bpy)(NMI)(OOH)CuII-
TEMPO and (bpy)(NMI)(CH3CN)CuIIOOH-TEMPO, which are considered to be the active 
species in the alcohol oxidation. 
 
Figure 40. (a) UV-VIS spectra of [(MeCN)4]CuOTf complex in MeCN (b)+bpy (c) after 20 min bubbling O2 at
20 °C (d) +NMI (e) +NMI after 15 min (f) +BzOH (g) +TEMPO. Adapted from ref. 304. Copyright 2015 Wiley. 
Asymmetric hydrogenation 
The rhodium diphosphine complex [Rh(Me-Duphos)(cod)]BF4 (structure 4, Figure 41 A) was 
reported as a catalyst for the in situ hydrogenation of the cylooctadiene group (cod) in MeOH. 
This catalyst generates the corresponding solvated complex, which is the active catalytic 

69 
 
 
species in the reaction (Figure 41 A).297 Due to low amount of the cod hydrogenation 
intermediate (cyclooctene complex (coe)),306 the consecutive reaction can be regarded as a 
pseudo-first-order reaction with a pseudo-rate constants k'2cod. The recorded UV-VIS 
absorption spectra during the reaction showed four isosbestic points indicating a kinetically 
uniform reaction (Figure 41 B). From nonlinear curve fitting of the decay, and fitting the 
evolution to a pseudo-first order kinetic equation, the pseudo rate constant k'2cod was calculated 
to be 0.119 min−1. 
 
Figure 41. (A) The formation of the solvate complex [Rh(Me-DuPHOS)(MeOH)2]BF4 via coe intermediate; (B) 
Reaction spectra for the hydrogenation of [Rh(Me-Du-PHOS)(cod)]BF4 in MeOH at r.t. Adapted from ref. 297. 
Copyright 2010 Elsevier. 
Similarly, the authors investigated the deactivation mechanism of the rhodium diphosphine 
complexes in asymmetric hydrogenation. The presence of aromatic solvents in the reaction 
such as benzene and toluene have the potential to inhibit these reactions. Hence, the stability 
constants of the rhodium diphosphine complexes were obtained via UV-VIS absorption 
monitoring of the benzene titration of [Rh(DPPE)(MeOH)2]BF4 to derive the desired stability 
constant of 18 l/mol after linearization using the Benesi-Hildebrand equation.307 
An enantioselective version of this reaction was explored using the chiral [Rh(dipamp)][BF4] 
complex as a catalyst for the selective hydrogenation of ethyl-(Z)-α-acetamidocinnamate (mac) 
to N-acetyl-(R)-phenylalanine.308 The reaction intermediate [Rh(dipamp)(mac)]+, generated 
upon coordination of mac to the rhodium center via the C=C and the oxygen atom of the amide 
group, normally leads to the formation of the S conformer. Thus, to understand this unique 
stereoselectivity, in situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy was used for thorough kinetic studies. 
The results revealed that the reactivity of the intermediate diastereoisomers towards hydrogen 
is at the origin of the observed enantioselectivity. 
In another study, 309 the transfer hydrogenation of acetophenone using the Ikariya–Noyori 
complex [(mesitylene)RuCl(R,R)-(TsDPEN)] in presence of formic acid/triethylamine 
mixtures was studied using operando UV-VIS-NIR absorption and NMR spectroscopy to 
monitor the reaction intermediates. The generated formate complex was detected by NMR, 
while the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy allowed tracking the unsaturated reaction 
intermediate during the reaction based on its characteristic absorbance at 565 nm. The UV-VIS 
absorption data imply an almost quantitative transformation of the unsaturated intermediate 
during the reaction towards the formate complex in presence of formic acid, but a less favorable 
decarboxylation of the latter towards the Ru-hydride complex. These findings suggest that three 
reactions can compete with the hydride complex formation, (1) a re-insertion of CO2 forming 

70 
 
 
the formate complex, (2) hydrogen transfer to form the desired product and (3) protonation by 
the excess of formic acid, which causes its consumption and a decrease in the reaction 
performance. 
Dehydroperoxidation  
In 2018, Schaub and coworkers investigated the chromium catalyzed dehydroperoxidation of 
cyclohexyl hydroperoxide (CHHP) to cyclohexanone.276 With in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectroscopy, the metal complex species were detected, a reaction mechanism was proposed, 
and the reaction conditions were optimized. The absence of strong absorption bands between 
300-1000 nm in the pure catalyst spectra indicates the +6 oxidation state (Ar electron 
configuration) of the Cr center. Further addition of CHHP leads to a decrease of the absorption 
band at 450 nm linked to the coordination of the substrate to the CrVI. Since no new bands are 
observed around 600 nm, the oxidations state is considered unchanged. Interestingly, a new 
absorption band at 480 nm, assigned to a peroxy to CrVI CT, appears, indicating the formation 
of the cyclohexylperoxychromium(VI)complex (Figure 42). A blueshift of the absorbance to 
420 nm occurs with the decomposition of this complex into CrO2(OH)2. The authors propose 
that the decomposition of cyclohexyl hydroperoxide proceeds via a non-radical, non-redox 
mechanism, through formation of the cyclohexylperoxy chromium complex as the reactive 
intermediate. This was confirmed by further NMR and DFT studies.  
 
Figure 42. UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of the reaction of CrO3*py in presence of CHHP in α,α,α-
trifluorotoluene at 5 °C. Insert: (blue line) Spectrum of the formed cyclohexylperoxychromium(VI) complex after 
2 min; (shifted black line) decomposition after 10 min and (red line) full decomposition after 48 h. Adapted from 
ref. 276. Copyright 2018 Wiley. 
Formic acid dehydrogenation 
Formic acid decomposes into CO2 and H2 and is therefore a liquid carrier for these gases under 
acidic conditions and at ambient temperature. The Fe/PP3 iron catalyst system, formed in situ 
from Fe(BF4)2-6H2O and the tetradentate ligand tris[(2-diphenylphosphino)-ethyl]phosphine 
(P(CH2CH2PPh2)3, PP3), is an effective catalyst for this reaction.310, 311 In situ Raman, ATR-IR, 
and UV-VIS absorption spectroscopies were used to identify the intermediate complexes.312 
The UV-VIS absorption spectrum of the Fe/PP3 system in THF shows the π → π* and MLCT 
transitions of the formed complex (bands at 277 and 508 nm, respectively), as well as the 
interaction between the Fe/PP3 complex and the additional THF ligand (318 nm absorption 
band) (Figure 43). Upon addition of formic acid, the band at 318 immediately disappears, and 
the MLCT band loses intensity and shifts to 499 nm. The formic acid coordinates onto the 
Fe/PP3 catalytic system by displacing the previously coordinated THF to form the Fe-PP3-
bidentate formate complex, which is considered the catalytically active complex for the release 

71 
 
 
of CO2 and H2. The addition of NaCl led to the formation of a deactivated [FeCl(PP3)]BF4 
complex in THF, as chlorine is preferentially coordinated over formic acid. This appears in the 
UV-VIS absorption spectrum through absorption bands at 327 and 380 nm, and a redshift of 
the 499 nm absorption band (Figure 43). 
 
 
Figure 43. In situ UV-VIS absorption spectra obtained from Fe/PP3 in THF, after addition of formic acid and 
further addition of NaCl. Adapted from ref. 313. Copyright 2014 American Chemical Society. 
A recently introduced setup to study homogeneous catalysts with in situ DRS uses a suspension 
of α-Al2O3 to diffusively reflect the incident light (Figure 3 B). The signal is processed using 
the K-M formula, with K/S assumed directly proportional to the concentration of measured 
species in the solution. The scattering coefficient was calculated as the reciprocal average 
distance between the α-Al2O3 particles.30 This system was used to study the catalytic 
dehydrogenation of formic acid to H2 and CO2 catalyzed by the iridium complex 
[Cp*Ir(4DHBP)(OH2)]SO4 at 80 °C. The turnover frequency (TOF) and turnover number 
(TON) of the reaction were determined at 5467 h−1 and 15094, respectively, in agreement with 
previous studies. Advantages of the approach are a high signal-to-noise ratio and the reliable 
collection of spectra without mechanical errors from gas meters. However, the impact of the 
escaping light should be critically evaluated, as the K-M assumption of infinite sample 
thickness (see section 2.1.1) is clearly not fulfilled. 
3.2.2 Non-organometallic transformation 
3.2.2.1 Imide reduction 
The reduction of imides and amides in the presence of phenylsilanes as hydrogenating agent, 
is a good example of homogeneously catalyzed non-organometallic reactions. They are 
commonly studied by in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption, ATR-IR, and Raman spectroscopy. 
Using boronic acid,314 or fluoride catalysts,315 two scenarios are proposed. The catalyst can 
either activate the target reagents or the hydrogenating agent.313 For the tetra-n-
butylammonium fluoride (TBAF)-catalyzed reaction, the in situ UV-VIS absorption spectra 
show no interaction between the imide (N-methylphthalimide) and TBAF, as the imide bands 
at 265, 302, and 324 nm remain unchanged over time in the presence of the catalyst (Figure 44 
A). However, upon the addition of Ph2SiH2, the 324 nm absorption starts fading indicating the 

72 
 
 
onset of the carbonyl group reduction. A new absorption band at 370 nm appears from the 
desired product (N-methylisoindolinone). An additional band at 536 nm indicates the formation 
of small amounts of chromophores as side products. It was derived that in the first stage of the 
reaction, TBAF is coordinated to the silicon atom of Ph2SiH2 forming a reactive penta-
coordinated silicon species. This readily attacks the carbonyl group of the imide yielding a 
hexacoordinated silicon species where the hydride transfer can take place.  
In the boronic acid-catalyzed reduction of N,N-dimethylbenzamide to N,N-dimethylbenzamine, 
interaction of boronic acid with the amide via hydrogen bonding is observed by in situ UV-
VIS absorption spectroscopy (Figure 44 B). The addition of PhSiH3 to the boronic acid reveals 
no change in the spectra whereas the further addition of the amide causes a significant blueshift 
indicating the interaction (Figure 44 B, C). The mechanism is proposed to be initiated by the 
activation of the carbonyl group of the amide by the boronic acid, enabling attack of the silane 
towards the formation of the corresponding siloxane and the desired amine. Thus, in both 
reactions with boronic acid or fluoride, the formation of phenylsiloxanes is believed to be the 
driving force. 
 
Figure 44. (A) In situ UV-VIS absorption spectra for the reaction of N-methylphthalimide and Ph2SiH2 using 
TBAF as catalyst; (B) UV-VIS absorption spectra of N,N-dimethylbenzamide, PhSiH3, and the boric acid and 
their mixtures in toluene; (C) in situ UV-VIS absorption spectra for the reaction of N,N-dimethylbenzamide and 
PhSiH3 with the boric acid in toluene. Adapted from refs. 314, 315. Copyright 2011 Wiley and 2013 Wiley. 
3.2.2.2 Cycloaddition 
Another example of non-organometallic transformations studied by in situ UV-VIS is the [3 + 
3] cyclocondensation reaction of ketene acetals with a diene. Depending on the nature of the 
Lewis acid and its interaction with the ketene acetal, different products can be obtained. The 
use of both AlCl3 and TiCl4 leads to the generation of salicylate,316 but via a different 
mechanism.317 A strong redshift of the absorption edge is observed in the case of TiCl4, 
indicating an increase in electron density of the C-O bond of the ketene-acetal by electron 
delocalization (Figure 45). This is due to the TiCl4 attack, transforming the group into a 
chlorine-containing titanium bis-chelate complex, active in the UV-VIS region. For AlCl3 the 
absence of dπ electrons prevents the formation of metal-donor resonance structures, and the 
absorption edge in the UV-VIS absorption spectrum is accordingly slightly blue-shifted. An 
aluminum tris-chelate complex without chlorinated ligands is formed instead. 

73 
 
 
 
Figure 45. In situ UV-Vis absorption spectra recorded during the (A) AlCl3- and (B) TiCl4-mediated reaction 
using ketenacetale (6a & 6b) in CH2Cl2. Adapted from ref. 313, using data with permission from refs. 316, 317. 
Copyrights 2013 American Chemical Society, 2011 Wiley and 2013 Elsevier. 
As illustrated above, in situ UV-VIS spectroscopy has proven its relevance for monitoring 
homogeneous transformations, especially when organometallic complexes are involved. 
Nevertheless, IR and NMR spectroscopies are preferably used in this field, as shorter 
wavelengths (higher energy radiation) often promote side reactions.318 As also shown in this 
section, detailed analysis and interpretation of UV-VIS-NIR absorption bands is rather rare in 
homogeneous catalysis studies. Instead, single transformations with clear isosbestic points in 
the absorption spectrum are commonly performed, and any dynamics in the region of the 
absorption spectrum can be used for kinetic measurements. As with heterogeneous catalysis, 
no single spectroscopy can provide full understanding of the relevant properties, and the 
coupling of different spectroscopies is common.319 This applies for instance to organometallic 
reactions, where UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is often combined with attenuated total 
reflection (ATR)-IR and Raman spectroscopy to identify the metal complexes as well as the 
intermediate species.320 
3.3 Enzymes 
Using complex metal-containing active sites, metalloenzymes perform some of the most 
difficult and selective catalytic reactions. Metalloenzyme catalysis involves active sites that 
combine properties of heterogeneous and homogeneous catalysis. They involve a large variety 
of ligated complexes that influence UV-VIS-NIR sensitive properties, derived from electronic 
transitions both on the metals and on the ligands, as observed for homogeneous TMI complexes. 
Besides this, they also are influenced by their mantle, similarly to the support effects in 
heterogeneous catalysis, but supplemented with properties unique to the extended protein 
ligand. This section shows examples of typical methods used in the kinetic and mechanistic 

74 
 
 
study of metalloenzyme catalysis with in situ UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopy. Then the power of 
combined absorption, rR, and MCD spectroscopy, originating from the field of 
metalloenzymes, is demonstrated. We summarize two recent applications of stopped-flow 
absorption combined with a higher resolution spectroscopic method on metalloenzyme 
intermediates. Spectroscopic definition of these intermediates and stopped-flow derived 
isotope sensitivity of kinetics (kinetic isotope effects) allowed determination of key reaction 
mechanisms. These intermediates also parallel relevant intermediates defined in heterogeneous 
metallozeolite catalysts from site selective spectroscopic studies. 
A challenge common to all characterisation of enzymes remains the measurement of in vivo 
properties in the setups used for in situ characterisation.321, 322 In heterogeneous catalysis one 
often refers to the ‘pressure gap’ to indicate the discrepancies between the productive 
conditions and the conditions under which spectroscopic measurement is possible. For 
enzymes the gap is of another nature. In vivo conditions often imply low enzyme 
concentrations and specific environments (e.g. cofactors, ionic strength, pH, temperature, 
embedding in cellular membranes, …). Deviations from these conditions can induce non-
negligible changes to enzyme structure and reactivity. Naturally, one may just as well be more 
interested in enzyme characterization in reactor conditions for industrial purposes. Both types 
of settings can bedifficult to correspond with in situ measurements, commonly on isolated, up-
concentrated enzymes at low temperatures. The fragility and redox sensitivity of enzymes, as 
well as their strong diversity can present additional challenges to sample preparation and to 
scholarly consensus. An well-known example is the discussion on the structure of the active 
site in particulate monooxygenase.323 Moreover radiative damage makes measurements with 
high energy radiation problematic. 
A more UV-VIS-NIR range specific challenge for the spectroscopic investigation of enzyme 
catalysis is the interfering absorption by the surrounding mantle. Aromatic amino acids absorb 
strongly in the UV and this can make certain active site signals difficult to discern. An option 
is to measure instead on smallermodel complexes, but then any relevant effect of the greater 
protein structure is no longer considered. 
3.3.1 Kinetics: stopped-flow absorption and rapid freeze quench trapping 
In their catalytic cycles, metalloenzymes progress through a series of key intermediates; 
understanding these individual steps is necessary to unlock deep insight into enzyme function. 
Stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy is used to investigate them. This technique is 
particularly useful for metalloenzymes since the open shell metal centers within the active site 
have clear absorption features that can be tracked. A great example is the measurement of a 
critical intermediate and its reactivity in the catalysis of soluble methane monooxygenase 
(sMMO). sMMO is a binuclear non-heme iron enzyme capable of oxidizing methane to 
methanol at ambient conditions, a difficult feat given the strong C-H bond of methane.324 
Stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption was essential in observing the CH4 reactive intermediate Q 
of sMMO through appearance of its distinct 430 nm absorbance feature.325 This feature 
provided a handle for tracking the kinetics of the reaction of intermediate Q with CH4. The rate 
of decay of the 430 nm feature with CH4 allows extraction of Q’s very low CH4 reaction barrier 
(ΔG‡ = 10.3 kcal/mol).326 Substituting CH4 for CD4 allows determination of an H/D kinetic 
isotope effect of 50 via the change in rate of decay of the 430 nm feature (Figure 46).327 This 
large KIE indicates Q reacts with CH4 via hydrogen atom abstraction (HAA) in a tunneling 
mechanism. These and other data have been used recently to rationalize the low barrier of HAA 
from CH4 by using the front oxo of the two oxo bridges of the diferryl active site to overlap 

75 
 
 
with the H-C bonding orbital of CH4 and using the oxo bridge at the rear to stabilize the electron 
gained from the HAA (Figure 47).328  
 
Figure 46. Change in the rate of decay of intermediate Q with varying CH4 and CD4 concentrations. (Inset) 
Stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectra of decay of intermediate Q from 4 to 30 s. Adapted from refs. 325, 327. 
Copyright 1993 The American Society for Biochemistry and Molecular Biology and copyright 1996 American 
Chemical Society. 
 
Figure 47. Density functional theory model of sMMO’s intermediate Q performing H-atom abstraction on CH4. 
(Color scheme: red = O, green = Fe, grey = C, blue = N, and white = H). Adapted from ref. 328. Copyright 2021 
American Chemical Society. 
The absorption features still lack the capacity to probe the chemical structure of intermediates, 
necessitating the use of methods that can be coupled to the absorption band for higher 
resolution analysis. This requires trapping of the intermediates through RFQ. Other key 
spectroscopies, also using UV-VIS-NIR range light, can be subsequently used to gain 
information from the absorption features of the intermediate in the frozen mixture. For charge 
transfer bands that have high absorption intensity, rR spectroscopy can be used to gain 
vibrational information on the active site of the intermediate. For ligand field bands, which are 
often too weak to observe in stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption, MCD at low temperature can 
allow visualization of these bands. Additionally, magnetic information on the ground state of 
the intermediate’s active site can be obtained through VTVH MCD of the excited state intensity. 
Background on these techniques and metalloenzyme applications paralleling studies on 
metallozeolites are explored below, but for a more in-depth background we point readers to 
our previous review.100  
3.3.2 Resonance Raman profiling of absorption bands 
In the case of the protein oxy-hemocyanin, the vibrations of a small four atom core in a 73 kDA 
protein that has a ~3 mM concentration can be observed in rR and used to assign the the 
vibrations and electronic structure of its μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) core (Figure 48 A, B).205, 

76 
 
 
329 Isotope perturbation (or lack of perturbation) was extremely helpful in vibrational 
assignments of 18O2 oxy-hemocyanin, assigning both the O-O stretch at 744 cm−1 from the 
significant redshift upon 18O labeling and the Cu-Cu stretch at 286 cm−1 from the lack of isotope 
perturbation.329 Additionally, non-totally symmetric vibrations are not resonance Raman 
allowed, resulting in weak intensity like the 543 cm−1 Cu-O (B3u) mode in oxy-hemocyanin; 
however, the first overtone at 2x the energy of the fundamental is symmetric and resonance 
Raman allowed, giving the intensity seen in the 1087 cm−1 vibration (Figure 48 B). Finally, the 
intensity of all these vibrations is measured at different laser excitation wavelengths to generate 
a resonance Raman excitation profile, helping assign the two LMCT bands of hemocyanin as 
the peroxide σ and peroxide π CT bands at higher and lower energy respectively (Figure 48 
C).205 
 
Figure 48. (A) Arthropod hemocyanin with active site highlighted. (B) Resonance Raman spectrum of oxy-
hemocyanin. (C) Resonance Raman excitation profile of the 286 cm−1 (Cu-Cu) and 744 cm−1 (O-O) vibrations of 
hemocyanin. Adapted with permission from refs. 205, 329. Copyright 1994 and 2014 American Chemical Society. 
Stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy coupled to rR spectroscopy was critical in 
solving a major outstanding issue in bioinorganic chemistry, the mechanism of 
monooxygenation by the enzyme tyrosinase. Tyrosinase (Ty) is a coupled binuclear Cu enzyme 
that binds O2 to catalyze the rate-limiting step in melanin biosynthesis. Oxy-Ty uses a μ-η2:η2-
peroxide dicopper(II) core to monooxygenate L-tyrosine to L-DOPA (Figure 49 A); this core 
can then oxidize catechols to ortho-quinones that couple to form melanins. This core has two 
clear peroxide to CuII charge transfer transitions at 345 and 650 nm. The same transitions are 
observed in oxy-hemocyanin. Lasers can be tuned into these CT bands to reveal the same set 
of rR features as in oxy-Hemocyanin: the Cu-Cu stretch at 280 cm−1, the O-O stretch at 739 
cm−1, and the rR allowed first overtone of the forbidden B3u Cu-O stretch at 1078 cm−1 (Figure 
49 B).205 These CT bands also provided a handle to gain kinetic information on active site 
reactivity via stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy. 

77 
 
 
 
Figure 49. (A) Conversion of L-tyrosine to L-DOPAquinone by the enzyme tyrosinase in melanin biosynthesis. 
(B) The main vibrational modes of the Cu2O2 core in tyrosinase. Resonance Raman spectra of oxy-Ty (blue) and 
the ternary intermediate of tyrosinase (red). (C) Dependence of kobs on substrate concentration in H2O (solid blue) 
and D2O (solid red) with a linear fit of the low substrate regime (dashed). (D) QM/MM reaction coordinate for 
the monooxygenation of phenol by tyrosinase. Adapted from ref. 330. Copyright 2022 National Academy of 
Sciences. 
Under single turnover conditions where oxy-Ty solely monooxygenates the phenolic substrate, 
the rate of decay of the oxy-Ty intermediate’s 345 nm feature has linear dependence at low 
substrate concentrations, but deviates from linearity at higher substrate concentrations (Figure 
49 C). This nonlinear dependence indicates the rapid formation of a ternary intermediate upon 
binding of substrate.330 RFQ of deoxy-Ty mixed with an O2 saturated buffered solution 
containing substrate allows trapping of this ternary intermediate. Tuning a laser into the 345 
nm feature of this intermediate for rR spectroscopy reveals the same three features as in Oxy-
Ty, but with the O-O stretch and the B3u overtone shifted by +3 cm−1 and +24 cm−1, respectively. 
This indicates the preservation of the μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) core but with an active site 
perturbation from binding of substrate. This spectroscopic perturbation combined with a 
solvent secondary kinetic isotope effect (Figure 49 C) was a significant revelation as it ruled 
out past models for the ternary complex in tyrosinase including direct bonding of a 
deprotonated phenol to one of the copper atoms or oxidation of the CuII to CuIII. Multiple 
QM/MM models were evaluated for possible substrate binding to the active site with one model 
reasonably matching the spectroscopic observations. This model has the phenolic substrate 
bound protonated above the μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) core and hydrogen bonding to one 
of the oxygen atoms of the peroxide (Figure 49 D left). Understanding this key intermediate 
allowed derivation of the reaction mechanism for monooxygenation. The docking of the 
protonated substrate at the core with the hydrogen bond to the peroxide allows proton transfer 
to the active site upon breaking the O-O bond. The subsequent non-protonated bridging oxo is 
then able to attack the ortho carbon of the aromatic core of the substrate to form the C-O 
bond.330 
3.3.3 The application of magnetic circular dichroism spectroscopy 
MCD spectroscopy was a particularly helpful characterization tool for the blue copper site in 
the intensely blue colored protein plastocyanin where the CT bands at higher energy (Figure 
50 bands 1-4) have smaller C0/D0 ratios whereas the low energy ligand field transitions (Figure 
A 
C 
B 
D 

78 
 
 
50 bands 5-8) have larger C0/D0 ratios. The low energy of the ligand field transitions coupled 
to ligand field theory first showed that the blue copper site has a distorted tetrahedral 
structure.331  
 
Figure 50. (Top) low-temperature absorption and (bottom) low-temperature MCD of plastocyanin. Adapted with 
permission from ref. 331. Copyright 2004 American Chemical Society. 
Additionally, as indicated by the scheme in Figure 6, changes in MCD intensity with variation 
in the temperature and applied field of the sample also reflect the magnetic properties of the 
ground state. VTVH MCD can be plotted (Figure 51) and fit to extract spin Hamiltonian 
parameters of the ground states of key intermediates. This has been extremely useful in the 
case of non-Kramers systems (i.e. integer spin) like high spin FeII and FeIV (S = 2) systems that 
are generally not detectable by EPR spectroscopy.332 These spin Hamiltonian parameters 
provide complimentary insight into geometric and electronic structure. These changes in 
intensity with temperature and field are also dependent on the polarization of the electronic 
transition, providing information that is valuable for band assignment.18, 333 
 
Figure 51. (Top) Saturation magnetization VTVH MCD for an S = 2 system. Adapted with permission from ref. 
332. Copyright 2013 American Chemical Society. 
UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy of a key intermediate was critical in understanding the 
mechanism of selective substrate halogenation by the α-ketoglutarate dependent Fe enzyme 
SyrB2, although here through coupling to variable-temperature (VT) MCD. SyrB2 is a 
mononuclear non-heme enzyme involved in the synthesis of the phytotoxin syringomycin E.334 
This enzyme reacts with O2 to form a high-spin FeIV=O intermediate that performs H-atom 
abstraction to selectively halogenate specific C-H bonds.335 This key FeIV=O intermediate can 
be clearly observed through stopped-flow UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy through 

79 
 
 
appearance and decay of a distinct 318 nm absorbance feature with additional lower energy 
weaker bands. These absorption features are broad and overlap, giving minimal information 
(Figure 52 top). However, MCD of the trapped intermediate at low temperature reveals detailed 
insight into these electronic transitions with three clear bands observed at low energy (Figure 
52 bottom, bands I-III). VT MCD of these bands at a strong magnetic field (7 T) was correlated 
to the zero field splitting of the ground state of the intermediate (D = +7 cm−1 and E/D = 
0.02).336 
 
Figure 52. Absorption (top) and 7 T VT MCD (bottom) of the Br-FeIV=O intermediate in SyrB2. The lowest 
energy bands are labeled with roman numerals. *Minor heme contaminant. Adapted from ref. 336. Copyright 2016 
American Chemical Society. 
The derivative shape of band I between 8 000 and 14 000 cm−1 combined with its C0/D0 ratio 
and temperature dependence allowed its assignment as the ligand field dxz/yzπ* → dz²σ* 
transition. The higher resolution of the low temperature MCD spectrum reveals the vibronic 
coupling of the Fe-O stretch in this electronic transition. Two sets of vibronic progressions are 
necessary to fit Band I, indicating a split in the energy of the degenerate dxz/yzπ* set to two 
transitions at ~9 500 cm−1 and 10 200 cm−1 (Figure 53 A). This is due to the halide binding to 
the FeIV along the y-axis in the equatorial plane of the halogenase active site that π-donates and 
raises the energy of the dyz orbital compared to the dxz orbital; the dxz orbital has a stronger 
interaction with the oxo p π orbital due to lack of competition with the halide ligand. Parameters 
extracted from the vibronic progressions allowed mapping of the excited state potential energy 
surfaces (Figure 53 B). The Fe-O bond elongates as this enzyme approaches the transition state. 
This elongation has a more dramatic effect on the dxz orbital compared to the dyz orbital due to 
its greater interaction with the oxo p π orbital. Thus, at the transition state, the dxz → dz² 
transition is lower in energy and more polarized towards an FeIII oxyl in the xz plane for H-
atom abstraction (Figure 53 C).336 

80 
 
 
 
Figure 53. (A) VT MCD spectrum of band I, the dxz/yzπ* → dz²σ* transitions in the FeIV=O intermediate in SyrB2. 
(B) Potential energy surfaces of the ground state and two lowest energy excited states. (C) FMO contours of the 
FeIV=O intermediate at the transition state with potential approach of the C-H bond in the yz plane (top) and xz 
plane (bottom). X refers to the halide. Adapted from ref. 336. Copyright 2016 American Chemical Society. 
Thus, there are two potential routes for approach of the reactant’s C-H bond for HAA: parallel 
to the Fe-O bond through the dz² orbital with oxo pσ character, the σ pathway, or perpendicular 
to the Fe-O bond through the dxz orbital with oxo pπ character, the π pathway. The π pathway 
requires promotion of an electron from the dxz to dz², which is high in energy (~32 kcal/mol) at 
the ground state geometry (Fe-O = 1.64 Å). However, as the Fe-O bond elongates to reach the 
TS at a bond length of 1.82 Å, this promotion energy lowers to ~7 kcal/mol (Figure 54).337 This 
decreased promotion energy combined with the greater oxyl character of the LUMO in the 
excited state results in roughly equal barriers for HAA through either the σ or π channel, 
parallel or perpendicular to the Fe-O bond respectively.  
 
Figure 54. Change in the promotion energy from the dπ* to dσ* orbital with elongation of the FeIV=O bond. 
Ground state dσ* LUMO with O character (bottom left) and excited state dπ* LUMO with O character (top right). 
Adapted from ref. 337. Copyright 2020 American Chemical Society. 
HAA through the σ channel can only allow rebound hydroxylation due to the orientation of the 
resulting radical along the resulting FeIII-OH bond. However, HAA through the π channel 
orients the radical above the Fe(OH)Cl plane (Figure 55 left), allowing kinetic feasibility for 

81 
 
 
either rebound hydroxylation or halogenation. Although hydroxylation is thermodynamically 
favored, the lower driving force of halogenation is compensated by the lower intrinsic barrier
