for halogenation rebound making it the favorable reaction pathway for the native substrate 
(Figure 55).338 This requires the second-sphere of the protein to play a large role in promoting 
halogenation. It orients the C-H bond to approach through the π channel to prevent the 
hydroxylation that would occur through the σ channel. It also positions the substrate radical for 
good overlap with the halide dπ orbital to promote halogenation. Stopped-flow absorption 
spectroscopy identified the key FeIV=O intermediate and RFQ coupled to low temperature 
MCD (and nuclear resonance vibrational spectroscopy)339 allowed elucidation of its geometric 
and electronic structure that determine its reactivity. 
 
Figure 55. DFT model of the Cl-FeIII-OH SyrB2 intermediate chlorination (green) vs hydroxylation (red) after 
HAA reaction coordinate through the π channel. Adapted from ref. 338. Copyright 2017 American Chemical 
Society. 
3.3.4 Correlation of enzyme active site intermediates to metallozeolite catalysts 
The constraints of the protein on the first coordination sphere of the metal cofactor and the 
complex second-sphere interactions of their active site pockets with the substrate bring about 
their highly selective and difficult chemistry. These have some parallels in heterogeneous 
catalysts. Specifically, metallozeolites are known to both bind metals with a rigid first 
coordination sphere and have complex second-sphere interactions with substrates that dictate 
reactivity.26, 100, 177 Thus, understanding of key intermediates in metalloenzymes gained from a 
combination of stopped-flow absorption spectroscopy, RFQ, rR, and MCD can provide insight 
into metallozeolite intermediates. This is enhanced by the site-selectivity of rR and VT MCD 
in probing an active site in a mixture. 
In particular, the μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) core seen in the coupled binuclear copper 
enzyme family is also observed in copper containing zeolites. In copper zeolite O2 chemistry, 
this μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) intermediate is a precursor that upon heating converts to the 
methane reactive mono-μ-oxo dicopper(II) active site (section 3.1.2.2).188 Understanding this 
active site in biology can provide insight into how the μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) converts 
to the mono-μ-oxo dicopper(II) core in zeolites, a phenomenon that is not well understood. It 
also indicates the potential reactivity of this μ-η2:η2-peroxide dicopper(II) precursor site in 

82 
 
 
zeolites. Monooxygenation of benzene at low temperature has been observed in Fe zeolites162 
but is a relatively uncultivated field in copper zeolites. The presence of a core that is known to 
monooxygenate phenol in copper biology could provide insight into whether copper zeolites 
can perform similar low temperature monooxygenation chemistry. 
Additionally, the FeIV=O core seen in a variety of iron containing enzymes also plays an 
important role in zeolite catalysis. The α-O FeIV=O intermediate in iron zeolites is able to 
oxidize methane to methanol at room temperature (section 3.1.2.5).17 The knowledge gained 
from understanding FeIV=O FMOs in these biological systems provides insight into the 
mechanism of HAA in α-O. Specifically, the lowest energy MCD band observed in α-O appears 
to have vibronic structure very similar to the lowest energy band in the FeIV=O intermediate of 
SyrB2. Just as analysis of this key ligand-field band in SyrB2 unlocked understanding of the 
HAA and rebound mechanisms occurring through specific d-orbital FMOs, similar insight in 
α-O could provide deeper insight into its mechanism of HAA and rebound that relates to 
differences in zeolite topology. 
3.4 Electrocatalysis 
In electrocatalysis, an external electric potential is used as the driving force, pushing reactions 
by shifting thermodynamic equilibria, and enabling thermally inaccessible reaction pathways 
and chemical intermediates.10 Electron transfer and changes in oxidation state are central to 
this type of catalysts, which are consequently mostly based on transition metals. The systems 
make use of solid state electrodes of conducting material and a catalyst that is either in solution 
or supported on the electrodes.340 UV-VIS-NIR excitations probe properties that are directly 
relevant to electrocatalysis, and it is therefore a very popular technique in their study. The 
measurement of high quality spectra can, however, be challenged by the multiphase nature of 
the systems and by the need for diffuse reflectance mode measurements. Moreover, while in 
thermal heterogeneous catalysis the surface is often most relevant and representative for the 
studied catalysis, in electrocatalysis electron exchange further below the surface may be 
relevant.341 This is less accessible for UV-VIS-NIR radiation. The occurrence of preferential 
conduction paths may lead to non-uniformities and non-representative measurements.342, 343 
Also research into electrochemical batteries and sensors, both driven by similar processes as 
electrocatalysis, is well supported by UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. The use of 
operando UV-VIS spectroscopy for (photo)electocatalysis was very recently reviewed by 
Mesa et al.10 In this section we will therefore limit ourselves to some recent examples. 
Despite decades of extensive research, the efficiency and stability of electrocatalytic processes 
still remain low.344 Of particular interest is the identification of bottlenecks and contributions 
to overpotentials, the elucidation of the preferential redox couples, and the investigation of 
their reversibility. Ott et al. used in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy to monitor the 
reversible redox behavior of cobalt species in metal-organic framework (MOF) UU-
100(Co).345 Under an applied potential of -1.50 V in DMF, freshly prepared UU-100(Co)(CoIII) 
undergoes reduction and new absorption bands at ∼520 and 670 nm linked to CoI appear 
(Figure 56 A). Reoxidation of the catalyst by stepping the potential back to -0.05 V results in 
a 670 nm bleach band and a slight redshift (∼1-2 nm) of the 520 nm peak, which indicates the 
formation of CoII. With repetitive electrochromic switching experiments and monitoring the 
absorbance changes at 520 and 670 nm, the reversibility of the CoI/CoII switching was shown. 
The switch between CoI and CoIII, on the other hand, was found to be prohibited (Figure 56 B). 
A similar CoI ↔ CoII system was investigated with in situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy by 
Song et al., who observed the same absorption bands (Figure 56 C).346 

83 
 
 
 
Figure 56. UV-VIS-NIR absorption data on (A) UU-100(Co) thin film switching between -1.5 and -0.05 V, and 
(C) COF-366-Co at steady-state at different applied potentials. (B) Optical Absorbance kinetics curve of the UU-
100(Co) thin films measured at 520 and 670 nm by switching the potential from -0.05 to -1.5 V. Adapted from 
ref. 345, 346. Copyrights 2019 American Chemical Society and 2015 American Association for the Advancement of 
Science. 
The evolution of the UV-VIS-NIR absorption was followed in electrocatalytic water oxidation 
over an Ir-based catalyst.347, 348 From the UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of IrOx under applied 
potentials (Figure 57 A, left) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) of the IrOx films, Carlota and 
coworkers identified three redox transitions and three redox states beyond the starting redox 
state as well as their characteristic absorption bands above the open-circuit potential (OCP).347 
By taking the spectroscopic handles identified by comparison to the absorption spectra of Ir 
mono-μ-oxo dimers containing IrIII-IrIII, IrIV- IrIV, IrIV- IrV and IrV- IrV moieties as a reference, 
the three redox transitions, at 600, 800 and 460 nm, are assigned to the sequential oxidation of 
IrIII-IrIII to IrIII-IrIV, IrIV-IrIV, and IrIV-IrV, respectively.349 With a model using the Lambert-Beer 
law and Gaussian distributions of concentration over potential, the absorption spectra were 
deconvoluted and concentrations of each redox state at different potentials were derived 
(Figure 57 B). Analysis of the bias-dependent optical signals reveals the change of these redox 
species with increasing applied potential from an OER pre-catalytic IrIII state to an OER active 
IrIV-containing state. The kinetics of the redox states in IrOx were further analyzed through the 
decay of the absorption bands centered at 600, 800 and 460 nm, respectively. To access the 
OER reaction kinetics of the active redox state as a function of its concentration, the decay was 
measured after turning off the applied potential. The decay is attributed to the depletion of IrIV. 
Its faster decay after turning off at more positive applied potentials implies a greater reactivity 
of this state at increasing potentials (Figure 57 C). Such spectral evolution is also observed in 
the case of an Ir dimeric molecular (Irmolecular) catalyst (Figure 57 A, right).348 The optical 
signals related to the three redox transitions in IrOx and Irmolecular have broadly similar features. 
The concentration distributions in Irmolecular shift anodically by ∼100 mV with respect to IrOx 
for all the three redox transitions. However, in contrast with IrOx, Irmolecular exhibits potential 
independent OER reaction kinetics (Figure 57 C). The difference between the molecular 
catalyst and the heterogeneous oxide film in terms of OER kinetics is attributed to the lack of 
co-operative effects in the Irmolecular system with isolated Ir centers. 

84 
 
 
 
Figure 57. (A) Absorption changes of (right) IrOx and (left) Irmolecular on mesoporous indium tin oxide upon 
applying an oxidative potential in 0.1 M HClO4 aqueous solution at pH 1.2. (B) Normalized deconvolution of the 
spectroelectrochemical data of Irmolecular and IrOx. (C) Absorption decay after turning the potential off. Adapted 
from refs. 347-349. Copyrights 2021 and 2022 American Chemical Society. 
Zachary et al. used operando UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy to elucidate redox 
behavior of pure Ni and Fe-doped NiFe catalysts for OER.350 When the applied potential 
increases, both Ni and NiFe catalysts develop a spectroscopic feature that corresponds to the 
band-edge absorption due to the decrease of band gaps of both the Ni and NiFe catalysts upon 
proton-coupled oxidation (Figure 58 B, D). The similar absorption onsets of Ni and NiFe 
systems (~1.5 eV) are consistent with the calculated band gaps. Figure 58 A, C illustrates the 
absorption at 2.5 eV as a function of applied potential, which demonstrates a direct correlation 
between the optical and redox changes in the catalyst. In the pure Ni catalyst system, the UV-
VIS-NIR absorbance depends on the applied potential before the onset of oxygen evolution 
reaction (OER) activity. According to first-principles calculations, the Ni is oxidized from Ni2+ 
to Ni3+ followed by further oxidation to a mixed Ni3+/4+ state at a potential that coincidences 
with the onset of OER activity and both Ni3+and Ni4+contribute to the conduction band 
minimum (CBM) in the Ni catalyst. In contrast, absorbance changes are negligible in a NiFe 
catalyst before the onset of OER activity due to the formation of significant amount of Fe4+, 
which is revealed as the dominant species for CBM in a NiFe catalyst. Within the catalytic 
wave, absorbance of both Ni and NiFe catalysts are independent of the applied potential 
because rapid water oxidation impedes the formation of species with higher oxidation states 
given these species have neither significant population nor lifetime.  

85 
 
 
 
Figure 58. Cyclic voltammograms (CVs) (Left) and UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra (Right) of the (A) & (B) 
pure Ni and (C) & (D) 25% Fe films. Data points in the CVs correspond to the absorbance at 2.5 eV with the 
same color coding as in the spectra. Adapted from ref. 341. Copyright 2017 National Academy of Sciences. 
By performing in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy with varying sample thickness, 
one can differentiate between the surface and bulk reaction process occurring over a catalyst. 
This is exemplified by Sacha et al. in the case of NiOx catalyst in water oxidation. Relationships 
between film thickness and UV-VIS-NIR absorbance were obtained in pre-catalytic and 
catalytic conditions.341 Increasing film thickness results in greater absorption at potentials prior 
to the onset of the OER, indicating that pre-catalytic oxidation of NiOx to NiOOH in alkaline 
electrolyte is a bulk oxidation process (Figure 59 A). In contrast, absorption on the electrode 
during the catalytic process is unaffected by the film thickness, suggesting that the intermediate 
species that formed during water oxidation catalysis are limited to the near surface of the 
catalyst (Figure 59 B). 

86 
 
 
 
Figure 59. Change in UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra of each sample; (A) after activation with respect to the 
absorbance before activation, and (B) after catalysis onset with respect to the absorbance before catalytic process. 
Adapted from ref. 341. Copyright 2020 Royal Society of Chemistry. 
3.5 Photocatalysis 
Photocatalysis harvests photon energy for chemical transformations. It has been widely 
investigated for applications including hydrogen evolution, pollutant degradation, organic 
synthesis, and biomass valorization, and is found in enzymatic catalysis.351-355 In general, 
photocatalysis begins with light absorption, which transforms the photocatalyst to an excited 
state. Most commonly electronic excitations are leveraged using abundantly available solar 
UV-VIS-NIR range radiation, with the catalytic valorisation of NIR range excitations being 
more cumbersome.356 The excited photocatalyst then activates the reactant directly or catalyzes 
the generation of active species for indirect reactant conversion.354 The catalyst may be 
heterogeneous, homogeneous or enzymatic, preferably in a form that maximizes the potential 
for light harvesting. Currently, the efficiencies of most photocatalytic systems are too low for 
industrial application, and further material development backed by deeper fundamental 
understanding is needed. 
UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopies are highly relevant characterization tools in photocatalysis, 
given the intrinsic similarity between the spectroscopically probed electronic transitions and 
the catalytically relevant excitation. Moreover the technique is easily matched with in situ and 
operando conditions.10 In situ and operando spectroscopies in general for photocatalysis were 

87 
 
 
very recently reviewed by Hamoud et al.39 Here we limit the section to representative 
demonstrations of UV-VIS-NIR absorption measurements for in situ photocatalysis studies, 
covering the broad timescales that are relevant. 
Light absorption, charge separation, and transfer of charges to the reaction centers are 
elementary steps in photocatalysis that mainly take place in fs-s time scales. Commonly, but 
not exclusively, semiconductor materials are used where an electron from the valence band 
(VB) is excited into the conduction band (CB) leaving an electron hole in the VB. Alternatively 
molecular reactive electronic excited states may be generated. Active species generated in these 
ultrafast processes, including electron-hole pairs and photo-excited chemicals – mainly radical 
intermediates – are typefied by their highly reactive features and short lifetimes. These photo-
induced short-lived excited states can cause characteristic optical absorption features in the 
UV-VIS-NIR spectral range. With the help of pump lasers, UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectroscopy is used to study photo-induced processes in situ down to the femtosecond regime 
(see also technical details in section 2.2.1.3 and Figure 9),62 in a technique known as ultrafast 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy or transient absorption (TA) spectroscopy. In addition 
to the ultrafast monitoring of the dynamics of excited species, electronic and structural changes 
at the catalyst surface can be monitored in combination with reaction progress over hours or 
days under different conditions providing mechanistic insight.357-359 
3.5.1 Transient absorption to monitor ultrafast processes (fs-to-s time scale) 
The TA spectroscopy measurement is accomplished by the pump-probe technique, wherein a 
fraction of the molecules are first promoted to an electronically excited state using a pump 
pulse. A probe pulse is then sent through the sample with a time delay (τ) relative to the pump 
pulse. The resulting TA signals are typically presented as the difference between the 
absorbance of the excited and ground state samples as expressed by eq 17. 
 
∆𝐴(𝜆, 𝜏) = ⁡𝐴(𝜆, 𝜏) −𝐴(𝜆)𝑔𝑟𝑜𝑢𝑛𝑑⁡𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑡𝑒 
(17) 
where 𝐴(𝜆, 𝜏) and 𝐴(𝜆)𝑔𝑟𝑜𝑢𝑛𝑑⁡𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑡𝑒 are respectively the absorbance of the excited state at delay 
𝜏 and of the ground state. In general, a TA spectrum contains contributions from ground state 
bleach (GSB, negative), excited state absorption (ESA, positive) and stimulated emission (SE, 
negative) (Figure 60). All these contributions can be tracked for catalytic insight,360 although 
mostly the SE and bleach bands are used in the UV-VIS-NIR range. ESA bands can be difficult 
to resolve, and may hinder the tracking of the SE and bleach bands.361 ESA bands can also be 
tracked by their vibrational excitations in the IR.362, 363 
 
Figure 60. Schematic of a pump-probe transient absorption experiment. At t0 the ground state absorption spectrum 
is measured, before applying a pump pulse. This is used as the reference spectrum A(λ)ground state in eq 17. At a later 

88 
 
 
time t1, the probe pulse is applied selectively exciting electrons from the ground state to an excited state (in this 
case from VB to CB). Then with delay τ, the absorption spectrum is measured again to get A(λ,τ) of eq 17. These 
pump-probe sequences are repeated for different τ to produce a time series of TA spectra. 
TA spectroscopy was performed to reveal the mechanism of ligand-controlled performance of 
cadmium sulfide (CdS) quantum dots (QDs) for the conversion of lignin.364 Electron decay 
processes of CdS QDs with different ligands were observed after applying laser pulses of 400 
nm. The ligands CdS-C3, CdS-C6, and CdS-C9 are schematically illustrated in Figure 61 A. 
They were monitored over ∼2.4 ns after excitation. The decay of photogenerated electrons was 
tracked based on the ~460 nm bleaching band, which corresponds to the excitation of CdS QDs 
from the ground to the first excited state (Figure 61 B). Figure 61 C shows CdS-Cx QDs decay 
almost independently of ligand length, indicating that intrinsic decay is largely responsible for 
the recovery of QDs. However, recovery kinetics depend strongly on ligand length in the 
presence of a lignin model compound, 2-phenoxy-1-phenylethanol (PPol) (Figure 61 D). 
Increasing electron decay rates are observed with decreasing ligand lengths, which corresponds 
to the catalytic efficiency of CdS QDs for lignin conversion, i.e., CdS-C3 > CdS-C6 > CdS-
C9. The electron decay kinetic curves were fitted into a multiexponential equation, which 
indicates three electron decay processes, a fast process (<50 ps), a slow process (∼100 ps), and 
a nanosecond range process (2 - 5 ns). The slow and nanosecond processes were assigned to 
the intrinsic decay of CdS QDs while the fast process was attributed to the electron transfer 
from the QDs to PPol.364 The weighted contribution of the corresponding decay process 
indicates that the electron transfer from QDs to PPol dominates the decay kinetics for CdS-C3 
and CdS-C6 QDs in the presence of PPol. The TA spectroscopy not only illustrates the electron 
decay pathways in CdS-QDs with different ligands but also demonstrates that surface ligands 
control the electron transfer process. 
 
Figure 61. (A) Schematic illustration of CdS-C3, CdS-C6, and CdS-C9. (B) The steady-state absorption spectrum 
of CdS-C6 (red line, upper) and transient absorption spectra of CdS-C6 after 400 nm excitation (bottom). TA 

89 
 
 
dynamics probed at 460 nm of CdS-Cx QDs in the (C) absence and (D) presence of lignin model compound PPol. 
Reproduced from ref. 364. Copyright 2019 American Chemical Society. 
Charge dynamics resolved by TA have tracked the mechanism of photocatalytic CO2 reduction 
using a NiII complex as catalyst, a RuII complex as photosensitizer, and 1,3-dimethyl-2-phenyl-
2,3-dihydro-1Hbenzo[d]imidazole (BIH) as the electron donor.365, 366 Upon laser excitation at 
440 nm, the bleaching signal of the ground-state RuII appears at 446 nm and the absorption 
attenuation signal of the excited-state *RuII at 365 nm. The addition of BIH gives a new band 
(510 nm) indicating the formation of RuI through the quenching of the excited *RuII state with 
BIH. Upon further addition of NiII, the decay of the RuI signal is accelerated. The following 
reaction pathway was derived: RuII is excited into *RuII upon irradiation, BIH quenches *RuII 
to form RuI, and then RuI turns back into RuII by donating an electron to NiII, converting it into 
NiI. Other in situ spectroscopic techniques, including time-resolved XAS, DRIFTS, and 
operando surface-enhanced Raman spectroscopy as well as theoretical modelling 
demonstrated that the reduced NiI is the catalytically active species for CO2 reduction.365, 366 
The lifetime of photogenerated charge carriers can be monitored by TA to evaluate the electron 
transfer rate of different processes.367, 368 An informative example is the photocatalytic 
degradation of intelligent ink, with glycerol and resazurin as major components. TiO2 in the 
anatase phase is a ~30 times more efficient catalyst than the rutile phase for this degradation. 
The phase-dependent lifetimes of electrons and holes in these systems were investigated using 
TA,368 using absorption peaks at 900 nm (photogenerated electrons) and 550 nm 
(photogenerated holes) to track the decay kinetics. TA spectra in presence of glycerol, resazurin 
dye, and intelligent ink, were recorded separately (Figure 62 A, B). Fitting of the electron decay 
indicates the addition of glycerol for anatase results in long-lived excited electrons with a 
lifetime in the second range. For rutile, on the other hand, the excited electron was short-lived 
with a lifetime in the millisecond range (Figure 62 C, D). Comparing the phase-dependent 
lifetimes of electrons in the presence of different reactants, the authors proposed that the lower 
activity of rutile is due to the deficiency of its photogenerated holes to efficiently drive glycerol 
oxidation.368 
 

90 
 
 
Figure 62. The normalized transient decays from TA for (A) anatase and (B) rutile probed at 900 nm. Summary 
of different time scales from TA dynamics of the photocatalytic processes with (C) anatase and (D) rutile. 
Reproduced from ref. 368. Copyright 2015 American Chemical Society. 
Another example is the derivation of the Z-scheme structure of black/red phosphorus (BP/RP) 
hetero-phase junction photocatalysts.369, 370 Excitation of BP/RP induces a broad absorption 
band centered at 550 nm linked to surface-trapped electrons. Its decay time profile was fitted 
by a function with two exponentials indicating the lifetime of electrons trapped at shallow (τ1) 
and deep sites (τ2). The decay lifetimes decreased from pure RP (τ1: 15.9 ps; τ2: 315.2 ps) to 
BP/RP (τ1: 6.5 ps; τ1: 166.2 ps) indicating interfacial electron transfer. With a similar 
methodology, the Z-scheme structure for TiO2/Ce2S3 was clarified using the decay of a 645 nm 
absorption band.369 
The wavelength of TA curves can provide information regarding the evolution/decay of various 
intermediate species involved in chemical reactions.371-373 Using the TA spectra, the dynamics 
of three distinct types of excitons of CdSe/CdS-Pt, 1D CdS nanorods (NRs) with a CdSe “seed” 
inside and a Pt “dot” at one end, were examined. These excitons are classified by their localized 
area, which can be differentiated by their absorption peak wavelengths. More specifically, the 
peak at ~540 nm is attributed to excitons localized in the CdSe seed (X3), the profound peak 
at ~ 450 nm is attributed to excitons localized in the CdS rod (X1), while the small shoulder at 
470 nm is attributed to both excitons localized in the CdS bulb region surrounding the CdSe 
seed (X2) and in the CdSe seed (X3) (Figure 63).371, 372 Fitting these TA spectra to 
multiexponential functions reveals the formation probabilities and half-lives of different 
excitons, i.e., ∼46%, 22.5 ns for excitons localized in the CdS rod (X1), 7%, 32.1 ns for 
excitons localized in CdS bulb region surrounding the CdSe seed (X2), and 47%, 8.3 ns for 
excitons localized in the CdSe seed (X3).372 These distinct features were used to study the 
photocatalytic H2 generation over CdSe/CdS-Pt and CdS-Pt NRs and the electron-donor 
dependence of these catalysts.371 When weakly reductive MeOH is used as electron donor, the 
trapped-hole transfer rate in CdS NRs is slower than that of the CdSe core. On the other hand, 
sulfite as electron donor can provide enough driving force for trapped holes in CdS NRs to 
transfer. Hole transfer from the CdSe core is more difficult since core-confined holes are harder 
to access by the electron donor.371 These trapped holes in distinct spatial locations, which were 
characterized and monitored by TA spectra, result in different activity depending on the 
electron acceptors. 
 
 

91 
 
 
Figure 63. (A) Schematic illustration of three distinct exciton states (X1, X2, and X3) in CdSe/CdS-Pt 
nanocrystals (upper panel) and their corresponding energy level diagram (lower panel). X1, X2, and X3 refer to 
excitons localized in the CdS rod, the CdS bulb region surrounding the CdSe seed, and the CdSe seed, respectively. 
Excitation energy decreases in the order X1 > X2 >X3. (B) TA spectra after 400 nm excitation for 0.2 to 1 000 
ps. Reproduced from refs. 371, 372. Copyright 2013 American Chemical Society. 
TA can also be used to track chemical reactions that take place at ultra-fast speeds. 
Phenothiazine (PTZ) can be used as a hole acceptor to facilitate H2 generation from water 
splitting through the efficient extraction of photo-generated holes.374 The monitoring of PTZ 
oxidation is possible with the well-separated characteristic absorptions of PTZ+ (∼520 nm) and 
the bleaching band of CdS NRs (~450 nm). TA in the 500-600 nm spectral range, which 
includes the signal of the trapped hole, decays with a time constant of ∼3.6 ns. This feature is 
accompanied by the appearance of the PTZ+ absorption band at ∼520 nm. These spectral 
features are direct evidence of the trapped hole transfer from CdS NRs to PTZ molecules. 
Fitting TA kinetics with multiexponential curves shows the trapped holes are extracted by PTZ 
(half-life = 3.8 ± 1.7 ns), leading to the formation of long-lived charge-separated states (CdS–
PTZ+, half-life = 310 ± 50 ns). A high extraction efficiency of 94.7 ± 9.0% was achieved, 
contributing to the much faster hole transfer rates to PTZ than the slow conduction band 
electron–trapped hole recombination (half lifetime of 67 ± 1 ns). These TA results illustrate 
that the key to efficient photocatalytic H2 evolution over CdS nanocrystals is the efficient 
extraction of trapped holes to prevent electron-hole recombination.  
3.5.2 Time-course spectra to monitor reaction changes (s-to-day time scale) 
In addition to monitoring the dynamics of excited species in an ultrafast way, in situ/operando 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy can also be employed to track the change of reaction 
systems parallel to the whole reaction course over hours or days under different conditions. 
Copper on porous titanium dioxide aerogel (Cu/TiO2) exhibit high efficiency in photocatalytic 
CO2 reduction to CO in an aqueous solution. Interestingly, the CO2 reduction rate improves in 
the presence of O2. As the O2 content increases, CO production reaches a maximum at 0.5 
vol%, while the competing H2 evolution is limited.357 In situ UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy 
was applied to monitor changes in Cu/TiO2 after different irradiation times in the presence and 
the absence of O2. Before irradiation, Cu/TiO2 is characterized by the band gap of TiO2 at 350 
nm and several less defined broad bands between 400 and 900 nm, which are ascribed to charge 
transfer transitions in TiO2 and CuO (below 450 nm) and d-d transitions of CuII (600-900 nm). 
This indicates that CuII is the main species in fresh Cu/TiO2. The in situ UV-VIS absorption 
spectra were expressed relative to fresh Cu/TiO2. A band with a maximum of 380 nm, assigned 
to CuI, is observed when the catalyst is exposed to 100% CO2 after 1 min irradiation (Figure 
64 A). Its intensity increases within the first 2 min of irradiation, and then disappears. After 
exposure to light for 10 min, a sharp minimum at ~580 nm caused by the formation of metallic 
Cu is observed. Cu0 and CuI species are also formed in presence of O2 with concentrations of 
0.1 and 0.5 vol % (Figure 64 B, C).357 The kinetics of CuII reduction and accordingly the 
fraction of different Cu species are affected by O2. Temporal changes in the UV-VIS absorption 
spectra suggested O2 leads to slower CuII reduction to CuI and Cu0 as well as a lower Cu0 
fraction. The highest rate of CO formation is achieved at the beginning of the photocatalytic 
process, i.e., within the first 30 min regardless of the O2 content. UV-VIS absorption spectra 
indicate CuI is present at this time and is therefore responsible for CO2 photoreduction. On the 
other hand, Cu0 is the main species after 30 min, the amount of CO formed between 30 min 
and 20 h is lower than that formed between 0 and 30 min, indicating a low intrinsic activity of 
Cu0. At O2 concentrations higher than 0.5 vol%, CuII cannot be reduced to CuI, thus resulting 

92 
 
 
in lower activity. Therefore, CuI was proposed to be the active species of Cu/TiO2 for CO2 
photoreduction to CO (Figure 64 D). 
 
Figure 64. UV-VIS absorption spectra (Kubelka-Munk) of Cu/TiO2 after different irradiation times in (A) 100% 
CO2, (B) 0.1 vol % O2/CO2, and (C) 0.5 vol % O2/CO2. (D) Proposed mechanism for photocatalytic conversion 
of CO2 over Cu/TiO2 and the nature of active copper species. Reproduced from ref. 357. Copyright 2019 Cell Press. 
In situ UV-VIS DRS was conducted to gain mechanistic insight into photocatalytic CO2 
reduction on a covalent organic framework (COF) photocatalyst, consisting of a photoactive 
two-dimensional triazine COF and a Re-based molecular catalyst (Re-COF) (Figure 65 A).358 
An induction period of about 15 min is observed over the Re-COF (Figure 65 B). As shown in 
Figure 65 C, in the initial 15 min (denoted Step I), prominent absorption in the 550 - 800 nm 
region that resembles the broad absorption of the intramolecular charge transfer (ICT) band of 
triazine increases, accompanied by decreasing absorption at 400 - 500 nm with an isosbestic 
point at 539 nm. This indicates the formation of the excited ICT state. Similar spectra are 
observed in the absence of CO2. The spectral evolution during the first 15 min can thus be 
attributed to the accumulation of charge separation (CS) state, before catalysis initiates (Step I, 
Figure 65 A). Following Step I, the absorption at 550 - 800 nm decreases significantly (Figure 
65 C, denoted Step II), while the absorption at <430 nm grows with a new isosbestic point at 
430 nm. The distinct features of Step I and Step II suggest the formation of different 
intermediate species. Indeed, step II evolution is negligible in the system without CO2 or when 
Re-COF is replaced by COF, indicating that the newly formed intermediate species is 
associated with CO2 and Re molecules. The spectra remain almost constant in Step III, 
suggesting that the system reaches equilibrium after Step II, and this is consistent with the time 
window for steady generation of CO (Figure 65 B).358 
 

93 
 
 
 
Figure 65. (A) Schematic illustration of Re-COF structure and the proposed catalytic mechanism for CO2 
reduction over Re-COF. (B) Production of CO as a function of time from the photocatalytic conversion of CO2 
over Re-COF. (C) The in situ DRS UV–VIS absorption spectra of Re-COF under photocatalytic conditions within 
15 min (upper panel) and 3 h (lower panel). The inset in lower panel shows the in situ spectra collected from 5 to 
9 h. Reproduced from ref. 358. Copyright 2018 American Chemical Society. 
A fully automated triple combination of in situ illumination, UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectroscopy, and NMR spectroscopy enabled the simultaneous and time-resolved detection of 
paramagnetic and diamagnetic species to study the photoreduction of aryl halides to aryls with 
N,N-bis(2,6-diisopropylphenyl)-perylene-3,4,9,10-bisdicarboximide (PDI) as photocatalyst.277 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is a powerful tool for the detection of stable radicals, 
which are often not accessible by NMR spectroscopy. After a few minutes of illumination, the 
absorption bands of PDI•− at 698 and 794 nm grow in while the absorbance bands of PDI at 
455 and 482 nm steadily decrease, enabling a semiquantitative reaction profile for the evolution 
of PDI•−. Complementary to the UV-VIS absorption spectra, NMR spectra give full 
quantitative information about diamagnetic reactants and products in the reaction.  
The coupled UV-VIS absorption, EPR, and IR spectroscopies were employed for mechanistic 
studies of a copper-iron system for photocatalytic H2 generation from water.359 The copper-
iron system contains a heteroleptic copper photosensitizer [Cu(P∧P)(N∧N)]+, which includes 
bidentate P∧P (Xantphos) and N∧N (2,9-dimethyl-4,7-diphenyl-1,10-phenanthroline) chelate 
ligands, and an iron-based water reduction catalyst. The role of the copper photosensitizer 
[Cu(P∧P)(N∧N)]+, which can be oxidized or reduced after photoexcitation, in the copper-iron 
system was investigated. [Cu(P∧P)(N∧N)]+ exhibits a UV-VIS adsorption feature at 390 nm, 
which decreases after several redox cycles, while a new band at 475 nm assigned to 
[Cu(N∧N)2]+ arises. UV-VIS absorption spectroscopy reveals the in situ change of the original 
heteroleptic photosensitizer toward a [Cu(N∧N)2]+ species during catalysis, proving the 
presence of the oxidative cycle. EPR spectroscopy shows the presence of the reductive cycle, 
and in situ FTIR spectroscopy provided evidence for the formation of a catalytic diiron 
intermediate. 

94 
 
 
4. Outlook and Conclusions 
In situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy is a simple and cheap technique to study catalysts 
and catalytic reactions. It is in most cases non-destructive and non-intrusive (with exceptions 
in homogeneous and photocatalysis). The spectra can give information on individual transition 
metal ions, on nanoparticles of TMI oxides and metals, and on organic molecules, radicals and 
ions. It is well suited for in situ and operando measurements in combination with specialized 
cells, making use of fiber optics, diffuse reflectance spectroscopy, ultrafast laser pulses, and 
microscopy. This enables its measurement in various methodologies including stopped flow 
experiments, transient response and step response experiments, transient absorption 
experiments, electrochemical conditions, and at variable temperatures and magnetic fields. 
These methodologies give access to a variety of catalytically relevant data including electronic 
structure and reaction kinetics. Data processing of the absorption spectra is necessary to extract 
band gap energies and to quantitatively measure concentrations and conversions, and is widely 
adopted. It is however important to keep track of the sensitivities and assumptions of these 
methods, particularly for Tauc/Cody plots and the processing of diffuse reflectance data with 
the Kubelka-Munk formula. 
The UV-VIS-NIR range has both its limitations and advantages for in situ use. Heterogeneous 
catalysis is usually carried out well above room temperature. The absorption bands are sensitive 
to elevated temperatures with absorption bands broadening and shifting through vibronic 
coupling. If the bands are weak, they may almost completely disappear in the background at 
high temperature. Such bands are of no use in e.g. kinetic or structural analysis. Low 
temperatures are a challenge too due to icing and physical obstructions such as bubbling and 
separation of phases in liquid environment. In homogeneous catalysis solvents should also not 
have absorption bands in the UV-VIS-NIR range. In heterogeneous catalysis black supports 
such as graphene and carbon black prevent UV-VIS-NIR absorption measurements. Finally, 
the UV-VIS-NIR induced transitions are assumed not to be destructive for the catalyst nor 
interfering with the processes under study. This is not always the case, most evidently for 
photocatalysis, but also for homogeneous catalysis in general. We see that in theory the 
potential of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy at varied conditions offers great 
opportunities in better understanding catalysts’ structures and electronic properties with 
relatively cheap and accessible setups, and that such measurements could alleviate the main 
drawback of poor spectral resolution. However such measurements remain scarce probably due 
to practical obstacles and challenges in data analysis. 
UV-VIS-NIR absorption offers a window into electronic structure with direct relevance to 
reactivity and catalysis. Nevertheless this potential remains underused in catalysis research, 
especially on complex heterogeneous catalysts and on homogeneous catalysts, despite the 
popular measurement of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra on these systems. Exceptions are the 
examples given on copper and iron exchanged zeolites. The assignment of spectroscopic bands 
to their electronic transitions is often omitted, using the absorption band merely for 
fingerprinting and/or kinetic tracking. One of the reasons is again the poor spectral resolution 
leading to difficult deconvolution, although often better resolutions could be obtained with 
state-of-the-art spectrophotometers and setups. Another reason is the difficulty in interpretation. 
The automated use of databases on absorption spectra could help here to benefit from the 
numerous existing measurements in literature, as well as advanced statistical analysis of time 
series data on absorption data from transient/step response experiments. Extension of the 
studies on copper and iron zeolites to other transition metals could provide useful reference 
data for other more complex catalyst. Moreover, knowledge transfer across the disciplines such 

95 
 
 
as links of identified species in enzymatic work to heterogeneous metal complexes can be 
instrumental and should be stimulated. Further advances in the simulation of absorption spectra 
can also be expected, with better infrastructure and algorithms giving better access to higher 
level, more accurate methods (e.g. from TD-DFT to coupled cluster and multireference 
methods). On that note an up-to-date review work on the computational simulation of 
electronic absorption spectra with guidelines and insights from benchmarking would be a 
welcome addition to the literature.  
There is no doubt that multi-spectroscopic approaches will remain the key to in-depth 
understanding in catalysis. The electronic information of UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectra is 
(for example) combined with (1) vibrational information from FTIR and/or rR; (2) with 
structural information from XANES, EXAFS ….; (3) magnetic information from EPR and/or 
MCD. Applying these techniques at reaction conditions remains a challenge, as not all 
spectroscopies are sufficiently sensitive. The technological hurdles to do so have, however 
progressed strongly.4 This review has highlighted the compatibility of UV-VIS-NIR 
spectroscopies with some catalytic systems and measurement techniques. The UV-VIS-NIR 
range uniquely allows the application of three techniques (absorption, rR and MCD) that give 
complementary information site-selectively by probing the same excitation events. The direct 
relation between the spectroscopic data can therefore be established with more confidence than 
for other spectroscopy combinations. Examples of in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption 
spectroscopy combined with rR and MCD have been given for copper and iron zeolites and for 
metalloenzymes. This could be extended beyond these applications. 
Numerous advances can be expected for in situ UV-VIS-NIR absorption spectroscopy. 
Developments continue in optical equipment for transient absorption to further expand its range 
into the attoseconds. Improved cells enable the combined measurement of more spectroscopies. 
Improved light collection and sample irradiation, and the more widespread use of statistical 
analysis and the availability of databases may alleviate the limits on spectral resolution. These 
developments will be important beyond the field of catalysis. With the exponential 
development of sensors and electrochemical batteries, UV-VIS-NIR spectroscopies have also 
entered the broader material science field. In the case of battery research S-based batteries are 
very well suited for UV-VIS-NIR range spectroscopy as each polysulfide has an absorption 
band in the UV-VIS range.372, 375, 376 Sensors are often semiconductors with a band gap or 
nanotarticles with surface plasmon resonance in the UV-VIS-NIR range.377, 378 An example is 
the NIR surface plasmon resonance of starch capped CuS quantum dots, sensitive to uptake of 
environmentally relevant divalent heavy metals.379 Hopefully the more widespread use will 
spur developments in spectroscopy, to the benefit of better understanding advanced materials 
in general.

8. Biographies 
Edward I. Solomon 
Edward I. Solomon grew up in North Miami Beach, FL, received his Ph.D. at Princeton (with 
D. S. McClure), and was a postdoctoral fellow at The Ørsted Institute (with C. J. Ballhausen) 
and then at Caltech (with H. B. Gray). He was a professor at the Massachusetts Institute of 
Technology until 1982, when he joined the faculty at Stanford University, where he is now the 
Monroe E. Spaght Professor of Humanities and Sciences and Professor of Photon Science at 
the SLAC National Accelerator Lab. He has been an invited professor in Argentina, Australia, 
Brazil, China, France, India, and Japan. Prof. Solomon’s research is in the fields of physical 
inorganic chemistry and bioinorganic chemistry with emphasis on the application of a wide 
range of spectroscopic methods combined with QM calculations to elucidate the electronic 
structure of transition metal sites and its contribution to physical properties and reactivity. He 
has received a wide range of medals and awards and is a member of the National Academy of 

121 
 
 
Sciences and the American Academy of Arts and Sciences and a fellow in the American 
Association for the Advancement of Science and the American Chemical Society. 
Alexander Heyer 
Alexander J. Heyer received his B.S. in chemistry at the University of Virginia where he 
researched proton-coupled electron transfer in Mo and W scorpionate complexes with 
Professor Dean Harman. He is now a Ph.D. student in inorganic chemistry at Stanford 
University in Professor Edward Solomon’s research group. Using both spectroscopic and 
computational methods, he studies methane oxidation in Cu and Fe zeolites, and his research 
is supported by the Anne T. and Robert M. Bass Stanford Graduate Fellowship. 
Bert F. Sels 
Bert F. Sels (1972), currently full professor at KU Leuven (Belgium), obtained his Ph.D. in 
2000 in the field of heterogeneous oxidation catalysis under the guidance of Prof. Pierre Jacobs. 
He was awarded the DSM Chemistry Award in 2000, the Incentive Award by the Belgian 
Chemical Society in 2005, and the international Green Chemistry Award in 2015. He currently 
leads the Centre for Sustainable Catalysis and Engineering (CSCE), and is active in designing 
heterogeneous catalysts for future challenges in industrial organic and environmental catalysis. 
His expertise includes heterogeneous catalysis in biorefineries, design of hierarchical zeolites 
and carbons, and the spectroscopic and kinetic study of active sites for small-molecule 
activation. He is cochair of the Catalysis Commission of the International Zeolite Association 
(IZA) and cofounder of the European Research Institute of Catalysis (ERIC). He is also a 
member of the European Academy of Sciences and Arts, member of the international advisory 
board of ChemSusChem (Wiley), and associate editor of ACS Sustainable Chemistry & 
Engineering. 
Dieter Plessers 
Dieter Plessers obtained his M.Sc. in Bioscience Engineering focused on catalytic technology 
from KU Leuven in 2017, with a Master’s thesis on the identification of the active sites in 
copper- and iron-exchanged zeolites for the selective oxidation of methane to methanol. 
Supported by a grant from the Research Foundation - Flanders (FWO), he obtained a Ph.D. at 
the Center for Sustainable Catalysis and Engineering (CSCE) under the guidance of Prof. Bert 
Sels, where he used spectroscopy and kinetic analyses to identify the copper speciation on 
copper-exchanged zeolites and quantify their reactivity in methane activation. This topic 
continues to be his primary research interest as a postdoctoral researcher in the framework of 
an FWO research grant. 
Fatima Rammal 
Fatima Rammal received in 2015 her B.Sc. in chemistry from the Lebanese university, Beirut, 
and her Master’s degree in organic and pharmaceutical chemistry in 2017 from the University 
of Paris Saclay, France. In December 2020, she obtained her Ph.D. degree in organic and 
photochemistry from the University of Caen, France, under the supervision of Dr Sami Lakhdar. 
During her research, she developed novel visible light mediated processes for the aromatization 
of polycyclic compounds, as well as for the functionalization of N-heteroarenes using several 

122 
 
 
non-activated radical precursors such as alkane, methanol, silane, and [1.1.1]propellane. Since 
February 2021, she is a postdoctoral researcher in the group of Prof. Bert Sels at the center for 
sustainable catalysis and engineering, KU Leuven, Belgium, working on the heterogeneous 
catalysis for biomass valorization and CO2 conversion. 
Jing Ma 
Jing Ma received her B.Sc. in chemical engineering from the Sichuan University in 2019. In 
2021, she obtained her M.Sc. in chemical system engineering at the University of Tokyo. In 
Tokyo she researched the influence of Al distribution in ZSM-5 zeolites on MTO reactions by 
transient kinetic methods, working with Professor Ogura Masaru and Dr. Takahiko Moteki. 
Supported by a grant from the FWO, she is currently pursuing a Ph.D. at KU Leuven under the 
guidance of Professor Bert Sels. Her research focuses on the investigation of methane oxidation 
in copper-exchanged zeolites by spectroscopic methods. 
Max L. Bols 
Max L. Bols received his B.Sc. and M.Sc. in bioscience engineering at the KU Leuven in 2015, 
doing his master’s thesis on the identification of the active site in iron zeolites for methane 
activation. Upon graduation, he started a Ph.D. with Dr. Benjamin Snyder, Prof. Bert Sels and 
Prof. Robert Schoonheydt on the synthesis, kinetics, and spectroscopic characterization of 
transition metal active sites in zeolites, supported by a FWO research grant. He continued this 
research as an FWO postdoctoral fellow, in collaboration with Prof. Edward Solomon at 
Stanford University. He is now a postdoctoral researcher at the Laboratory of Chemical 
Technology of Ghent University working on molecular modelling, spectroscopy, and kinetic 
studies for the characterization of heterogeneous catalysts. 
Robert A. Schoonheydt 
Robert A. Schoonheydt is emeritus professor of the KU Leuven, where he obtained his 
bioengineering degree in 1966 and his Ph.D. in 1970 under the guidance of Prof. Jan B. 
Uytterhoeven. He did a postdoc in the chemistry department of the Texas A&M University 
under the guidance of Jack H. Lunsford on adsorption of SO2 on MgO. His research interests 
at the KU Leuven are the surface chemistry of clay minerals and zeolites with special emphasis 
on transition metal ions and spectroscopy. Presently he is a member of an international research 
collaboration of zeolite chemists, spectroscopists, and quantum chemists on active sites in Cu- 
and Fe-zeolites for selective oxidation, NOx abatement, and electrophilic aromatic substitution 
reactions. He was dean of the Faculty of Bioscience Engineering from 1998 until 2004 and 
president of the International Association for the Study of Clays from 2001 until 2005. 
Sara Navarro-Jaén 
Sara Navarro-Jaén received her Ph.D. degree in Science and Technology of New Materials 
from the University of Seville (Spain) in 2019, under the supervision of Prof. José A. Odriozola. 
She then joined the UCCS CNRS laboratory (France) as a postdoctoral fellow in the groups of 
Dr. Andrei Khodakov and Prof. Franck Dumeignil. Since 2022 she works as a researcher at 
KU Leuven (Belgium), in the group of Prof. Bert Sels. Her research interests focus on the 
design of catalytic materials with environmental applications and the understanding of the 

123 
 
 
underlying reaction mechanisms, ranging from conversion of GHGs into fuels and chemicals 
to biomass valorization. 
Xuejiao Wu 
Xuejiao Wu received her B.Sc. degree (2015) from Xiamen University and obtained her Ph.D. 
degree (2020) from Xiamen University under the supervision of Prof. Ye Wang. She then 
carried out postdoctoral research at Prof. Bert F. Sels group at KU Leuven. Her research focuses 
on the valorization of lignocellulose, in particular by photocatalytic approaches.
